READ THIS.
Western Australia is a much richer colony than is
generally supposed as regards minerals, agriculture and
pastoralism.—Sir Samuel Griffith.
Professor Brown, of Victoria, who made a thorough
examination of the south-western portion of Western
Australia, states that not the other colonies only, but
even the people of Western Australia evidently do not
know how much there is waiting for development. He
saw thousands of acres in contiguous blocks of fine choco-
late lands, light clay loams, and peaty soils. He thinks
the important features of the district are its grand climate
and regular rainfall.
“ History repeats itself,” and as surely as Sydney and
Melbourne have made lightning-like progress, so will
PERTH,'
the Capital of West Australia.

Noble-street, Newton, Geelong,
10th December, 1888.
To H. F. Kugklmann, Esq.
Dear Sir, —This is to certify that I, Patrick Keenan, was
taken ill about three years ago with a severe cold, which
turned into Bronchitis and became chronic. I consulted
inure than one doctor, and during that time took a great
quantity of medicine without it doing me the slightest
good, hut I became worse every day, wasting away until
I became a perfect skeleton. About February last I began
to spit up blood, and what with the cough and severe
pains in my left lung, and the quantity of bad matter I
was expectorating every day, I became very much alarmed.
Hearing of your professional skill I consulted you, and
you told me you could cure me for a given sum. I agreed
and went under your treatment in March last. At the
end of three months I was taken bad one morning, and
vomited about two pints of blood, matter and stringy
phlegm. From that time I commenced to improve, and
was gaining in weight every day. About a month ago the
blood ceased coming, the cough disappeared, and the
lung healed completely, and thank God and your pro-
fessional skill, I am as well as ever,
P. KEENAN.
Declared and subscribed before me, one of Her Majesty’s
Justices of the Peace, for the Southern Bailiwick, in the
County of Grant, this 10th day of December, 1888.
Peter Smythb, J.P.

The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in tae
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted
’whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or say 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MSS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for the preservation or transmission
:f such MSS.

By sending direct ffiTiiE Bulletin Office Twenty Shillings
sterling—which you may pay by Post Office Order, One
Pound Note, or Stamps of any Australian colony—you
can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every week
for a year.
This offer is not open to residents of Sydney. In that
city The Bulletin is not delivered by mail, but is entirely
in the hands of our carriers, with whom we do not wish
to interfere.
SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 13, 1890.
“When this Cruel War is Over!”
The long agony endured by the people of
the dis-United States of America during the
continuance of their protracted civil war
found expression, as always happens in such
cases, in the lamentations of poets and the
wailings of musicians. A languid breeze of
pathetic ballads, set to sobbing airs, breathed
throughout the continent. The mournful
cadences were sighed in melodious grief in
millions of desolated or anxious households.
The tortured nation evinced and relieved its
sufferings by the universality of such harmoni-
ous heart-throbs. Among the most touchingof
a multitude of such ballads there is one
known by the initial line of the poetry:
“When this Cruel War is over.” Brief as
has yet been the period of t the contention
justly styled the Great Strike, in Australia,
the sentiments of all the more temperate
among the people and the speculations of the
more thoughtful, already begin to drift in the
direction which those words express. Every
man of feeling longs for the cessation of the
acute stage of strife. None but the super-
ficial imagine that the settlement of the
strike will signify the cessation of the
struggle. The strike, in fact, is not the
disease from which the body-politic is suffer-
ing. It is but a symptom, unpleasant and
painful in itself undoubtedly, but still only a
symptom. Pustules are very offensive and
inconvenient. But it- is the constitutional
derangement which is really the malady of
a small-pox patient. The eruptions are
indicative of the nature of the disease, and,
revolting as they are, of themselves, they
constitute a hopeful sign. If these indica-
tions fail to appear the physician is liable and
apt to make a false diagnosis and prescribe a
fatal treatment. Suppress these outbreak-
ings—the constitution is destroyed and the
patient expires.
* * #
When the subject of the socio-political
disturbance affecting civilised communities
in general is thus comprehensively regarded,
it is readily understood that it matters very
little in what fashion the existing eruption
may come to an end. It is but a pimple on
the body afflicted. There occurred in Great
Britain alone, during the period from 1840
to 1877 (the latest statistics we can lay hands
on relating to this subject) no less than 2352
strikes. But there has been only one
malady. The question really deserving of
attention is—in what manner will that
malady terminate ? The first steps towards
a solution of this question must of course be
to ascertain what the malady actually is, and
the next to investigate its causes. There is
general agreement as to the character of the
disease. It is a social fever. The sufferer—
Civilised Society—is restless, irritable, sub-
ject to convulsions almost epileptic in their
character, the patient losing sense and biting
his own tongue in the paroxysms. He
is afflicted with terrible waste of sub-
stance, with general lowness of spirits oc-
casionally intensified to sheer hopelessness,
with intermittent reactions of irrational
gaiety and sanguineness. The treatment
hitherto recommended and adopted by the
physicians has been very nearly identical
with that period approved for bodily
derangements of all kinds indiscriminately.
In the days of our grandfathers, if a man
had a bilious attack the doctor whipped out
a lancet and bled him. If he broke his leg,
he was bled. If he had a fever, he was
bled. If he were in a condition of collapse
from cholera, he was bled. If a wretch
fainted in the street owing to starvation, he
was promptly relieved of half-a-pint of blood.
If one broke a blood-vessel, the chances
were ten to one that the first practitioner
who got at him would instantly bleed him.
* * *
Those pretenders to skill in treating the
derangements of society—Monarchs and
Ministers—are yet scarcely emerging from
that ridiculous condition of ignorant empiri-
cism from which the medical profession has
extricated itself. The physician and the
surgeon of to-day look back with shame and
indignation upon the brutal practices and
unwarrantable presumption of their by no
means remote predecessors. But the social
physicians are still the arrogant, pretentious
humbugs that these diplomaed quacks
were. They still prescribe blood-letting
for every complaint. Their faith in the
efficacy of the remedy has, it is true, begun
to waver. But on all occasions when they
find themselves nonplussed, and really can-
not conceive what they should properly do,
they take out the lancet and the actual
cautery. Steel and fire, the bayonet and
the cartridge, these are the last resource of
the Governmental quacks, even when they
are not the first. No better illustration of
this tendency is needed than the present
attitude of the Governments of New South
Wales and Victoria. In Sydney and in
Newcastle the troops are readied up, ball-
cartridge is parcelled out, the commanders
of the British war-ships are invoked to be
ready to invade the country with alien blue-
jackets and machine-guns from Woolwich.
In Victoria the soldiery are even more pro-
minently in evidence. In short, the Govern-
ment Sangrado has his lancets out of their
case, and, if the patient takes a bad turn,
the Hon. Sangrado will have his blood
sooner than confess his own incompetence.
# #
Meanwhile a new school of practitioners is
steadily working its way towards acceptance
and appears destined, ere long, to take the
place of the habit-encrusted disciples of the
old formulas. This school it is which pro-
nounces that curing some diseases is for the
most part hopeless, once they enter the
system. Their doctrine is emphatically
that prevention is the true method. If
it be sought to have a healthy com-
munity and to ensure reasonable freedom
from epidemics of a distressing or violent
character, the conditions which generate and
foster such epidemics must be sought for,
ascertained, and eradicated. They profess
to have diagnosed the disease which so
severely affects modern society. It is, they
say, the unequal distribution of wealth—of
all those things, circumstances, and condi-
tions which tend to make entire peoples
comfortable and contented. They point out
that the old blood-spilling practice has abso-
lutely failed—that even when not followed
by fatal consequences, the utmost it has ac-
complished has been to reduce the patient
to a state of transient feebleness, and that
with the restoration of vigour the original
malady has never failed in one single in-
stance to reappear with aggravated intensity.
With unblenching persistency they proceed
to elaborate their modern system, and every
day they gain consideration. At the outset
the public, long accustomed to defer with
almost superstitious awe to the grave pre-
tensions of the pompous practitioners who
supplanted their authority with diplomas
endorsed by ancient usage, and had on their
side all the respectability of big practices,
fine appointments, and responsible profes-
sorial station, looked askance at these new
aspirants, who ventured to proffer advice
without producing vouchers from any recog-
nised stagnant university or decayed school.
But by sheer force of reason and by unim-
peachable demonstration of exceptional abili-
ties, the new school of political teachers have
won their way. Proudhon, Louis Blanc,
and St. Simon have survived the fire of de-
nunciation and affected contempt which
enwrapt their systems for long years. Rous-
seau becomes so increasingly esteemed that
it is felt necessary to revive to-day in refer-
ence to his ideas the attacks which more than
a hundred years ago were directed at his
individuality.? Lassalle and Marx are
names known all the civilised world over.
Among living Englishmen the names of
Hyndman and Morris are nearly as well
known as those of Disraeli and Gladstone,
while that ©f Henry George is fully as well
known. Complete identity of opinion in
matters of detail has not been established
among these teachers. But in this general
principles they are in substantial accord.
Without exception they pronounce that a
healthy Society cannot be expected -unless
those inequalities of condition which deprave
one section by the possession of dropsical
superfluity, another section by the emacia-
tion of painful deprivation, and all by the
fever of perpetual striving, anxiety or sus-
picion, be extirpated and removed.
* * *
Hence it may be understood that while
the success of the great Australian strike
may in some slight degree advance the
period when relief will come to modern
society, its repression would in no wise tend
to an amelioration. It would only drive
inwards an external manifestation, with the
certain result of an aggravation and intensi-
fication of the actual trouble. At the same
time, it would be folly to expect more than
almost imperceptible amelioration of the
general condition from a successful issue of
the Australian effort. Trades-Unionism is
but one ingredient in a compound remedy,
just as Land Nationalisation is another single
ingredient. Each is beneficial in its degree,
and tire latter is essential. But without
other accessories Both would prove inade-
quate. Let it be supposed that Trades-
Unionism were to secure a victory all along
the line. There could be no permanence in
its success. This truth is neatly exemplified
by the case of the London Dock Labourers’
Union. That stands flushed with all the
glow of a recent triumph. Yet at this
moment, after the lapse of but a few months,
the Union totters. It already changes its
character. In lieu of being a combination
to secure to labourers better wages and more
regular employment, it now has assumed
the aspect of a monopolist guild, which re-
fuses to admit to participation any out-
side labour. But outside labour, desperate
and starving, clamours tor admission.
If this demand be acceded to, there will
no longer be employment enough for all
members. If it be refused, the employer
will be able to break down the Union by
availing himself of the men excluded. And
what is true of one Union is true of all, in
this respect.
* # #
Were all the labour in the world to
organise into trades-unions, there would
be the same surplus of unemployed as
there is to-day. And whenever there is a
surplus of unemployed, other conditions re-
maining as they are, wages must fall. For
it matters not at all whether the Union
worker labours fewer hours, at so much per
hour, in order to share out employment
among his mates, or whether working full
time, while his mates remain idle, he con-
tributes to their maintenance. It is true,
indeed, that were all workers banded
together in unions they could demand that
all should share what employment there was,
and that every man should be paid, no
matter how short his hours of work, a wage
adequate to support him in decency and com-
fort. But, unluckily, under existing arrange-
ments, however willing employers might be
to concede these demands rather than to give
up business, they could not make occupation
for all claimants and pay them wages. The
matter is very simply demonstrable. Every
employer seeks to increase the number of his
hands, because he makes some profit on each
individual he employs. If he could increase
his hands he would do so without com-
pulsion. But he does not —because he
cannot. And no compulsion could make
him. In reality there is little jus-
tice in the vindictiveness witli which
employers in general are regarded. Some
deserve it, indeed, on account of their per-
sonal arrogance and griping practices. But
the best and most generous of employers is
restricted in his opportunities of liberality.
If he pay high wages, he contracts his profits.
If he contract his profits, he is less able to
avail himself of opportunities as they arise,
in the way of renewing plant, of introducing
fresh inventions and processes, and in a
hundred other ways. And. he who gets
behind in these essentials is in danger of
dropping out of the race altogether. Com-
petitors undersell him, and his business is
ruined.
* * *
The remedies lie deeper, and are complex.
Land monopoly must be abolished. Other
monopolies must be repressed, and there-
after competition can be dealt with and
co-operation established in its place. Al-
though the present strike be successful,
nothing will have been permanently gained
unless other and larger victories be won. It
is absurd to imagine that wages can long be
maintained at a high level in Australia if
they be at a low level in Europe. Protec-
tion coupled with Trades-Unionism can
stave off the inevitable for a while. And
during the interval additional measures might
be adopted were the people but properly
acquainted with the steps to be taken.
Meanwhile, the most foolish course possible
would be any recourse to violence. The Aus-
tralian Colonies are but dependencies. It is
now that this humiliating condition is most
keenly realisable. A speaker on a platform
at one of the Sydney demonstrations in-
formed the unionists present that they were
the public. This was a silly mistake. But
even if they were 300,000 against 100,000,
instead of, perhaps, 150,000, including all
sympathisers, against 200,000, and could
support a stone-thrower against the police
with a thousand men, and the police, mili-
tary and special constables (as their oppo-
nents, authorised, organised and armed by
the Government, are styled) with 20,000
men, and overwhelm all their local antago-
nists, they would next have to cope with
the greed and-might of the British Empire.
Some Economic Sophisms.
“The iron law of wages” is one of those
fiscal lies invented by economists for the
dual purpose of reconciling the rich to the
sufferings of the poor, and of reconciling the
latter to their hard lot. The meaning of
the inexorable law mentioned is that wages
can never be depressed beneath the point at
which they will support life. This is false
in two particulars. In the first place, the
law does not apply to those who starve
through being unable to find any work at
all; and secondly, a workman, even when
in constant employment, may have hours so
long, and food so inadequate or unwhole-
some, as to mean for him a short life and a
sad one. This is well put by a recent writer
who says : —*
What is the minimum of income that will support an
individual '/ Evidently it is that which will keep his
system in a good condition, and allow him to develope
fully and attain to the natural limit of his life. As soon
as he attempts more than his system is capable of endur-
ing, or gets less food, warmth, and sleep than his system
requires to remain at the summit of its type, then he
falls into physiological distress. Over-work is as equally
the cause of organic decline as insufficient food, but
the latter is synonymous with slow starvation.
* #
But it is not necessary to go beyond
statistics to see that this “iron law,” which
complacently assumes that every labourer
earns at least as much as will keep his body
in the highest state of efficiency, is funda-
mentally unsound. Recent vital tables show
that the wage-earning classes in European
countries enjoy an average life of from one-
third to one-half less than their well-to-do
fellow-countrymen. Yet all live in the same
land, with the same sky above and the same
soil below. What is the cause of this fearful
mortality that robs the workman not alone
of the pleasures of life, but of a considerable
portion of life itself 1 Long hours, insuffi-
cient or insufficiently-nutritious food, occa-
sional spells of actual hunger, and insanitary
surroundings from his cradle. It is only
necessary to wander through the slums of
any great English or European city to see that
the conditions under which vast masses of men
live are far worse than those which surround
the life of the savage. This state of things
has not yet reached Australia, but it is
fairly on the way. So far, then, as Europe
is concerned, the “iron law of wages” is a
transparent fraud. And if the workmen on
this side of the world are not careful and
loyal to each other, the time may come when
its falsity will be proved by sad experience
in what within twenty years will, we hope,
be the Australian Republic.
** , *
Two other economic fallacies lie on the
surface. They have been repeated so often,
have so frequently passed from mouth to
mouth or from writer to writer, that simple
people are half inclined to believe them
much as many simple people to this day
believe in that most vulgar of all blasphemies
—the divine right of kings. The fallacies
alluded to are (a) that pauperism began with
the advent of man himself, and (6) that in
earlier ages of the world the disproportion
between rich and poor was even greater than
now. In the world’s earlier ages pauperism,
as we are familiar with it, was unknown.
A strong or unscrupulous people might over-
come a weak and carry them into slavery ;
but if they made them work they at least fed
them well. Besides, poverty is both abso-
lute and relative. The England of the 14th
and 15th centuries was a much poorer
country than the England of to-day. But
the physical well-being of the workers from
the hind up was far superior to what it is
now. This has been abundantly proved by
the researches of Professor Thorold Rogers.
So long as a man can so fully supply his physi-
cal wants so that by abundant food, vest and
leisure his organism has a chance of coming
to its fullest maturity he cannot bo called
poor, even if clad in home-spun and un-
acquainted with the thousand-and-one in-
ventions of science for making life agreeable.
But to tell a starving man that he cannot
be. poor, must be prosperous, for his native
land exported a hundred millions worth of
goods and imported a similar amount during
the year; to argue that peasants of the present
can obtain luxuries unknown to monarchs
of the past is cruelty run mad.
* * *
Absolute poverty is a different thing. It
is a product of our modern civilisation. You
feel no compassion for the relatively poor
man. Diogenes was not remarkably wealthy,
but, from all accounts, he seems to have
been remarkably comfortable. The world
still contains many savage and semi-savage
nations, whose inhabitants, although rela-
tively poor in the main, neither expect nor
desire sympathy. Nature informs them
when they are hungry, and experience
teaches them how to get over the incon-
venience. But a highly-developed civilisa-
tion on the present lines invariably condemns
a large proportion of every nation to abso-
lute poverty—to that physiological distress
which renders such words as “manhood,”
“self-respect,” and “independence” mere
expressions of mockery. This is a fact that
cannot be gainsaid, that no sophistry will
dissipate, and that is forcing itself daily upon
the notice of those who—wedded to the old
theories of supply and demand, of unlimited
production and constant cheapening —vainly
endeavour to close the door against its un-
welcome entrance. The real question, then,
is : Can such a state of things be altered. 1 Is
it necessary that many, or any, should perish
in order that many, or any, should ostenta-
tiously flourish?
# * *
There remains the other blunder—that in
the world’s earlier ages the disproportion


betweeii rich and poor was even greater than
now. If anything, this is more glaring than
the preceding. Asa recent author puts it a
little brusquely : “It is all only a trumped-
up, learixed fraud.” No medieval fortunes
were lit to be named beside the American
and European hoards of to-day. Even in
Old Rome, when a consul returned with the
plunder of a province, he usually lost both
it and his life together. Very little of it
came to his offsprixxg, except when they were
extremely alert and, in more senses than
one, were in at their father’s death. But at
present there are, as our author points
out, scattered thx’ough the world not
alone millionaires by the hundred, but
thousands upon thousands of people with
princely fortunes and incomes. These are
the people who, for old fiddles axxd old china,
give sums that would feed many faxxxilies for
life. It is in the face of facts like these —
that never befox’e in the world’s history were
there so many enorxxxously rich people, and
never before such a multitude of abjectly
poor —that political economists of the dainty
modern type have the assurance to proclaim
the glad tidings of the gradually-narrowing
disproportion between rich and poor. Any-
one who undex’stands the case can confidently
give to this a flat contradition.
* * *
Almost at the same moment two essayists,
one tlxe American Bellamy, the other the
Hungarian Max IS ordau, have dealt at length
with the lamentable state of things at present
existing. There is as great a difference between
the books asbetween their methods, foroptim-
ism is the dominant xxote in one and pessimism
in the other. Yet, even in “Looking Back-
ward,” whose object is to teach that it is
possible to lay the foundations of a society
not, as at present, in human baseness, but in
human virtue, there are many passages
which make one ask “is it so visionary to
think that such a state of things could be 1”
Men like Bismarck deride the idea—utterly
contemn it when they stoop to mention it at
all. The Napoleons, Fredericks and
(Lesars of the past as well as the Jay Goulds,
Vanderbilts and Rothschilds of the present
would certainly act in the same way and
think that the place Socialists would best
adorn would be a cart’s tail. Why 1 Bis-
marck himself told us the other day that So-
cialism would bring all men down to a dead
level, and give no proper scope for ambition.
This is delicious. It would certainly bring
kaisers and statesmen to their proper level,
prevent their hounding on millions to throttle
each other, and banish hunger from the face
of the earth. But fancy anyone declaring
there would be no scope for ambition in a
society that gave to all a chance of venturing
their speed and bottom on the illimitable
highways of literature, science, art, and in-
vention ! So far from giving no scope for
ambition, it would give to multitudes chances
for which they vainly strain and hunger under
existing conditions. Suppose the Bismarcks
and Krupps did fail in the race % They could
always fall back into the positions Nature in-
tended them to fill, and pass their timo com-
fortably in healthy manual labour.
* * *
M. Nordau’s work, of which we print a
first instalment in this issue of The Bulletin,
is of an entirely different order. It is the
carefully-written work of a subtle thinker,
and has power stamped upon every line.
What is M. Nordau’s panacea for the
world’s ills ? Briefly this —“The right of
inheritance must be abolished.” This is
precisely what Bellamy says—the difference
txeing that the Hungarian would abolish it
while preserving Individualism, the Ameri-
can while introducing Socialism. Which is
right ? We lean to the American side. But
in neither case can the desired result be
brought about without a long and bitter
struggle —a struggle whose end is certain,
but whose course and period no man can
foresee. There must be a welding together
of all wage-earners—not of those who work
with their hands alone, who wield the pick
and the hammer, but of those far less for-
tunate and less independent ones who spend
their lives in shop, factory, or office. Nothing
could withstand such a combination. There
would quickly be a Parliament vociferous ixx
the cause of Labour. Everything else would
follow in its proper place. Can such a con-
summation be attaixxed ? It is the opinion
of practical men that it can.
On Bad Liquor and other Matters.
The human race drinks a good deal too much,
and the world’s old maiden aunt is harassed
thereby. The teetotal cause is vastly stronger
than it was ten years ago, and at the same time
the national drink-bill is a great deal larger.
The demand for anti-liquor legislation and the
demand for liquor keep oh growing together.
There is a coxxstantly ixxcreasing trade in those
good little books and snuffle-busting pamphlets,
ixx which Thomas J ones is represented as coxxxing
home to his once happy domicile, and runxxixxg
amok among the furniture with an axe; but
they appear to leave xxo perceptible effect behind
them. Yet the attractioxxs of drink, so far as
they are visible to any intelligent understand-
ing, are rather diminishing than increasing.
Whisky, as a rule, is partly kerosene and the
rest liquid fire, and to the persoxx who meets it for
the first time, it resembles nothing so much as
a live lobster swallowed with the claws fore-
most. Brandy and the rich old wines of Europe
labour under a serious disadvantage, inasmuch
as the quantity openly produced is only one-
third or thereabouts of the amount coxxsumed,
and where the balance comes from no one knows.
The vine-clad slopes where xxxost of the world’s
liquor is produced are situated up three pairs of
stairs in a back-slum, axxd the unwashed culti-
vator sleeps oxx top of the vintages, and the
mellow sunshine which ripens the harvest is
kept in a keg, and the poetry and bright romance
of the wine-cup are bought at the chemical
factory round the comer. Yet the world hangs
on tenaciously to its kerosene and carriage-
varnish, axxd the world’s old maidexx aunt bangs
herself about to very little purpose. Teetotalism
is spreading and so is liquor, and it is difficult
to say w hich is spreading the faster.
* * #
The fact is that a great deal too much of the
religious element is imported into the contest.
Teetotalism should take its staxxd solely oxx
three simple truths—first, that the huxxxan in-
side is not strong enough to exxdure a coxxstaxxt
swallowing of explosives and combustibles;
aecoxxd, that if the world insists on devouring
about twice as much liquor as it can conveniently
supply, it must not be surprised when it finds
that it is consuming all sorts of substances that
are about as wholesome as pounded glass ; and
third, that ixx any case the humaix race can’t
afford the bill. On these three issues there is
room to conduct a square and honourable con-
test, and the long-tailed coat and holy hymxx
which are so extensively imported into the dis-
cussioxx are merely so much extraneous idiocy.
If gin, as compounded nowadays, produces a
xxefarious action on the liver, the public may be
interested in the fact, but the observations on
the drink question which Solomon left behind
him have no effect either when applied in-
ternally or rxxbbed on outside. The only
tangible result is that one sectioxx of the com-
munity spends its time in hunting for verses in
condemnatioxx of rum axxd similar fluids, and
another section wastes its energies searching
for quotations in support of them, and both
pax’ties meet with a reasonable amount of
success. Then the question dribbles into all
manner of side issues, and presently all chance
of an improvement in the quality of “ colonial
beer ” is indefinitely postponed on account of a
difficulty about the pants of the ancient
prophets, or the authenticity of the second
epistle of Peter, or some equally thrilling
problem of aixeient date. The mere fact
that the men who pray endeavour to arrogate
to themselves the sole right to refornx a world
which drinks too much, makes it tolerably cer-
tain that the men who swear will take the
opposite view, and the latter are always much
the more numerous and interesting section of
the two. It is pleasanter axxd much more
agreeable to take to drink, even whexx there is
more thaxx a suspicion of kerosene in the flowing
bowl, than to follow a person with a great, sad
expaxxse of shirt-collar and a corporation which
is blown out with beef and tea and buns, and
a Scripture sticking obtrusively out of the hind-
pocket of a doleful-coloured tail-coat, and a fat,
patronising utterance and a wearisome text
which is always ready to be heaved at the
sinner. The average temperance apostle is an
individual who makes the world feel very tired,
* * #
Teetotal literature, too, falls flat on account
of its exceeding solemnity. There never was a
humorous observatioxx in all the hundred thou-
sand weary, tear-sodden volumes which exhibit
the cussedness of drink to a lachrymose world.
The Church never made a joke in its life, and
seldom appreciated one, and the Church ixxsists
on editing teetotalism till it leaves nothing ixx it
but dxxst and ashes. Now and then the temper-
ance journal attempts to lighten up its pages by
a profound, abysmal, bottomless article on the
disgraceful condition of the kidney of some
drunkard who died miserably and was dissected
at the hospital, but anybody, except the tee-
total fanatic who wrote the thing, is apt to con-
sider, after reading it, that some more success-
ful way of illuminating the earth might be
discovered by a little vigorous research. Ex-
cepting a funeral, with a ton of paving-stones
on top of it, there is xxothing heavier on earth
than a temperaxxce exhortation, and then before
the effort gets into print it is edited by some
weeping apostle or saved undertaker, who
paints it a deeper black than it was before, and
throws sundry new shades over it, and buries it
a hundred fathoms deep in a sepulchre of type,
and mourns grievously over its last resting-
place. The joys that lie in pernicious beer,
and whisky adulterated with lightning, may be
limited, but even aloes or castor-oil are xxxore
cheerful than the outpourings of the teetotal
edition ; and the horrible solemnity which en-
shrines the cause is a difficulty of xxo ordinary
kind.
# # *
Good liquor, as a rule, is ruinously expexxsive,
and our interest in bad liquor is limited, for we
prefer to die by some shorter and less obtrusive
xnethod. The Australian publican, regarded as
a man and a brother, is a failure ; he is unwhole-
some and puffy and fat, axxd not calculated to
adorn a summer landscape. His bar is generally
dingy and gritty, axxd tlxe malarious loafer who
haunts it is unpicturesque and offensive, axxd
the barmaid who attends it is a hopeless
and heartless deceiver. Liquor, ixx general,
is a curse, and teetotalism is another.
The prospect of a world that is filled with
hiccups and whose feet are tied ixp ixx a hard
knot is eminently offensive, the dream of a
world of teetotal drivel and cold tea is, if
possible, a shade worse. If drink axxd teetotal-
ism could be wiped out simultaneously, and the
bar and the pioxxs bixxx-struggle could go together,
axxd the public-house blasphemy and the washy
temperance tract were both extinct literature,
and the dethroned barmaid would marry the
superfluous temperance lectux’er, and they would
go away axxd make a respectable livelihood in
soxxxe place whex’e they would xxever draw public
attention any mox’e or rouse any further con-
troversy, then a golden age of peace and rest
woxxld dawn upoxx this troubled earth.
* # *
If the teetotaller is willing to jxxstify his fad
by obtaining reasonable compeixsatioxx to the men
whose property he desires to confiscate, then we
cordially invite him to go on with his labours
and win if he can—and if he loses instead it
will suit us equally well. If it were possible
that both factions could lose our satisfaction
would be complete, but at least there is gratifi-
cation in the knowledge that both cannot
possibly win. But to the teetotaller who howls
for confiscation and ruin, and illustrates his
position by tales concei’ning the holy mission of
Joshua, and the necessity of extirpating the
Amalekites, we can only point out that frenzy
of this kind is out of date, and when a Joshua
goes forth to extirpate an Amalekite in these
days he is apt to be hanged for his enthusiasm.
But so long as this scriptural afflatus is not im-
ported into the contest we can cheerfully stand
aside and let matters take their own course. If
the public demands refreshment for man and
beast—and particularly for beast, as he is much
the more numerous of the two—it will probably
succeed in obtaining it, and if there is a wide-
spread feeling in favour of something more
cheering than cold tea the natural law of supply
and demand will doubtless meet the emergency.
Therefore we present our whole-souled curse to
both combataxxts—to the maxx who compounds
bad liquor, axxd the man who seeks to abolish
good—and trust that like the rain it may affect
them both alike. If two Chinamen choose to
fight it is a matter of small importance to any
average white maxx which Mongol gets oxx top.
And if the relevancy of this remark* should fail
to strike many of our readers we can only
lament the fact that teetotalism and dubious
colonial beer both lead to density of intellect
and that each unfits its victim to comprehend
an intelligent observation when it is placed
before his muddled eye.
A Legal Swindle.
A Bill was lately introduced into the New
South Wales Legislative Council by Mr.
Trickett to amalgamate the legal professions,
and was so framed as to do it thoroughly. Its
effect would have been to efface altogether the
distinction between the lawyer in a wig and the
lawyer without one. Had it passed there would
have been nothing to prevent any solicitor from
instructing the Full Court in the proper con-
struction of the Act 28 Geo. IV., c. 655, in the
light of Privy Council’s decision in ex parte
Pokehorney, with all the jaunty confidence of
the young gentleman who put on his horsehair
for the first time the week before last; and this
young gentleman in his turn would have had all
the law could give him—for the protection of
the public—in converting his clients’ securities
into cash for use at Cup-time. The Bill cut both
ways. It removed the prohibition against a
solicitor’s putting his case before the Supreme
Court without the intervention of a barrister;
and it removed the prohibition against a
barrister’s dealing with the public except
through the intervention of an attorney. The
client who employed Mr. Solicitor Trickett
to get up a case might, if he chose, let him see
it through from start to finish without paying a
big fee to Barrister Salomons, Q.C., to “ men-
tion the matter” and otherwise talk to the
judge ; and on the other hand he might employ
Mr. Salomons to attend personally to the whole
thing, and explain his business to that gentle-
man himself, without paying Mr. Trickett to
put it all down on a brief. While the Bill was
in this shape, it met with bald-headed opposi-
tion from the Parliamentary barrister party,
and no secret was made of the intention to
block it in committee. However, just as the
obstruction was about to begin, an amicable
arrangement was made, an amendment blandly
moved by Attorney-General Simpson was agreed
to, and the Bill with the amendment was re-
ported to the House.
The effect of the amendment is simply this:
the distinction between the professions remains
untouched ; the Bill as altered leaves the senior
barrister exactly where he is to-day ; but it
gives the solicitor the right of audience before
the Supreme Court. At first sight this looks
like a concession by the Q.O’s. in the Council,
of the privileges the solicitors wanted, with a
generous waiver of the privileges they were to
have had in return. But first impressions
are misleading ; and the fact that the
alteration of this Bill was the result of an
agreement between lawyers is in itself a
circumstance fraught with the gravest sus-
picion. Judge Windeyer is perfectly right
when he says, in effect, that the senior Bar has
squared matters with the attorneys by throwing
the. junior Bar overboard. There is not a prac-
tising barrister in the New South Wales Upper
House who is not a Q.C. or on the verge of
becoming one, and a Bill like the present one
affects them in a very different way from the
young men whose tailors have to trust to the
precarious fees of the Probate Court. The
original proposal was an honest give and take,
and promised the public a fair show. A solicitor
was to be entitled to do the work for which he
has now to pay a quite unnecessary fee to. a
junior barrister; and the junior barrister, by
way of squaring matters, was to have a chance
to cut into the solicitor’s business. None of the
senior Bar, however, would be touched by that.
No solicitor would think of going into Court to
do the really heavy work for which he now
finds it worth while to fee big men like Salo-
mons or Jack Want ; and, on the other hand,
Salomons and Jack Want, Q.C.’s, couldn’t
afford to fool round with trumpery clients and
collect six-and-eightpences. It is only the
young barrister whose work would be taken
away by the invasion of the unwigged, and only
they who would take advantage of the right to
deal with the public direct.
# # *
That is not what was the matter with
the senior Bar. It was none of their
trouble. But what the Bill would have done
with the senior Bar was this. It would have
made them responsible for the work they
are paid to do. It would have interfered with
their little game of “refreshers.” The man in
the silk gown with cases in four courts at once,
besides a consultation in his own chambers
which occupied the whole day, would find that
the guineas marked on his briefs didn’t get into
his bank-account ; if he collected his fees
beforehand he would have to refund them, and
an unfeeling jury would tot up the damages
which his clients had sxxffered from his scamp-
ing their cases. The man who is ready
to work for his money, whether he be a
barrister or a bricklayer, doesn’t object
to taking the responsibility for his work,
and doesn’t undertake three jobs when he know s
he can only thoroughly attend to one of them.
To put the barrister and the bricklayer on the
same footing with respect to their contracts
would promptly distribute the work that is now
scandalously neglected by a half score men at
the Bar, amongst younger men who would
attend to it and would be willing to be respon
sible for it. That was the trouble. That was
the secret of the objection to the Bill on the
part of the senior Bar. And that is what
makes us say there is something fishy about a
compromise which gives the solicitors all they
want, which scrupulously avoids any encroach-
ment on the monopoly enjoyed by the senior
members of the Bar, and which patches up a
peace at the expense of the juniors. The Bill
in its amended form is simply a shameless and
conscienceless put-up job.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Jingoes at Work.
Late intelligence from the New Hebrides is
directed towards giving a spurt to Australian
Jingoism. The settlers in those islands are
represented as being dissatisfied with the
appointment of Dr. Hanedr as French repre-
sentative of New Caledonia, and, through New
Caledonia, of France. It is also stated that a
combination of all the settlers outside of the
“ French Company ” has been formed to agitate
for the annexation of the New Hebrides to
Australia, Even Captain M‘Leod, who was
negotiating to sell his interests to the “ French
Co.,” is “ off ” his proposed bargain, and stands
in with the new Australian px-oject. The pro-
moters ask for an “official representative ” from
Australia. What sort of status in French eyes
would an Australian “ beadle,” or “ consul,” or
“agent” have? Let us talk sense once in a
while. Australia has enough to do to mind her
own business as yet, and her first paramount
duty is to achieve her own freedom—absolute,
untrammelled and isolated. Then, and not till
then, may she consider her own “ Monroe
doctrine ” in all its bearings, together with her
reasonable claims to influence the nearer islands of
the Pacific. Australia has a hard and complicated
battle before her. But the complications force
their own unravelment and thus actually hasten
the day of Australian liberation. Just now the
Imperial faddists are busily at work, and they
have their shadow-echoes numerously and
not uninfluentially represented in Australia.
Parkes’s inglorious scheme of “ Federation
under the Crown ” has evoked a return bark
from Canada’s Sir John Macdonald, Public-
ists, too, are promulgating schemes for the
closer commercial and political alliance between
Australia, Canada, Africa, and India—all as a
subsidiary cob-web to a “ great British Em-
pire.” The strings are pulled by Imperial
Federationists. Let us sniff suspiciously at all
such high-falutin sentiments. An infallible
test is that any scheme that does not offer the
gold of the highest individual and State free-
dom—the government of ourselves by ourselves,
and for ourselves, without reference to anyone
else whomsoever—but presents instead the
tinsel of a gilded-over Imperialistic bondage, is
essentially evil. Meanwhile, let the New
Hebrides work out its own salvation, and let
us work out our’s—not in fear and trembling,
but in hope and confidence.

News of Battle.
There are all manner of warlike demonstrations
in progress in various parts of Australia. The
awkward squad is getting its gun ready, and
Authority, as the friend of Monopoly, is ob-
trusively shining up its battle-axe in readiness
for that revolt which it knows will never come,
and which, if it really did come, would make
Monopoly gather up its carpet-bag and take a
bee-line for the hills. In Melbourne, Sydney,
and other cities the wild note of preparation
fills the aii’, and as no one wants to rise in in-
surrection the rulers of the State feel that it is
safe and convenient to strike a military attitude,
and announce that all insurrection will be
promptly squashed. The undersized softgoods-
clerk has sworn himself in as a special constable,
and it is generally understood that if Anarchy
rears its offensive head he wfill be prepared to
swim in blood. The lumbering money-grabber
has quitted his store or his pawnshop to
rampage round with a baton in the theatre ol
war, and all manner of unwar like heroes are
ready to die or scream, or run away, in defence
of a Constitution which no one is attempting to
attack. Proclamations have been issued here
and there to the effect that if two or more people
are seen landing in one place, and not doing
anything, or if one person is observed standing
anywhere and keeps on remaining in that
attitude, he may be lawfully called upon to
disperse in various directions ; and if he declines
to do so a magistrate shall be authorised to
arrive on the scene and collect an inquiring
crowd by reading aloud a long blue document,
and if the document does not take effect in
fifteen minutes the troops may be called out and
the loafer’s blood will be on his own head. At
any other time and under any other circum-
stances the insurgent would be removed by the
ear to the police court and fined in the sum of
five shillings, but as this is an insurrectionary
epoch in which nobody insurrects it is considered
necessary to lend a certain air of military fervour
to the proceedings. It is even propose'! that a
general mobilisation should take place * Muni-
tions of war are also being hunted up, and the
dyed sawdust which was passed off as gunpowder
has been duly overhauled ; and the big gun,
with the cannon-ball that is six sizes bigger,
and won’t go in in consequence, is getting into
working order, and it has been duly discovered
that the gunner in charge doesn’t know how to
work it; and elaborate measures have been
taken for the defence of the Treasury, lest an
excited ujob should rush in and seize the de-
ficiency Also, paragraphs appear at intervals
in the daily Press setting forth that the various
Governments are filled with the calm of stony
resolution, and are prepared to die at their posts
through old age while waiting for a non-existent
enemy ; and while they are waiting for death
they will continue to draw their salaries and
pickings with that quenchless courage which
always animates the politician on pay-day.
The same heroic solemnity has spread through
the ranks of their subordinates, and the various
departments will present an iron front to the


hordes of spoilers from nine to five as heretofore,
and will fill in their time by sketching barmaids
and actresses on a convenient blotting pad ac-
cording to custom, with that deathless heroism
which is the secret of Britain’s greatness.
Owing to these and other precautions it is
believed that the country may yet be saved,
though no one has discovei'ed why it should
want saving now any more than it does at other
times. And when the danger which never
existed has passed away, and the people who
never dreamt of rising haven’t risen, and the
softgoods counter-jumper lays down his amateur
baton and ceases to be a cheap, unnecessary
hero, and peaee has been restored after being
continued all along, the names of the men who
got up and made an exhibition of themselves to
no purpose will be' embalmed in history, and
they will have their reward in the gratitude of a
«muntry that will never be able to find out what
it has to be grateful,for.
Moral Suasion.
Capital always protests against the pressure of
force. That is to say, it protests against force
when someone else applies it to Capital, and the
peaceful and legitimate pressure which the
trades unions have employed in order to main-
tain their right to co-operate for their own pro-
tection has roused the monopolist press to
frantic indignation. If Labour has any wrongs,
moral suasion should be employed to set them
right ; the inert influence of public opinion
should be brought to bear on the case ; and
argument and rhetoric, and gentle exhortation
should be made use of to bring about a general
reformation. It is true that the monopolist
cares not one straw for moral suasion, and the
weight of 'public opinion has as little influence
on his conscience as a pound of butter might
have if fired at the brazen gates of Tophet, and
argument and rhetoric have no effect on his
bank-account, which is his only vulnerable
point; but he always demands that Labour
should trust to these empty weapons, and be
content. More than a generation ago Charles
Dickens drew a vivid picture of the miseries of
the London deck-labourer—that waif and stray
who slept in a kennel, and lived on a crust, and
gained a miserable pittance by hanging on to
the skirts of commerce. No stronger moral in-
fluence was ever applied in a good cause ; but
though the sympathy thus aroused was illimit-
able, and the air was filled with snuffle and
bogus philanthropy, the dock-labourer was left
as before with his attic and his precarious crust,
until the force of combination did for him
what no amount of sympathy and cheap, un-
considered drivel would ever have effected.
Hood’s pitiful “ Bong of the Shirt ” drew forth
an ocean of wasted tears, and though years
have passed since the world first read it, the
tears are flowing yet —and the unhappy pariah
for whose salvation that sad lament was written,
sews in her lonely room to-day for lower wages
than she received when Hood endeavoured to
arouse the world with the tale of her miseries.
W ALTER Besant, when he wrote the “ Children
of Gibeon,” made a brilliant effort to do what
Hood had failed to accomplish, and as the
result of his endeavours, philanthropic societies
were formed, and pompous addresses delivered,
and secretaries and treasurers and committees
were hired and paid, and the moral atmosphere
was filled with wailing and gas and fat
hypocrisy, and the sewing-woman of Loudon
remains just where she was before. The law
of elevenpence-halfpenny a day for sixteen
hours of weary toil still prevails, for experience
has proved that a woman can live—or rather
exist—and., work for a bloated Christian
employer, and die quietly of cold and misery
and want and premature old age, and be buried
by the parish without any trouble or ungentle-
manly fuss, on that wretched maximum ; and
as woman has never learnt the art of combina-
tion, or discovered how to obtain redress by
Force, she has no prospect of relief except
through outside assistance. But, thanks to
Hood and Besant and other sympathisers, her
employer has been induced, by the influence
of public opinion, to subscribe an in-
finitesimal fraction of the money he has ground
out of his victim to one or other of the
fashionable societies organised for her relief ;
and the secretary has got his salary out of that
subscription, and the committee has used it to
liquidate the bill for the official lunch, and the
printing of the annual report has been charged
against it, and the hungry toiler who paid for
all this thrice-accursed philanthropy remains
hungry and in rags to this day. The amount of
moral suasion contained in such a book as
“ Uncle Tom's Cabin ” was incalculable, but
the work which its author meant to do had to
be done by the Federal bayonets at Gettysburg
and the Wilderness all the same. Victor Hugo
compressed a world of burning woe into “ Les
Miserables” and the “Toilersof the Sea,” and
the result was practically nil. Moral force,
unsupported by the brute strength of numbers
and combined physical effort, is an empty
thing; and the protest of Monopoly against
what it is pleased to term the lawlessness of
Trades-Unionism is merely an invitation to the
masses to spike the only weapon that ever did
or will ever do them any service. In employing
material force by strikes and boycotting,
Labour employs the one argument against which
the rhinoceros-hide is not proof. There is
more knowledge, but no more conscience or
humanity, in the world than there was a cen-
tury ago, and if by any possibility the power of
the Trades Unions could be destroyed, Hood’s
lament would gain a wider application, and the
worker would become again the hopelessly-
degraded slave that he was in the days of old.

Andrew Geddes, a Melbourne City Councillor,
major in the Militia, treasurer of public
charities, and pillar of the Church, has gone to
chokee for three brief months, during which
time he will be carefully preserved from contact
with the common or garden kind of prisoner
who steals boots or chickens, and left to
ruminate over the fact that the Law makes
considerable distinction between Tweedledum
and Tweedledee. Geddes, whose engagements
ran into Heaven knows how much, was charged
with misappropriating a single tenner, and tiie
font of Justice, as represented by J edge Casev,
evidently assumed that the poor councillor
couldn’t help it, and the unfortunate major
deserved much sympathy, and the church-
warden was a persecuted individual. Anyway,
this combination of greatness got off with about
95 per cent, less sentence than is usually served
out to the postman who steals a registered letter,
and a much more genteel form of punish-
ment than descends upon the larrikin,
who says dam within the hearing of the police.
One witness for the defence stated that he
hoped up to the last minute that Geddes’
defalcations would be made good by public
subscription, or some similar means, in which
case the prisoner would have waltzed out of
court without a stain on his character, and
have continued to carry round the Sunday
offertory-dish harder than ever. However, the
excellent Geddes had embezzled a lot
more than could be “ arranged,” so it
became the painful duty of Judge Casey to
pass sentence; and as that stern, unyielding
guardian of morality could make no distinction
between persons—for if he did so “the law would
be brought into disrepute ” —why Geddes got
three solid months of seclusion from the world
and the other prisoners ! The Bulletin doesn’t
wish to kick a Melbourne city councillor when
he’s down, or trample on a prostrate Mayor’s
feelings, still less does a respectable journal
desire to inflict pain upon a churchwarden.
Therefore we will merely observe that Geddks*
with heaps of luck to assist him and every
possible inducement to remain honest, was a
humbug who has once again proved that it is
easier for a camel to enter the Kingdom of
Heaven than for an “influential citizen” to get
his deserts.

Mr, Hyndman, the English Socialist, casts a
side-light on the advantages of labour :
No industry which was carried on under conditions
which meant degradation to those who produced that in-
dustry was profitable. At the present moment health
was being sapped in every way and in every trade.
Physiologists said that eight hours’ hard work a day was
too much. A horse could not be worked more than three
or four hours a day. The effect of overwork was that the
working-classes died at just half the age of those who
lived upon labour—namely, at 27 instead of 55. During
periods of strike or distress the death-rate actually fell
below what was normal, although the workpeople were
exposed to semi-starvation ; and it was the majority who
at the present time were being slave-driven and injured
for the benefit of a comparative few. If that was so the
Legislature w'as bound to interfere. In every industry in
the country the power of each workman to produce wealth
had been multiplied a hundredfold in the last 50 years.
Why was it that the workers alone should reap no benefits
from the change ?
It is a pity that the world cannot call together
a congress of its hoary veterans in order to
verify this statement. Queen Victoria would
be there collecting her salary with her accus-
tomed vigour at the ripe age of 72, and along
with her Field-Marshal Gamp, as healthy and
vigorous as when he fled in consternation from
the Alma nearly 40 years ago. One of Nelson’s
old friends is still successfully drawing his pen-
sion as an Admiral on the verge of his 100th
birthday, and his presence would compensate in
some measure for the loss of Gamp’s mother
who was prematurely cut off at the age of 92.
William, the intoxicated and unwashed King
of Holland, would shine at the gathering and
prove conclusively that when a man has no
work to do he can bear up against nearly 9(»
years of dirt and beer ; and the House of Lords
could send along a noble contingent of octo-
genarian peers, and successfully explain the
absence of many more who married shady
actresses in their old age and were prematurely
jawed to death in consequence. And Parkes
could attend to testify that politics are more
healthy than the button-making industry, and
Robertson could endorse his statement in the
beautifully choice language for which he is
famous. And, as for the men of toil, the bulk of
them are mostly pretty old at 45, and rheumatic
and venerable at 50, and at 60 they are only a
memory and a tombstone, for which reasons
they would probably be unable to attend.
Apropos of certain N.S.W. Parliamentary
occurrences last week, we may remark that very
rarely indeed are corrupt overtures made to a
reputedly virtuous politician, any more than
they are to a reputedly virtuous woman. There
are exceptions to every rule, but, broadly and
figuratively speaking, the member who is
approached with a bribe has sealskin on his
back, rouge on his lips, and dye on his hair, and
is dawdling late at night under a gas-lamp on
the political side-walk. What a wealth of un-
conscious humour there was in the naive admis-
sion made not long since by a N.S.W. member
of Parliament: “I hadn’t been in the House
six weeks, Mr. Speaker, before I had a dozen
bribes offered me.” The wise man who does
happen to be offered a bribe never says any-
thing about it—any more than the wise woman
whose virtue is affronted ever seeks to publish
the circumstance to the world.
The N.S.W. Art Society’s Dinner.
The New South Wales Art Society’s annual
banquet, which is justly regarded as being of
an exalted description amongst the effete social'
“ functions ” of our day and generation, was even
better this year than ever. It was less pre-
Raphaelite and officially ornate, and rather more
impressionist in its character. Parkes wasn’t
there, nor Governor 0., nor Alfred Bennett, nor
several other people whose possession of metal
decorations and yawns of more or less aristocratic
chasmality make them the envy of the dazzled
throng. Then there was an air of chastened
wisdom in the ministerial arrangements. Dan the
Jaynil, the resplendent postage-stamp of public
life, was present in a dual capacity. He repre-
sented not alone several moral aspects of Free-
trade ; he blossomed over the festive chalice of
literature and the fine arts as the friend of Phidias
and the member from Greece. And the M ‘Million
was there to illuminate with the electric rays of
his wholesale-warehouse intelligence all those
moral aspects of Ereetrade and all those scintillat-
ing facets of ancient Hellas which were too insig-
nificant for the mighty brain of the O'Connor to
sweep into the waste-bin of ministerial culture.
* * #
The speeches were decidedly better, though few
were so well-prepared as the turkey that groaned
•m the artistic palette. Then there was an inno-
vation of a cultivated and refined and high-toned
character—an innovation which was only an inno-
vation to the barbarous natives who chop out their
artistic sensibilities from iron-bark back-logs, and
whose notions of chiaroscuro are fashioned from
beef-wood shingles and undressed garden palings.
for the innovation put on the guise of an angel of
light and came down to us in a dress-suit from the
days of King Lud, and his “function” was to
issue proclamations from behind the President’s
chair touching the healths of various people whom
it was thought proper to swallow in a liquid form.
Ah ! it was a touching sight, but the occasion was
deprived of considerable of its impressive awe-
someness inasmuch as the Toast-master-hereto
alleged—had forgotten his trumpet and his armo-
rial quarterlies and looked no more imposing than
a fish-waiter in an oyster-saloon; moreover, his
voice suggested early prawns, and he had his hair
oiled. Nevertheless, he is a long-felt want, and
he marks a distinct advance in artistic culture in
this land; likewise, it is so,English, you know,
deah boy !
# * #
The artists drank the Queen and the Queen’s
colonial effigy with that frigid respect and suppressed
emotional calm which seems so perfectly to suit
toasts of this kind, and then the president of the
Art Society erected himself and made several
patronising allusions to the Ministry. This
attack brought the M‘Million to his feet,
and, passing his hand wearily over his brow, he
proceeded to unwind all he had heard and read
about art in the newspapers and otherwhere,
and to pretend to know something definite
and conclusive in the matter. But he finally
gave it up as more hopeless than the
Chinese cause in New South Wales, and
more confusing than the complications of a
budget. In spite of his efforts, printed calicoes
and painted canvases would get confusedly mixed,
and after having, in defiance of some anticipated
contradiction, courageously asserted that art was
art, he sat down with a mixed-15oking bump of
perception and a relieved appearance, and simply
devoted himself to smiling at Daniel bearding the
lions in their native den.
* * *
Now, if there is any sight on earth more im-
pressive than the Laokoon or the lions in
Trafalgar Square, it is the Jaynial speaking to
the toast of “the ladies,” or unfolding the mas-
sive creases of his brain on a question ■ like art,
assisted by side-lights from Lempriere’s classical
dictionary and the reminiscential exhortations of
Sheridan Moore. Dan’s erudition is abysmal,
but it is tilled with flowers. It is like a fantasti-
cally-decorated void of Hibernian nothingness,
rich with the spoils of time, more diaphanous than
a shadow, more unreal thah a dream, situated on a
chaos of snowy beard in the midst of an ocean of
brogue. Dan dragged Phidias and Praxiteles and
Epaminondas and Raphael and Titian from their
little beds in the quiet of the centuries, and then,
with the calmest insouciance, he piled on top of
their classic and mediaeval frames the carcases of
Michael Angelo and Leonardo da Yinci and
Rubens, and laid the whole osseous trophy at the
artistic feet of President Ashton.

down.

“An attempt is being made by Messrs.
Devitt and Moore, at the instigation of Lord
Brassey, to make the mercantile marine popular
among the better classes. Two ships belonging
to the firm have just sailed from the Thames for
Melbourne, carrying between them 48 midship-
men, or pupils, for whom are provided instructors
and special accommodation. The fees are gradu-
ated, £7O for the voyage out and home, which
is the hightest amount, being paid by greenhorns,
and a less sum charged to those with some ex-
perience.”—Ex.
Mr. John Hickson, of Cambridge (Tas.),
hung himself in a temporary way a few days ago
because ho wanted to find out how long a man
could hang without losing his breath. He meant
to climb down again when his thirst for know-
ledge was satisfied, but something went wrong
with the arrangements, and he stayed where he
was. When he was found, two hours later, he
was still up in the air and investigating the point.
Verdict: Died through looking for unnecessary
information.
Dear Bulletin, —A costly little game,
which sadly needs exposure, is being carried on
by certain sharp Sydney attorneys. When a
Sydney merchant has a number of small out-
standing accounts due to him —under £lo—he
employs an attorney to x-ecover, with the stipula-
tion that all costs are to be paid by the debtor.
The attorney, knowing that no solicitor's fee is
allowed either in the Petty Sessions or District
Court for cases under £lO, issues a Supreme Court
writ, which document exhorts the defendant to pay
the debt —say £'S~ and £llls. fid. for costs (besides
mileage) within eight days, otherwise judgment,
with more costs. Very often the defendant is
frightened and pays up the debt and costs. But
unless he is ignorant and foolish he will do no
such thing. Let him enter appearance, which he
can do personally, and then await developments.
The sharp attorney will probably not proceed
further, knowing that £2 will bave to be paid into
court to set down the case for trial before a jury,
and knowing also that, even if successful, he
cannot get costs, unless the Judge certifies that
there was reasonable cause for bringing the case
in the Supreme Court, which his Honor will cer-
tainly not do. If, then, the plaintiff’s attorney
does not file his declaration within the time
limited by the Court Rules, the defendant comes
with a rush and the fun begins. He had better
now employ an attorney, with the stipulation
that all costs are to come out of the sueing practi-
tioner. His attorney will at once sign judgment
against the plaintiff of now pros, and tax his bill
of costs, which will come to over £5: and as
plaintiff’s attorney cannot allow his client to be
levied on for the amount, he will promptly pay
the costs out of his own pocket. The above course
was lately adopted by a defendant with much
success, and as no joy can equal that of making a
very “smart” attorney pay his own client’s costs,
we advise all defendants similarly situated to
follow the blessed example above quoted.—Yours,
Through the Mill.

Viewed through the medium of anticipa-
tions raised by previous Shakesperian revivals at
Her Majesty’s, “ Macbeth ” cannot be pronounced
an unqualified success. Unadorned by the spec-
tacular grandeur that fitly surrounded “Julius
Osesar,” some of its supernaturalisms—admirably
suited to the pantomimicry of “A Midsummer
Night’s Dream ” are out of place in this sternly
tragic story. Stage realism up to a certain point
is necessary for dramatic effect; that overstepped,
it destroys the suggestiveness that permeates the
truly artistic in all things. Eor instance, the
ludicrous bobbing-up of Banquo’s head in the
banquet-scene simply kills the impressiveness of
Macbeth’s distracted utterances. If the visions
apparent only to a fevered brain are to be thus
materialised, a dagger might fitly be dangled from
the flies and plucked upward when the moody
Thane says “Oome, let me clutch thee.” The
audience should be driven by the intensity of the
actor’s art to see through his eyes, to feel the
verity of all that he professes to see. Lacking
that intensity, the appearance of fifty gory heads
will only excite ridicule. So far, the Macbeth of
George Rignold has, for the most part, felt his
way through his lines and acted like one whose
mind was pre-occupied. Cognisant of the fact,
and combating it, he at intervals electrifies his
audience with a stirring reading, and will in time
doubtless get rid of this heaviness ; nevertheless, it
is unlikely that “Macbeth” will ever be deemed
one of his strong parts. The Lady Macbeth
of Janet Achurch—somewhat “dolly ” in make-
up—is. an olla podrida, a dish of various in-
gredients in which a satisfying and full-flavoured
morsel is only occasionally encountered. In fact,
she seems to have thoroughly studied only two or
three speeches, and, therefore, her performance
suggests a dramatic recital, not a coherent imper-
sonation. Charles Charrington’s finnikin utter-
ance is not suitable for the delivery of blank verse,
and he has not half the physical and vocal force
necessary for the impersonation of Macduff. In
the combat he should have died a thousand deaths,
and, as a gentleman remarked from the gallery,
the fight was too evidently “ a put-up job.”
The much-cut-down Malcolm of Herbert Flem-
ming is a pink and pretty creature for whose mis-
fortunes no one in front evinces much sympathy,
and stage-manager Hazlitt, careful and intelligent
though he be, is not particularly well-placed as
Banquo. In Harry Jewett’s hands Duncan’s
elocution is admirable, but the monarch has no
“ proper pride,” and is not half so haughty as a
suburban mayor. Governor and Lady Carington,
accompanied by the Countess of Hopetoun, will
attend on Saturday evening.

None of the Sydney papers have expressed
a straight-forward opinion as to the respective
merits of the two Boccaccios. Our vote has been
secured by Nellie Stewart’s, which is as exhilara-
ting as sparkling wine. But tastes differ : years
ago a satisfying, full-bodied beverage named
Marian's Burton Ale was Very popular in Aus-
tralia. It was too heavy for our palate, but, Lord
bless you, lots of people swore by it. Hnazelle’s
Lambertuccio has been broadened and developed
since first night, until, in the second act at all
events, it is a very good low comedy performance.
By the way, what a Baillie Nicol Jarvie little
Johnny Gourlay would make. He and the
kittenish Kelton are prime favourites with the
populace. Bessie Major is getting lots of fun out
of Beronella in the scenes with Knight Aston as
Leonetto. On Monday last “ our Nellie ” was
hoarse as a raven, but the dashing Burton was
rusticating, and so she had to scrape through.
Houses are not up to the “ Paul Jones ” average.

With a change of bill, the engagement of
Tracy and Adson—two clever song-and-dance
men—and Daisy Blair, Harry Rickards again
finds Sydney School of Arts too small at times for
his audiences. Harry has several new songs, in-
cluding Godfrey’s clever sketch, “The Road to
Ruin,” and “Break It Gently,” a truly comic
song; and the versatile Fred. Davys and Georgie
Devoe have also worked up some new specialities.
The opening item now is “ In a Fog.”
There’s heaps of room in Sydney Center
nial Hall for the afternoon Orchestral Concert
audiences, when, contrary to the usual practice,
folks get as far from the platform as possible.
The novelty last Saturday was Grieg’s “Peer
Gynt,” of which the last movement—a martial,
barbaric, and weird strain—was encored and re-
peated. The Nofcturno and Scherzo of Mendels-
sohn’s music to “Midsummer Nights Dream,
familiarised in Sydney by the late Rignoldian
production, between which and Raff’s “Dance of
the Dryads” played later, there is a family resem-
blance, were highly appreciated. The exquisite
“ Berenice” was repeated by special request, and
the Scherzo of Sylvia was encored as usual.

The Australian Society of Marine engineers,
who really command the-situation as regards the
strike, refuse to come out, preferring rather to put
on the screw for themselves. They are mostly
canny bodies frae Scoatland, who fight purely for
“ their ain hand,” and have at their credit funds
to the extent of eight thoosan’ pun’.
“ School,” that attractive, delusive narra-
tive of love in a ladies’ seminary, started its first
Melbourne term on Saturday by filling the Bijou
with more money (stated manager Mayne) than
the theatre, now or old, had ever before netted at
one haul. Robertson’s comedy, built as it is upon
a, foundation of fresh, giddy girlhood, and em-
bodying the possible result of two Romeos being
permitted to prowl at large amongst a shrubbery
full of sentimental virgins, and waft kisses over
the garden wall, appeals to the tenderest emotions
of either sex. (lazing upon this picture of vestal
frolic the audience must fain let their imaginations
run riot. When Mrs. Sutcliffe orders her mob of
spring chickens off to roost, one relapses
into a languorous dream of golden tresses,
rumpled pillows, hairpins, and curl- papers.
The thought of those girls’ dormitories, whose
chaste darkness is filled with the sighs of virgin
innocence and the murmurs of muffled giggles
gently stealing from underneath sheets scarce
whiter than the forms they enfold —the thought
of all this causes old men to break out into a
warm glow, and the eyes of stern matrons to
sparkle anew with’ the light of other days. As for
the young men and women present, the effect
upon their feelings is beyond the power of mortal
pen to tell. Although much of the moralising
and repartee of the first act is out of date.
“School” has an enduring charm, inasmuch as
love can never grow old, and the Sydney success
of B. and B.’s production is likely to be equalled
in Melbourne.

“Alone in London” has been going so
well at the Alexandra that it may last over another
week. The violence of Albert Norman’s sneer and
the comparative maturity of Nan’s seven-year-old
offspring are the only blots upon an otherwise first-
class rendering of a good drama. Charley and his
faithful Liz deserve the special word of praise
they didn’t receive last w r eek, while the accuracy
with which the venerable Jenkinson slips down-
stairs is more remarkable than ever, and covers
up such a multitude of sins that the old villain s
popularity with the gallery-bcys can be under-
stood, if not excused. In ability to break all the
Commandments at once, without losing the love of
his fellow creatures, Bland Holt stands alone on
one leg, like a pelican in the wilderness.
* * *
“Fun on the Bristol” is alleged to be still
booming at the Opera House, but the show has a
worn and jaded appearance altogether. Matters
progress much as usual at the Gaiety and Victoria
Halls, and the St. George’s Palace of Wonders,
which opened on Saturday, seems to he running
on the same line as wonders usually run. Captain
Jorgensen expounds the mysteries of his Storm
King lifeboat to enquiring minds at the Wax-
works, and the Cyclorama continues to drive a
steady trade in Waterloo, where old Teremiah
Brown is still on tap.
** * *
Mrs. Potter and Bellew have come to their
positive last appearances at Melbourne Princess’s,
and a non-productive season is dying hard.
Monday night saw the finish of “Hero and
Leander ” which was to be withdrawn—more’s
the pity I—that Mrs. Potter might take her leave
in “Camille,” the part best suited to her strange
mannerisms. Then the slender enthusiast will
paok-up Worth’s millinery and journey to
Adelaide, or somewhere, taking Bellew with her.
On Saturday the old comic opera crowd, recruited
by baritone Ryley, come back to the scene of their
former triumphs, &e. The ecldt attaching to a
new opera will be badly wanting, however. They
start with “ Dorothy,” to be followed by other
chestnuts previous to the production of “The
Gondoliers.”
** * *
The exHiusband of a certain smart little
actress states that the late Phil. Beck, “being
annoyed at something that had taken place,”
admitted to the husband that he had been too
giddy with the lady aforesaid. Phil. Beck was
ever a worm of a Lothario. In London—before
coming to Australia the first time—he had been
theatrical guide, philosopher, friend and leading
actor to a deluded manageress who dropped
countless thousands by her venture. When all
her capital was exhausted Phil, packed up his
portmanteau and left his patroness to pine alone.
The fellow had no conscience, and no anything to
recommend him beyond a faculty for photograph-
ing words upon a diseased brain, and two ugly
furrows in his fat cheeks which passed for dimples
behind the footlights.

that both actors and actresses used to he fearfully in-
temperate in the last generation, but were sobered up
now. Said the parson, with a sigh, “The same with us!”

Just take the programmes of Melbourne Theatre
Royal, for some months past, and reflect that this classic
ground was that of G. Y. Brooke and Barry Sullivan.
“ Formosa,” “ Flying Scud,” “ Lucky Lot,” and that
genus 1 Civilisation is built up but to be demolished.
Matilda Heron, a little American actress with almost
the genius of a Rachel, used never to be happy unless
getting divorced, but at last she fell into the clutches of a
big Irish husband, a. kind of Sullivan. One day be held
her, at arm’s length, out of a three-story window of an
hotel until she shrieked out, “ I apologise The audience
in the street declared that this was truly a dramatic
sensation.

like much-travelled Madeira.

F. B. Ohatterton once wired to Mrs. Soott-Siddons to
know her lowest terms per week for Drury Lane. The
answer came “ £3O a week,” to which Chatterton replied,
“ Make it shillings, and consider the matter settled.”
Mr. John K. Rogers wired to an American manager to
know on what terms he would book Minnie Palmer for one
week. The manager, knowing the combination, wired,
“Can book M.P., I to take 99 per cent, of the gross
receipts.”

During the “ Otello” season in New York an autograph
huntress persuaded the primo tenore to contribute some-
thing to her album, and with customary modesty he
wrote: “I am the greatest tenor in the world.—
Tamagno.” Soon after this a rival having had a similar
request made to him, opened the book, and reading
Taraagno’s self-satisfied lines he finished them off by add-
ing, “Me, too. — Campanini.”
Quarterly Review : On the Parisian stage, nowadays,
the aisthetic questions seem to be what the men wear, and
what the women don’t.
Sarah Bernhardt may reach Australia little impaired.
She has filled out nearly twice the size she was ten years
ago, and is no longer a living skeleton. Her luscious
golden voice is gone a bit hoarse, like Mrs. Potter’s, but
the Australian voyage may bring it out again, like G. V.
Brooke’s magnificent organ.
There are three African pigmies on view in Adelaide.
The tallest measures about four feet.
Florence St. John states that she brought her weight
down from 13st. Mbs. to 10st. 41bs. by going without
drinking, either with meals or at any other time in the
day, “only just a little sip to allay the dreadful thirst,
and walking a great deal. It requires more self-denial to
do that than almost any other method of reduction, but
it’s a sure thing.”
Miss Duggan, of Melbourne, is thought to be the coming
Essie Jenyns. After three years’ study under Mrs. G. B.
W. Lewis, she debuts at the Theatre Royal as Juliet, her
brother having taken the theatre for that night. He is
an amateur of some genius, leader of the Roseians Club,
with which bis performances of Hamlet, Macbeth, and
Shylock have been excellent. Miss Duggan is being
boomed by Dr. Neild, and other good judges, which looks
as if there is something in her.
The Paris theatres are importing ballet-girls from Lon-
don in large numbers. British leg is rising in the market
every day.
Mrs. Digby Willoughby, who acted and quarrelled with
St. Maur in these parts, is spoken of as a new tenant for
the London Opera Comique.
The true value of daily newspaper notices w r as clumsily
exposed by a paragraph in the Age of September 1,
announcing that St. George’s Hall, Melbourne, re-opened
on the previous Saturday, and “the programme was
received with every mark of pleasure and gratification by
the large audiences which were present.” As a matter of
fact the show didn’t start until September 6, and the
'• large audiences ” consisted of one solitary liar compiling
his monotonous slaver in the Age reporting-room.

Miln, the tragedian, has made a decided “ rise,” paid off
his creditors, and given £lOO to the Ballarat Shakespeare
Statue. He cronied greatly with Robson, the Ballarat
Admirable Crichton. Robson is tragedian, comedian,
musician, bank business manager, and goodness knows
what. He can read Macbeth nearly as well as Miln, sing
all the music if need be, and play all the orchestra
between the acts.
