An English actress travelling in Australia with a
manager, noted for the munificence of his salary-list, at a
stipend of three guineas weekly, complains to us that she
was turned loose friendless and penniless on this mighty
continent, and has been actually compelled to take her
passage home on credit. It is evident that the profession
can’t be too careful about agreements. Passage out and
back is the usual thing.

Fortune Hunting.
It is a difficult thing in these days of speculation
and peculation, of combinations and trusts, for a
man to acquire a fortune in any of the usual avoca
tion, if he follows the lines of strict integrity. But
it can be done. What has been done can be.
There are men now living enjoying large fortunes
which were acquired honestly—strictly so—they
enjoy the results of sagacity, tenacity, and hard
work. The time has passed when men can hope
to play at business and get on ; competition is too
great. Fortunes are sometimes acquired by chance,
they are usually lost the same way. Experience
teaches that the old saying is a true one, “Easy
comes, easy goes.” The fortunes that are held
and increased are almost always the result of close
application to business. The millionaires are,
as a class, the hardest-worked men in the
country. One of the most important ele-
ments of success in almost any walk of life
in this country is good health. It requires a
robust grappling with the chances to wrest success
from the thousand competitors for the same prize
—nine hundred and ninety-nine of whom must
fail. This is why the country lads so frequently
outstrip their city-bred competitors in the race for
commercial success, though the natural chances
would seem to be strongly in favour of the latter.
The man who is able to attend to his business
every d-ay in the year necessarily has an advantage
over the man who has to lay off a week or so
every now and then because of ill-health. It can-
not be too strongly impressed on our minds that
ill-health is most generally the result of neglect.
Health is our normal condition. If the main
purifying organs, the liver and kidneys, are kept
in healthy action, or if deranged, are put in such
condition, the danger of being stricken down
with sudden disease is very slight. Good health
is a prize not duly appreciated until lost. Ill-
health is a great misfortune—you cannot afford to
take chances on it. Keep your system right.
“ It is a fact that many of the best proprietary
medicines of the day,” said the late Dr. .1. G.
Holland, in Scribner’s Magazine, “ are more
successful than many physicians, and most of
them were first discovered or used in actual
medical practice. When, however, any shrewd
person, knowing their virtue and foreseeing their
popularity, secures and advertises them, in the
opinion of the bigoted all virtue went out of
them.” Failure of eyesight, fickle appetite, head-
ache, extreme wakefulness, frequent desire to
urinate, especially at night, gradual failure of
strength and dropsical swelling—these are symp-
toms of kidney disease. If you neglect the symp-
toms you will eventually have Bright’s Disease.
Warner’s safe cure is the only specific which has
ever been discovered for this disease. The late
Dr. Dio Lewis, over his own signature, said : “If
I found myself the victim of a serious kidney
trouble I would use Warner’s safe cure.”

The Stolen Colours.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
An exclusively male society, such as ours was on
the Baroma River, is apt to get along very
smoothly for years, provided that all of the indi-
viduals composing it are fairly companionable.
There were five stations on the Baroma, all cattle-
stations. Sheep had been tried on the country,
but without success, and now the only marks of
their former presence were some roofless huts and
the bare patches of ground where once stood sheep-
yards ; for it was before the days of fences and
boundary-riders. Three of the stations were
managed by their owners; the other two belonged
to Sydney and Melbourne firms, and were in the
charge of superintendents, of whom I was one.
Markwell, the other superintendent, was per-
haps the only man who did not quite fit in to our
little coterie. There was no gainsaying his smart-
ness and ability, but he had a bad temper, and an
evil habit of sneering.
Elliot, the owner of Bendalla, the largest station
on the river, was a widower. He had one son,
who lived on the station with him, and two daugh-
ters, who at the time of my story had just arrived
’ on a protracted visit, and created quite an upset
in the hitherto even tenor of our way.
Hayward, who owned Pixie, the next station
to the one I was managing, was a professed
woman-hater, and affected to look on the
interest displayed in the new-comers Avith
lofty scorn. Kelly, who had the station on
the opposite side of the river, called after that
stream, Baroma Plains, fell head over ears in love,
straight away Avith Mary Elliot, the elder girl,
and having successfully persuaded that young lady
that she was in as hopeless a condition, in less
than three months their engagement was a pro-
claimed fact.
Agnes Elliot was two years younger than her
engaged sister, and of a very different character.
Both were handsome girls, but Mary was easy-
going and good-tempered to a fault, while Agnes,
was of a more active and passionate nature. A
tale of wrong that would win from Mary a feAV
compassionate remarks, Avould wring hot indigna-
tion from Agnes.
Jack Elliot, their
brother, had always been
my especial mate on the
river, and naturally on
the arrival of his sisters
I was thrown a good deal
into their society ; nor
was it long before I
learned to love the bright
and eager Agnes with a
love that could never
have been inspired in
me by her placid sister.
I kept my secret, as I
thought, very well, for I
had no more prospect
of marrying on the ex-
tremely limited screw of
a cattle-station super,
than I had of being
made Governor. I might
even have got over the
tit altogether and cured
myself by enforced ab-
sence, but for the rivalry
of Markwell.
He was my senior by
some four or five years,
had travelled more, and
had far more social quali-
ties, when he choose to
exert them, than I had; but no better worldly
position to recommend him, and knowing the
real nature of the man I felt that to suffer him
to win such a girl as Agnes Elliot would be a
downright wrong to her.
That, you may say, is always the way in which
the jealous man argues. Men do not love women
so deeply as they hate their rivals in the affections
of those women. Marriage is more often the re-
sult of a man’s determination to avoid injury to
his own armour-propre than of anything else.
Kelly and Mary Elliot were duly married,
Baroma Plains rejoiced in a new house and other
embellishments, and I was still uncertain as to
whether the wayward heart of Agnes inclined to
me or not. She had come over to stay with her
sister for a while, and one evening I rode across
the river ostensibly to see Kelly on some station-
business. That was one of the evenings that has
ever dwelt in my memory. Never had Agnes been
so kind to me. Kelly and his bride, with whom I
was first favourite, left us much to ourselves, and
half-a-dozen times an avowal of my love was on
my lips. How different things might have been
had 1 spoken then !
She came on to the verandah to say “ Good
Night!” to me when I left. For a moment I
lingered with her warm hand in mine, and her
bright eyes looking at me from the dusky shadow
of the verandah ; then, with sudden desperation, I
pressed her hands to my lips and, with a broken
“Good Night!” hurried to my horse, mounted,
and rode away. She was still standing in the
doorway as I turned for a last look, and waved
her hand in reply to my salute; and I rode home,
the happiest man on the river.
When I entered my small sitting-room I saw
that, during my absence, the mail had arrived,
for the station-bag was lying on the table. I
opened it without much interest in the contents,
for I was quite absorbed in a dream of Agnes,
but was rather surprised to find among the
ordinary correspondence a letter from my only
brother in England, with whom I had not corres-
ponded for years—not from any quarrel, but sim-
ply through the disinclination to write that grows
on most men in the bush. The letter had been
delayed through going to a former address. The
contents were important, as they notified to me
the death of an uncle, who had left his money to
ray brother and myself and two cousins.
My first thought naturally was that now I was
in a position to propose to Agnes, and I lay awake
all night, determined that not many hours of day-
light should elapse ere I was over at Baroma
Plains,
The next morning the delays seemed never-
ending. Horses had escaped from the paddock,
a black boy got bucked off and broke his collar-
bone, which I had to set, and a messenger came
over from Hayward with a letter requiring a reply.
It was IX o’clock ere I was once more in sight of
the verandah where I had last seen Agnes.
Mrs. Kelly met me with her usual kind smile.
Agnes had gone home nearly an hour ago. J ack
Elliott had come up for her in a hurry, as his
father was starting down to port and wanted
Agnes to go down with him. I hesitated whether
to go on to Bendalla on the chance of overtaking
them, but my presence was absolutely necessary
on the station : I could not leave without going
back first.
“When will they be back ?” I asked, blushing,
as I thought that my fair hostess was
reading me like a book.
“ They will stop until the races. You know we
are all going down, and you must come with us.”
The local races were coming off in about a week
at the little shipping port; and most of us on the
river had entered horses for the occasion. I
thanked Mrs. Kelly for her invitation, which I
gladly accepted, and, without telling my news,
rode home.
Only a week! but it loomed before me like a
year. I could not even write, for we had only a
weekly mail, and I would be down myself by that
time. To add to my annoyance, Markwell, whose
place was at the head of the river, came over that
night, on his way down to port. I managed to be
civil to him, but when I saw him ride away in the
morning to the township, where he would be
within call of Agnes for the whole week during
which I should be tied to the station, I felt in-
clined to try on him the range of my new rifle.
Hayward came to see me next day, and to him I
confided the news of my inheritance. He had
been talking of going out west and taking-up
some country for which, at that time, there was a
great rush, and he urged me to accompany him
now that I had the means. He was a shrewd man,
for whom I had a great liking, and I promised to
weigh the matter carefully.
At last the time came when I could fling dull
care aside, and, seated on the boxbes.de Kelly,
relieving him occasionally of the task of tooling
four horses along an abominable road, I felt that
every mile brought me nearer to the consumma-
tion of my hopes. Arrived in the little port which
in those days had not assumed its present dimen-
sions or importance, my first thought, after I had
made myself presentable, was naturally to call
upon Mr. Elliot. The family, including Kelly and
his wife, were located in the house of a friend, a
widower, with one little girl, whose habitation
was far too big for him. Agnes was out —gone

She refused to give him
money, so he took the revolver
and said, “You don’t know
what I will do!!”
Frightened through
this remark, and grieved
about her own heartless -
ness, she went to the
police-station to prevent
something terrible.
He went to the
pawnshop.
He was found late at night
by a constable who brought
him home to his loving wife.
out for a ride with her brother and Markwell.
This was a check at the start.
While chatting with Elliot the party returned,
and, with a very sore feeling in my heart, the
meeting I had been so longing for came off.
How bright and handsome she looked in her
close-fitting riding-habit as she came along the
verandah and greeted me ! Every jealous thought
in my mind would have been exorcised but for
the presence of Markwell, who gs,ve me a super-
cilious nod and looked on with an air of insolent
proprietorship—at least I thought so—while the
ordinary commonplace words were exchanged.
She noticed instinctively the change in my manner
and her pride took fire. Ah ! these wretched con-
ceits of self, which do work we can never undo !
I stayed to dinner with the Elliots, but had no
opportunity to speak to Agnes until some time
afterwards, when by chance I saw her sitting
apart from the others in the drawing-room with
the little girl, the daughter of their host, at her
knee. She was stitching industriously at some
ribbon and I lost not a minute in reaching her
side.
“ What is the work, Miss Elliot ?” I said rather
awkwardly to break the ice.
“My colours for to-morrow’s races,” she re-
turned.
“ And who is to wear them?” I asked, bending
down and speaking earnestly.
“That depends,” she answered, glancing up at
me with a smile that put every sore feeling to
flight, for I knew they were meant for me.
“So we have to congratulate you, Murray,”
said the hateful voice of Markwell close behind.
I turned sharply round, and Agnes flushed
quickly, for his words seemed to have a double
meaning.
“ What do you mean?” I asked, rather rudely.
“ Why, they say you have come in to a pot of
money. Is it true ?”
(|So some gabbling idiot from the station had
brought the yarn down and now it was blurted out
like this when I had meant to tell Agnes just when
I asked her to be my wife! I replied very shortly
that an uncle of mine was dead from whom I in-
herited some money, but my chance was spoilt,
and if it had been my rival’s object to break up
our chat, lie succeeded admirably. I guessed that
Agnes was hurt that I had not told her brother or
father, who had so long been my friends, of my
good fortune, not divining my reason for keeping
silence.
Next morning the whole township was on the
racecourse. I rode out the two miles with Jack
Elliot, for Kelly had his trap full. I wondered
whether Agnes had entrusted the knot of ribbons
to her brother, but apparently he was charged
with no such mission, so I took the earliest oppor-
tunity of presenting myself before the party as
soon as I saw them arrive. I had promised to
steer a horse of Kelly’s in the Hurdle Race, for, as
a newly-married man, all such pleasures had been
sternly denied him, and Markwell, who was a
sporting man, had entered a horse of his own.
It was in vain that I lingered around the buggy.
Agnes several times seemed on the point of speak-
ing to me confidentially, but something always
interfered, and I had at last to go away unsatisfied
as the time for our race was approaching.
I had donned my jacket, or rather Kelly’s, when
the man himself came into the tent.
“Now, then, Davie,” he said, “ just one word.
I want to win this race, and you can do it if you
race Markwell right away from the, jump. The
others have no show; his horse has more foot than
Beeswax (Kelly’s horse), but he can’t fly his junjps
like old Bee can, and if you make it hot from the
start, he’ll either come to grief or drive you home.”
' I signified my comprehension, and was leaving
the tent when he added : “By the way, Mary gave
me a message for you from Agnes—something
about having lost something; however, she can
tell you herself after you’ve won the race;” and so
we sallied forth, I much puzzled at the meaning of
the half-forgotten message.
Markwell was already mounted and had taken
his horse over a hurdle, and was walking him
back when I passed him, and there, on the breast
of his jacket, was the identical knot of black-and-
orange ribbons I had seen Agnes putting together
the night before.
The sight roused every jealous passion within
me ; I knew they must be the same, or else why
had I not received them according to the half
promise made ?
“ I’ll win this race or break Markwell’s neck,” I
muttered to Kelly as he gave me a leg into the
saddle. He squeezed my hand, and the next
moment Beeswax was over the hurdle like a
swallow.
There were six of us in the race, but Markwell’s
horse was the only one I dreaded. We had a
good start although Beeswax, excited by his canter,
was dancing to get off. I took ttie lead at once at
a pace that few hurdle races are ever ridden, and
for half a mile kept it, without seeing a horse,
Beeswax taking his jumps just as a matter of
course. At the end of the half-mile I heard a rap
and a clatter, and glancing round found Markwell
just behind me.
His horse had twice the pace of Beeswax and he
had bustled him over the jumps so far without
coming to grief. On the flat he raced up to me
without an effort and I knew that my only chance
was to rush him at the hurdles. Twice his horse
blundered dreadfully, and I was well ahead when
we were two jumps from home. If he got over
those two jumps safely I was beaten for he would
catch me on the straight run in.
I knew Markwell’s horse was as excitable as he
could be, so I pulled Beeswax a little and let him
come up and get about half a length ahead, then
I put Beeswax at the next jump “alll kneW,”and
he seemed to take the hurdle in his stride. Not so
my rival’s mount; maddened with the rush of
the other horse alongside of him, he scarcely
attempted to jump and went down headlong, but
fortunately for his rider, sending him rolling
ahead.
The others were all out of it, so I pulled Bees-
wax in ; put him quietly over the last hurdle and
cantered past the winning-post.
“ He’s all right,” said Kelly, as he came and
took my bridle to lead me in to be weighed, “ but
he got a devil of a purler.”
Strange to say, Markwell had escaped almost
without a bruise. He had been flung forward, a
little to one side, just as the horse was in the act
of turning over, and had rolled out of harm’s way.
The saddle was smashed, and the horse could never
be got to face a jump again, but I was really glad
that no worse accident had happened.
He came up pluckily, in spite of his shaking,
and forced a jesting remark about the pace we
had ridden. I changed my jacket, and after the
usual noisy congratulations, found my way up to
Mrs. Kelly and Agnes.
“Your colours were unlucky, Miss Elliot,” I
said, regretting the words the next moment.
“My colours, Mr. Murray! I did not know
they were in the race.”
“Mr. Markwell wore them, at any rate,” I
answered, rather surprised.
“You are joking,” she replied; “and I don’t
think it in good taste.”
I f I drew back, snubbed and offended. Mrs. Kelly
put her hand on my shoulder as I stood beside the
buggy, and, stooping down, whispered to me,
quite energetically for her,
“ Davie, don’t be a fool.”
I guessed her meaning, and if I could have made
my peace then, I would ; but I was not afforded
the opportunity. Agnes kept me at a distance for
the rest of the day.
There was a race-ball that night, and I fondly
hoped that I should have a chance of an explana-
tion. Markwell, in spite of the heavy fall he had
experienced, was there, and exerting all the agree-
able qualities he possessed to the utmost.
Agnes gave me no excuse for trying to get a
moment’s private conversation, and in the course
of the evening I was lounging in a sulky fit on the
verandah, when I saw her and Markwell in deep
conversation in a secluded part of it. She was
speaking earnestly, although I could not see the
expression of her face. I had seen enough, how-
ever, for my then temper; apparently she had
afforded him the benefit of the confidential interview
that she had avoided giving me. I left the ball,
and a month afterwards Hayward and I were out
west. I stayed out there two years, I heard from
Kelly and J ack Elliot, but the name of Agnes
seemed carefully tabooed in their letters.
One evening I received a visit from an old
friend who had a station not very far from the
little port where my love-dream had been shat-
tered. He had taken up country beyond Hayward
and me, and was on his road to port when he
called.
“By the way,” he said, “you may remember
Markwell who got such a spill at the races that
time ; but, of course, you do, as you rode against
him.”
I nodded assent, and he went on.
“ I heard he had come out this way to take up
country for his firm.”
“ He didn’t marry Miss Elliot, then ?” I said in
as easy a voice as I could command.
“I should think not,” replied my friend, “she
found him out in a most shabby trick. I’ll tell
you all about it. You remember the ball after
the races ? I was out on the verandah behind a
screen of plants and flags doing a quiet flirtation,
when Miss Elliot and Markwell came on the other
side. We couldn’t well get out and had to sit
there and listen to what they were saying. It
seems he had bribed the little girl at the house
where the Elliots were staying to steal a knot of
ribbon that Miss Elliot had made for somebody
else and he had worn it openly at the races as
though she had given it to him. That young lady
could be very indignant when she lilted, and I
shouldn’t have liked the sarcasm she lashed Mark-
well with.”
I was silent for some time, while Russel smoked
vigorously, recalling the scene.
“ I’m tired of this place,” I suddenly said ; “ I’ll
go down to port with you.”
“That’s famous,” he returned, “I wanted a
mate down.” And he turned in while I com-
menced my preparations.
Half-way across what was known as “ the dry-
stage,” was a shanty, professedly an accommoda-
tion house; but, of course, a sly-grog shop. The
man who kept it met us outside the rude thatched
verandah and said he was glad we had come as
there was a man inside who had “ a touch of the
sun ” and he was afraid he was going to die.
We went in to see the poor fellow, and there, on
the frowsy bunk, lay my old rival, Markwell. The
man said he arrived the day before, on foot, his
horse had fallen with him and got away, and he
had walked on to borrow another to go after him.
He was light-headed and had been so ever since.
Russel and I sat up all night doing what we
could, but it was useless; the poor fellow died
before morning. Once he recognised me and
laughed wildly, “You didn’t get Agnes after all,
Murray,” he said, then he relapsed into mad talk
about racing.
It was a lovely evening when, with my heart in
my mouth, I rode up to Baroma Plains, for I pre-
ferred to see Mrs. Kelly first. She and her hus-
band were standing on the verandah watching me
curiously as I came up. Suddenly Kelly gave a
wild whoop and sprang off the verandah. “It’s
Davie by gum!” he exclaimed, and I was soon
standing between them.
I looked hard at Mrs. Kelly, hoping she would
take pity on me, but I had to ask the question
after all.
“Agnes?”
Kelly pursed up his lips, “Don’t you know?”
he asked.
“Know what?” I demanded, swallowing a big
lump in my throat.
“Why, Agnes was married a year ago—she
lives— let’s see- in Melbourne !”
I suppose I could not smile, nor even look
cheerful, for kind Mrs. Kelly broke in with, “It’s
a shame. Nothing of the sort, Davie. Agnes
Elliot is in there just now; and to tell you the
truth, she must have recognised you before we
did, for she slipped-in without a word when you
were a long way off. Go in and ask her.”
I did not ask her. I only said one word, and
that was her Christian name; and when I took
her in my arms for the first time we forgot all
about mutual explanation until some time after-
wards.
Danger!
Few weeks now-a-days pass by but what the
community is startled by the intelligence that
some prominent citizen has been struck down by
sickness or disease. The constant strain on the
nerves and the cerebral blood-vessels caused by
continual attention to work, thought and business
must tell its tale in the end, the constant worry of
the struggle for existence will break up the most
robust constitution. Dr. Allen McLane, the re-
cognised authority of the United States on cerebral
disease, states that insanity is largely on the
increase through the continual wear and tear, that
he frequently has cases under his care, solely due
to extreme mental fatigue. The practice of taking
alcoholic stimulants to ensure a false energy is
most baleful, and generally results in disease of
the blood-vessels and finally paresis.
With such facts staring you in the face, and the
medical profession powerless to grapple with the
case, nothing can be done but have recourse to a
reliable remedy like Clements Tonic. This article
has proved itself the greatest remedy ever produced
for sufferers from nervous diseases and the effects of
over-work or other debilitating causes. Clements
Tonic is prescribed by the profession, because it
knows that recovery invariably follows its use,
that its uniform composition is the greatest
security for sufferers, and that its pharmaceutical
perfection and therapeutical efficacy can always
be relied on. Mr. P. J. Wallace writer from his
residence, Kangaroo Valley, N.S.W.
Dear Sir, — I have very much pleasure in bear-
ing witness to the great value of Clements Tonic
in cases of Lassitude, Nervousness, and Con-
stitutional Debility generally. For more than
twelve months I was a martyr to extreme lassitude,
loss of appetite, sleeplessness, and their con-
comitant train of physical troubles, so much so
that I feared I should have to abandon my pro-
fession as a journalist, writing being particularly
irksome and laborious to me. I tried several
advertised tonics (?) with little or no good result,
and began to think my case beyond the power of
medicine to cure. However, seeing Clements
Tonic advertised in some of my exchanges, I con-
cluded to try it as a “ dernier resort ,” and lam
pleased to say the result was most satisfactory.
After taking one bottle I found my appetite much
improved; I could sleep more soundly, and felt
refreshed next morning. I continued the med icine,
taking in all six large bottles, and gradually all
my distressing symptoms disappeared. I now feel
myself stronger than ever, and as near perfect
health as a man who has to bear the strain of
constant literary work can expect to be. I can
confidently recommend Clements Tonic to all
persons suffering from loss of nerve force through
sickness or mental overwork, and am most happy
to support my testimony with my signature.
Yours faithfully, P. Jefferson Wallace.
This is only one out of the hundreds of similar
endorsements we are constantly receiving. F. M.
CLEMENTS, Newtown, N.S.W.

Personal Items.
Stanley held his cane under his arm while
he was getting married.
Lord Brassey’s eldest daughter is 25 ;
Hiss Oapell, his intended second wife, is 32.
Hiroch Fink, the Melbourne financier and
head of the astute Fink family, left £24,000.
Jefferson Davis’s only daughter is to be
married to the grandson of a Unionist general.
The brother of one of N.S.W. richest
Legislative Councillors is boss-barber in a Northern
township.
When President Lincoln caught small-pox,
he said, “I have now something to give every
applicant.”
Of the Irish Secretaries since the Union
(3800) four have met violent deaths, Castlereagh,
Peel, Mayo and Cavendish.
Revd. Andrew Gardiner says he is going
to clear himself. We hope so. But he erred
tactically by clearing so early.
Jubilee Juggins Benzon contracted £70,000
worth of debts ere succeeding to his fortune, and
paid them all on coming of age.
The members of one of the oldest and
wealthiest firms in N.S.W. —Dalton Bros.—dis-
solved partnership a few days ago.
Jay Gould is fined on an average £5O a
month for disobeying jury summonses. Business
before pleasure any day with J. G.
Jefferson Davis declared in public, shortly
before the American Civil War, that he would
“drink all the blood shed over secession.”
Bismarck, contemplating political memoirs,
js baulked bv the cast-iron rule which he himself
made to crush his enemy Count von Arnira.
The Bulletin has in the press a book,
“Governors we have known.” The first of the
series will be the governor of Darlinghurst gaol.
Squatter Horn, Kintore’s pal, has utterly
forgotten his democratic principles since he ob-
tained open sesame at Adelaide Government
House.
Dr. Goode, incarcerated in the Debtors’
prison at Darlinghurst for non-payment of libel-
damages, refuses any other “tucker” than that
allowed by the gaol.
Dailey’s youngest son was present at the
unveiling of his father’s memorial tablet in St.
Paul’s Cathedral, but the two elder boys were
down with measles.
The lady whom Sir James Ferguson, once
an Australian Governor (now British Foreign-
IJnder-Seeretary) is to marry, will be his third
wife, and is 18 years younger than he.
G. W. Goyder, Surveyor-General of S.A.'
is author of nearly all the Land Acts on the S.A-
Statute Book. He is known as “ Little Energy,’
and has held his present office since 1851.
They are again going to look for Leich-
hardt. After 40 years of rains and droughts they
might as well look for the tracks of Dampier’s
boots on the rocks of the far North-West.
Sir Walter Barttelott, brother of Major
Barttelott, who died during the Stanley exped i -
tion, and whose memory Stanley has aspersed,
challenges the explorer to mortal combat.
Prominent loyalists will hear with regret
that the Duke of Connaught is now about as bald
as an egg, and all the nostrums in the world fail
to raise a single hair on his sterile cranium.
Mirams, Gourlay and Co., of the Premier
Permanent Building Association, are lying dark
off the weather-bow until October, when Judge
Higinbotham will probably hear the conspiracy
charge.
It seems that the death of the old Duchess
of Cambridge has left her daughter, the Duchess
of Teck, without a house in London to which she
can fro for board and lodging. This is heart-
rending.
There must be a few ladies in Australia
who remember Alfred Dupont, proprietor of the
fashionable riding-school at Brighton (Eng.) He
was recently thrown off his horse and killed on
the spot.
The Earl of Dysart comes into £200,000
worth of New Zealand property when he is thirty-
live. New Zealand is evidently in a fair way to
become another Ireland so far as absenteeism is
concerned.
Dr. Kennedy, who poisoned himself at
Adelaide, the other day, on learning that a
warrant had been issued for his arrest for theft,
did not, it is now certain, commit the offence with
which he was charged.
Mrs. “Brigand” Stanley, in her hoyden
girly-girl days, always said that if she ever
married, it would be to a man who had made a
great name. What would she have thought of the
late Mr. Ned Kelly, of Glenrowan?
Stanley is said to be now very rich. It s
also said that his mother-in-law, long ere the de-
velopment of the serious illness which attacked
him on his wedding-day, announced her intention
of accompanying him on his honeymoon.
In the last generation of the Victorian Bar,
the H’s had all the pull in the chances for Supreme
Court Judgeships; Higinbotham, Holroyd, Hood,
Hodges. Next came the W’s—Williams, Webb,
but the other letters were strangely out of it.
It is remarkable how Chief Justice Higin-
botham mumbles out his judgments in the Vic-
torian Supreme Court, contrasted with the clarion
voice in which he used to speak in Parliament.
He cannot be drawn on the subject of the Strike.
According to all accounts Lord Carnarvon,
who delighted to palm himself off upon sassiety
as a poor man, has died as rich as a Lord Mayor’s
dinner. One of his acts of thrift was the selling
£150,000 worth of land to pay off family
mortgage 8.

same table with him because he was
attached to a, penny paper. He also relates that
George Barrington, ere being transported, picked
his (Sala’s) grandmother’s pocket,

Ihe Librarian of Darlmghurst prison has.
been incarcerated there 18 years, having, when a
lad of 16, been convicted of murder and sentenced
to death, which penalty was commuted to one of
imprisonment for life. He is a native of the
colony and has never seen a tramline, a ship, or the
ocean, and has mastered four languages ! Has he
expiated his crime ? What says Minister Gould ?

An old Australian identity, “La Mo*a- '
dor ,» Countess de Chabrillan, died in a Paris
hospital lately, aged 66. She was a notorious and
beautiful Parisian cocotte 35 years ago, when she
became Countess de Chabrillan, after experience
as a circus-rider, Jardin Mabille cancamst, and
real demirep. Chabrillan brought her to Mel-
bourne when he arrived as the first French
Consul, and for a while she shone in the best local
society.
Rosa, the Melbourne agitator, is a pirati-
cal-looking cuss, about a sixteenth of an inch taller
than Lawyer M‘Kean, the man with the long body
and short mind. He has a big, Punch-looking
nose, with terrific red hanging mustachios, like
the late King of Sardinia, matching his shock of
red hair, and is always grinding his teeth, like
boar-tusks. He wears a long frieze coat, stuck all
over with straw, out of tho» stable where he has
been sleeping. Another account to hand says he
is a very quiet-looking, pale, inoffensive, little
young man.
Mr. Fitzgerald Moore, accused by A. G-
Taylor of attempting to bribe N.S.W. members
of Parliament in connection with one of the
Broken Hill water-supply schemes, came pro-
minently before the public not long since in con-
nection with the Dix-Moore Round-hill share-
trouble at Melbourne Exchange. Moore, who is a
candidate for the Victorian Legislative Council, is
understood to have made money out of silver mine
speculations and has in his time been a lawyer, a
ship’s carpenter, and an engineer. He absolutely
denies Taylor’s allegations and produces strong
circumstantial evidence in support of his denial.
Lord Rothschild, as a memory of Beacons-
field’s exertions for the Jews, charges himself with
all the cost of “pushing on” young Qoningsby
Disraeli, Dizzy’s nephew. This kind of thing
rarely comes off. Gladstone failed to push on his
son W. H., and Russell wasn’t satisfied with his
son Amberley. Codlingsby is said to be clever
and fluent, but no Rothschild made Dizzy. Coddy
will have to make himself—perhaps by securing a
millionaire wife, fifteen years older than himself,
as Dizzy did when he married the widow of
Wyndham Lewis, and wrote “ A man may resolve
to be great, but only a woman can make him so.”
POLITICAL POINTS
A political job lot our legislators
generally.
The Playford Ministry (S.A.j talks about
a stiff beer-tax.
New Zealand, it is plain, will not join in
Australian federation.
The N.Z. railway-employes have decided,
by an overwhelming majority, to strike if called
out.
Mr. Frank Farnell, hitherto a very un-
settled personage, is to be the N.S.W. Government
whip.
During six months ending June 30 last
South Australia exported cereals to the value of
£1,388,000.
There are only two right-out labour mem-
bers in the Victorian Parliament—Trenwith and
Dr. Maloney.
Gillies’ coalition Ministry is a black and
white contrast; Griffith’s is a rainbow ; Playford’s
a kaleidoscope.
AN AMIABLE SUGGESTION.
HE (about to propose): “I HAVE SECURED MY LAND-SELECTION AND AM
NOW QUITE ON MY FEET.”
SEE (pointing to the door): “WELL, NOW THAT YOU ARE ON YOUR FEET,
DON’T YOU THINK YOU HAD BETTER WALK?”
Since the commencement of the strike, the
circulation of one Sydney penny paper has fallen
off 9000 a day.
South Australia has expended nearly
£300,000 in immigration, or nearly £1 per head of
the population!
Gambetta’s concluding words, in his last
speech, were an appeal to maintain the French
alliance with England.
R. S. Haynes, solicitor, brother of Mudgeo
Haynes, M.L.A., is a candidate at the W.A.
elections under the new Constitution.
It is proposed to have two more Ministers
of the Crown in S.A.-—one to be Minister of
Agriculture, and the other without portfolio.
The N.S.W. Ministry pretend that they
are going to pass an electoral registration and
One-Man-One-Vote Bill before Parliament dis-
solves.
Mr. J. L. Dow is once more acting as
Victorian Minister for Lands, previous to being
formally re-installed in the billet and re-elected by
his constituents.
The Gentiles only carried the Salt Lake
elections by disfranchising all polygamists and ex-
polygamists and “all those whose relations with
women were at all open to criticism !”
The N.S.W. National Club “declines to
be responsible for the attitude of the Star during
the strike.” The Labour leaders want to know
why the National Club can’t declare its own atti-
tude.
The term of membership of S.A. Legisla-
tive Council is to be reduced from nine to six
years. By this arrangement some of the old
centenarians will actually have to go before their
constituents before they die.
A Melbourne conservative leader-writer
made an odd slip in his morning journal the other
night. He wrote that the Employers’ Union was
“hateful,” but meant “hopeful,” and had to
apologise in the paper next day.
Failing an immediate revision of the
electoral system, the N‘S W. Parliament will
consist of 140 members. At this rate, the United
Kingdom ought, on the basis of population, to
have 26,800 members in the House of Commons.
St. James’ Gazette says that Australian
Trades-Union officials “enrich themselves by
selling the right to work.” The person who wrote
that story must have been reading about the way
in which people get into a certain Legislative
Council.
People are just beginning to discover that
the much-vaunted Wales Education Act is
neither free, secular, nor compulsory. As to free-
dom : “ This account must be paid by Monday,”
writes a head-teacher on a memo, of fees sent to
the father of poor pupils.
Mr. O’Sullivan, M.L.A., says that the
N.S.W. trades-unionists are not “ socialists, nor
communists, nor levellers.” It is a pity that the
soi-disant representatives of labor in Parliament
should still be so ignorant as to see in the philo-
sophic and honorable term Socialist only a re-
proach.
In New Zealand one single bank, built by
British speculators, owns 410 square miles of
country and has a lien over some 4000 more, most
of which will fall in by-and-bye. A few other
British speculators and syndicates own the rest of
the colony. The advantages of foreign capital
here shine out conspicuously.
The Playford policy is stale as a ripe egg.
In S.A. they kick out a Ministry as soon as it is
twelve months old, and the new Ministry solemnly
asks for two weeks to prepare something, and then
they bring down the very same thing on which
their predecessors were turned out. The Playford
hash is the Cockburn hash, and so on.
Trenwith has come out of his shell a good
deal on the Radical side, and drives the Mel-
bourne Trades Hall Council on, for, in sooth, it
has a Conservative tinge and is almost entirely
formed of employers. Trenwith absolves himself
from trimming, wherefore the other side tries tc
scandalise him about his private affairs.
Melbourne Daily Telegraph tries to de-
fend the tumbling and shuffling of the Gillies’
Coalition over its Railway Scramble Bill. Ever
since Balfour, representative of Much Kirk and
More Mammon, delegate of the Pulpit and Purse,
joined the “ Gillies Reignbow,” the D.T. has held
a brief for the latter’s political salvation.
Advt. from a Glasgow daily :
POUNTY VOTE.—South Ayrshire. A qualification
for this is for sale. Rental, £8 12s. Price, £5O.
Address, 3395, Herald Office.
Faggot-voting is worked for all it is worth in
Australia, too, but the knife is into it already.
Bismarck seems to have dried-up entirely,
the Emperor of Germany hasn’t said a word about
the precious “peace of Europe” for at least a
fortnight, the Czar of Russia doesn’t appear to be
bothered with any “ plots ” worth speaking of, the
usual earthquake in South America has forgotten
to come off, and altogether the cable liar is off-
colour at time of going to press.
Stoinfeld is the most notable Victorian
convert from Freetrade to Protection, unless
Service be held shaky on Murray Smithian
orthodoxy. Steinfeld stands for Parliament as a
bold protectionist, after his trip to Germany,
before which he was indeed the typical rabid free-
trader. He is the most influential man in the
Victorian Chamber of Manufactures. '
At last general election the Sydney wharf-
labourers threw over J. D. Young, one of the
trustees of their society, in favour of Mr. Alfred
Lamb. Now Mr. Lamb prosecutes workmen who
have left his service without due notice, and has
them fined £lO each or gaol in default. Mr.
Lamb is undoubtedly one of the best masters in
Sydney, but when will Labour learn to stick to its
own cloth ?
Labour is solid to-day. But let the Unions
now consider whether they will allow the clergy
to disrupt them, whether they will ever be foolish
enough to tight again about the Pope and King
Billy, whether one workman will ever again be
ready to bite the carotid artery of another work-
man at the instigation of the old-world bigots who
have come to make for us a hell here and show us
the way to a hell hereafter.
The economist, Professor Marshall, says
that the world has outgrown the belief that
slavery is necessary, and is also gradually out-
growing the belief that poverty is necessary—is
gradually dismissing the idea that any large
number of people are doomed from their birth to
hard work in order to provide for others the
requisites of a refined and cultured life from which
they are themselves excluded.
Ex-Attorney-General Hensman considers
the new Constitution of Western Australia, with
its members’ qualification clause and restricted
franchise, a “ piece of narrow, selfish legislation,
unworthy of a council of Chinese mandarins.” He
declines to take part in the elections under such a
“miserable Constitution,” and condemns in un-
measured terms the efforts of the old regime “ to
keep political power from the people and from
new-comers.”
G. D. Carter, M.L.A. (Vic.), is of opinion
that Trenwith, M.L.A., won’t go to Heaven,
because, if he got in there, he would immediately
want to block every non-unionist who sought to
work a harp or wear a halo; also, that he can’t be
admitted to Tophet, lest he calls out the stokers
and puts the fires out, and the half-roasted capi-
talist, and the partially-grilled employer, and the
haricot steamship-owner would be left to get cold
on the hob. Where, in thunder, is Trenwith to go?
The anti-labour proclamations in Mel-
bourne purport to emanate from “ His Ex
cellency the Right Honourable John Adrian
Louis, Earl of Hopetoun, Viscount Aithrie, and
Baron Hope, in the Peerage of Scotland ; Baron
Hopetoun of Hopetoun; and Baron Niddry of
Niddry Castle, in the Peerage of the United
Kingdom ; Knight Grand Cross of the Most Dis-
tinguished Order of St. Michael and St. George;
Governor and Oommander-in-Chief in and over
the Colony of Victoria and its Dependencies,
&c., &0., &e.” Ain’t it sickenin’!

Once upon a time the frogs petitioned
Jupiter to send them a Governor. "Only,’'
said they, " you are to consult us first, you
know ”
“ Right you are,” replied Jupiter , " hov?
would a log suit you ? Stand from under ]”
And he threw it in with a splash.
Moral. — Tis sweet to be consulted,
Also, a log will do as well as anything else.

Evidences accumulate that the proposed
Ganngton-Horsfall marriage is a dead certainty.
. , a BS a Show dinner Governor Oarington
publicly spoke of “his good friend, Mr. Horsfall,
whom he shortly hoped to call his kinsman.”
* * *
In the event of the daughter-begetting
spell, which now clouds the Carington family, re-
maining unbroken, the title may yet be worn by
Brother ” Rupert. The odds are certainly in his
favour, as the second eldest brother, William, (who
married a Miss Juliet Warden, of New York), is
childless, and likely so to remain. In the opinion
of a matrimonial-agency manager, this would be
a decided consideration and probably boom Brother
Rupert to 25 per cent, above his real value.
* * *
The South Australian police have prose-
cuted a man for not vaccinating his child. The
kid s name is Earl Kintore Jones. Do the police
really think a child with such a name would ever
get the small-pox ?
* * *
The Hon. Henry Cholmondeley has been
appointed an extra aide-dfe-camp to the Earl of
Jersey. In the all-important interest of “space ”
this noble youth’s rear name should be written as
pronounced—“Ohumley.” As The Bulletin space
is precious, The Bulletin will adopt its own sug-
gestion unanimously.
* * *
Lady Hopetoun, a little, shrinking violet,
who abhors the paltry pomp and circumstance of
vice-regal command nights,” is evincing a dis-
position to patronise the drama incog., under the
official supervision of a weird, sandy-haired aide-
de-camp, whom a merciful Providence has en-
dowed with gigantic teeth and a sweet horti-
cultural title. Half hidden behind the curtains of
a stage-box, with her face to the performance and
her back to the sassiety foe, the Victorian
Governoress seems to find as much enjoyment as
a mortal can hope for while situated in the vicinity
of the high-born spectre aforementioned, but when
stuck in the centre of the dress-circle, the cynosure
of a hundred pairs of eyes with streaks of Indian
ink round their lower lids, the distress of her
ladyship is sad to see. Obviously, the time
has arrived for theatrical managers to abolish
the old vice-regal cattle-pen, and give the
great ones of this hemisphere a chance to
take their dose of drama in a quiet sort
of way. Which reminds us that Lady Hopetoun ! s
latest unofficial visit to Melbourne Bijou was
remarkable for her persistent scrutiny of Pattie
Brown, an actress who, for reasons we blush to
think about, will be an object of vast interest to
the fair sex for the proverbial nine days ;
Pattie, Pattie, neat and natty,
We like a story spicy,
And that decree appears to be
Quite naughty though it’s nisi.

A certain divorce-case materially increased
the business done by a Melbourne theatre. The
Eiece, a revival, was on for a fortnight. Poor
ouses first week. Rule nisi granted beginning of
second week, and crowded houses thence-forward.
The circle is full of curious Toorak darlings who
have only known the conservatory squeeze, and
couldn’t dream, my dear, of loafing in the Divorce-
court at any time.
* * *
A well-known Sydney masher, who wears
an eyeglass, affects the law as an amusement, and
the stage as a profession, and is in some matters
dreadfully absent-minded, sat down in the Metro-
politan Hotel the other day after lunch and asked
the waiter for a cup of coffee. By-and-bye some-
body came along and held out his hand, and the
masher put into it a half-sovereign. Presently the
waiter came along for the coffee-money. “My
deah fellah ! ’ said the masher, “ I have given you
a half-sovereign, and you haven’t brought the
change.” “ Beg pardon, sir,” said the waiter, “ but
you must have given the half-sovereign to the
man who was collecting for the music outside.”
And then the band played again—but oh, such a
long way off!
* * *
A lady undertaker told a Sydney man the
other day that she found business best just after
sudden changes of the weather, whether from heat
to cold or from cold to heat. Verb sap.
* * *
A Californian married man lately suspected
that he was being supplanted in his wife’s affec-
tions by someone. tie turning suddenly, and
stealing softly into his residence at night, he shot
a hole through the destroyer of his peace. He
afterwards discovered that he had killed a neigh-
bour, whose note he had endorsed for 4000 dollars
and which the injured husband will now have to
pay. Married men cannot be too careful how they
handle fire-arms,
# # #
The lawyers of Melbourne have almost
unanimously resolved to swear themselves in as
special constables. Being themselves the best-
protected and also the meanest trades-union on
earth they consider it their duty to go out and
smash every trades-union except their own. If a
not should arise they will promptly issue forth and
charge the mob six-and-eightpence.
* * *
. „ The swearing-in of 1000 special constables
to preserve law and order” was certainly the
most noticeable feature of the week in connection
with the strike in Sydney. The majority of the
specials” were lawyers’ or bankers’ clerks, a
coliar-and-cuff species of young men who are
chiefly remarkable for the possession of police-
sized feet. The way they rolled up and took the
oath at the bidding of their masters— showed at
heart that they were all plucky young fellows
doncherknow. A small knot of unionists, who
stood on the sidewalk while the “specials ” came
and went, seemed to have summed them up pretty
accurately, for one remarked, “Good ’Havens'!
ancy them special constables to keep law and
order! Why, a smack from a feather-duster would
kill any of ’em.” What The Bulletin wants to
know, however, is, if law and order are outraged i
and the specialshave to come out on active
duty, will Inspector Fosbery allow their mothers

(A SUPPOSED INCIDENT OF THE GAS-FAMINE IN MELBOURNE.)
and sisters to accompany them? There would
surely be general slaughter, and every young man
likes to die in the presence of some of his family.
* # *
A farmer down at Rochester (Vic.) saw
his young wife kissing one of the hands, but
didn t discharge the Lothario, because “ he was
under three months’ engagement, and the harvest
had to be got in.” Apparently the harvest was
got in, for, when the lady gave birth to a baby,
she stated that it would be a lord some day, owing
to the aristocratic descent of the other fellow.
TJp country the womenkind have a sublime faith
in blue blood, and the farmer’s wife evidently ex-
pected her unfortunate offspring to make his deMt
in a coronet and carriage-and-pair.
* * #
Another big building society deficiency,
arising, in the customary way, out of the old
cause, is reported this week. The manager, as
usual, was an irreproachable Christian, and at-
tended church regularly. He was also an unob-
trusive philanthropist, and greatly esteemed in
private life, and his principal mission on earth
was to do good to his fellow creatures. The entire
visible capital of the society is gone, and pretty
nearly everything else, barring the postage-stamps
and the grate, which were left behind through an
oversight. The floor wasn’t embezzled, owing to
the fact that it was nailed down and had the safe
and the counter piled on top of it; but probably
these difficulties would have been overcome if the
operator had had a little more tune at his disposal.
Unhappily, however, he was cut off in the midst
of his exertions, and the cold breath of disclosure
suspended the proceedings just when they were
about to arrive at maturity.
* * *
If any Australian newspaper man gets a
chance to interview the Russian heir-apparent on
his visit to this country it might be interesting to
learn that gentleman’s unbiassed opinion of his
ancestor, the venerated Nicholas, who is still
esteemed in Russia as the last Czar who filled the
bill as a Czar ought to do. Nicholas left a great
history behind him, and one of the brightest spot
in hxs career was his order that a girl in the Im-
perial school for the daughters of officers should
be flogged for writing a letter to her sweetheart.
Also, on second thoughts, he ordered that the
330 girls at the same academy should be flogged
likewise as a warning to them not to do the same
thing at any future time, and the command was
executed with the unobtrusive vigour of that era.
He was a grand old emperor, and of such is the
Kingdom of Heaven.
* * *
A little trouble arising out of the presence
of a Western financial magnate on a neighbour’s
premises at ungodly hours, and the sudden and,
of course, totally-unexpected return of the husband
of that neighbour’s wife, has just been satisfac-
torily adjusted per medium of a leaf from the
Lothario’s cheque-book. Quite a number of
N.S.W. people have lately secured substantial
sums by this increasingly popular process.
Two or three well-meant attempts to “make a
rise ” by such means have, it is true, been bowled
out, yet the plan is one which, as a rule, pays
excellent dividends, and will continue to do so
while poor, misguided man, though “game” to
make an unlawful private assignation, is afraid to
publicly confront Mr. Justice Windeyer in the
Divorce Court.
* # ■ *
The periodical Weeding divorce-case has
been poppingupinMelbourneagain, naughtier than
Visual. This time the aggrieved baker unfolded a
pathetic story of how Dr. Rose and Mrs. Weeding
swap robes de nuit, or rather of how the lady sits
on the edge of the bed simpering in the doctor’s
day-shirt while he ambles round his pansy-blossom
in a long arrangement of frills and tuckers, which
catch in his little tootsies every time he attempts
to pitch the gibbering baker out of the window.
At other times it seems that the guilty pair take a
candle into the stable for the purpose of extinguish-
ing the flame (which doesn’t blaze forever, like the
fire of love) whereupon the still, small voice of
the doctor comes stealing through the darkness
enquiring “Where was Moses when the light
went out?” In a general way, too, they
are singing hymns together, without the slightest
regard for the feelings of Weeding, who presses his
cord, disjointed nose against the parlour-window
and hasn’t heart to join in the chorus of “Room,
room, still room,” because he no sooner gets into
the room than they proceed to lay him low with
coal-scuttles and marble-clocks, and bash his pro-
trate form with a book-case. It grows very tiring.
Just at present the baker has got £lOOO damages
on a kind of judicial approbation, while the doctor
seems to be maintaining possession of the sweet
bone, of contention, and poor Mrs. Rose has got
nothing at all. In the interests of society this
long-drawn-out struggle orer one small lump of
female persuasion ought to be concluded, and then
Rose will be able to renew operations as a lay-
preacher—a line of commerce in which he formerly
shone to advantage, and is eminently adapted to
shine again.

Sunday papers are becoming a Melbourne
institution, and the prohibitive law simply means
that Bailey is not allowed to bring one out.
* * *
The centre of the special-constable agita-
tion is a small gang at Sydney Exchange who are
always well to the front when any opportunity
arises of publicly uniting hystericism and toady-
ing- j V ns V s the crow d which not long since
hounded on the authorities to hang a set of miser-
able boys who had had no trial, and proceeded in a
mob to Government House for the purpose of
telling Gov. Carington almost in as many words
to be sure not to fail in his manifest duty as they
were ready to back up Jack Ketch. And the
worst of it was that Gov. Carington hadn’t enough
sense to see the grossness of the insult involved in
the making of such an assurance to him in his
capacity as holder of not the prerogative of
slaughter, but the prerogative of mercy.
* * *
The idea that the Grenadier Guards have
been sent to the Bermudas to die of fever and
brandy is altogether wrong. The trip is, in fact
a calm, drowsy picnic for the officers thus hastily
banished from their own beloved London. Climate
is delightful in these islands of fruits of gold, and
it was there that the sybaritish Louise of Lome
used to winter when hef downtrodden husband
was freezing in his dull vice-regal court at Canada.

* * *
The strike is a godsend for those Mel-
bourne syndicates who are endeavouring to
establish a trade in brown coal. If this article
ever becomes popularised it will be awkward for
the poets who sing about “ young Rupert’s coal-
black steed ” and “ the maiden’s nut-brown hair.”
they will have to describe the damsel’s wool as
coal-brown.

gave evidence that the godly Dr. Rose used to
take Mrs. Weeding out “bird-nesting.” Which
smacks strongly of Beecher and Tilton.

The N.S.W. Art Society has a hard heart,
and obviously takes for its motto, “ Strictly biz,”
At last annual committee-meeting the name of
Mr. John Smedley, the hospitable friend of the
Society’s youth, was dropped from the list.
Which is the harder to bear —prosperity or adver-
sity ? The Society is now prosperous.
* * *
The N.S.W. Art Society, by the way, has
this year asserted its independence at the cost of
a considerable diminution of the amount usually
spent by the State on the purchase of pictures
from the Society’s exhibition for the N.S.W.
National Gallery.
* * *
A callow member of the Mounted Rifles
(now stationed in Melbourne for the purpose of
suppressing a possible “disturbance”) has been
heard to boast that the Government sent for him
and his mates “to shoot the devils down if they
didn’t behave themselves.”
What is the secret of the length to which
financial-society and other embezzlers are per-
mitted to go in Australia? Simply defective
auditing. The average Australian audit is a de-
lusion, a lie, a sham, a fraud, a farce. The audi-
tor, who gets £2O or £5O for what should perhaps
be two months’ hard work for two first-class men,
just looks in, rubs his hands, says it’s all right’
and then adjourns for whisky-and-soda with the
man who has the hoodie in his pocket.
* * *
The N.S. W. National Gallery trustees
have just acquired for 100 guineas a landscape
shown at the N.S.W. Art Society’s exhibition, by
that rising young artist. Mr. Lister-Lister; but
they have also just awarded to Mr. Piguenit the
£2O prize offered by Mr. Richard Wynne for the
best oil-painting shown at the exhibition aforesaid.
THE LAY OF THE MOUNTED RIFLE.
He is a Mounted Rifle,
And he doesn’t mean to stick
At any kind of trifle
lf the “masses ” dare to kick :
From a station, near the Murray
Up to busy Melbourne town
They have fetched him in a hurry—
•hist to shoot the devils down.
He would put those surly sailors
(Eor the sea h 6 doesn’t plough)
In the care of prison gaolers
If they try to raise a row ;
Or, better still—so chatters
This young gory Tory clown—
It would quickly settle matters
If he shot the devils down.
With his carbine on bis shoulder
And his bullets by his side,
No braver boy or bolder
Ever learned the way to ride ;
He is death upon “ sedition,”
He is loyal to the Crown,
And the height of his ambition
Is to shoot the devils down.
He is always looking daggers
At the “ dirty Melbourne mob ”;
In his simple way he swaggers,
He is fairly on the job;
He has forty rounds of cartridge,
And his suit is yellow-brown,
And as lief as pot a partridge
He would shoot the devils down.
He hasn’t tried to master
The position of affairs :
Sure, the strike is a disaster—
How it came he never cares ;
They fetched him from the station
To the barracks here in town,
Full of eager expectation
That he’ll shoot the devils down.
Poor, foolish Mounted Rifle,
’Tis Croesus seeks your aid
The voice of Truth to stifle—
Eor this are soldiers made;
But not as Labour’s foeman
Can you hope to win renown—
T he Devil take the yeoman
Who would shoot the devils down !

A North Shore (Sydney) farrier has brought
n action for £lOOO against the proprietor of the
North Shore and Manly Times, who disturbs
his rest at an early hour every Friday morning in
“getting out” the paper by the aid of a gas-
entrine. Priority of occupancy ought to decide
such oases. If the farrier came to live next door
to the noise he has no remedy; if the noise came to
live next door to the farrier it should be silenced.
Call the next case.

As Mayor Burdekin has manfully admitted
that he made a mistake in furnishing the Mayoress’
reception apartments at Sydney Town Hall with.
out consulting the Council, little more remains to
be said on the subject, except that the money would
have been more advantageously spent in city im-
provements, We sink up to our ankles in the
mayoral carpets! Yes. So do we in the mayoral
side-walks. However, the allegation made by Mr.
John Lawler that the Town Hall authorities
spent £67 ss. more than they need have done
in furnishing chairs for the Centennial Hall (he
having offered to tender a supply, as per official
sample, at rates representing a saving to the extent
named) is quite another matter and calls for
thorough explanation.

The gentleman who went to Sydney
debtors’ prison with a revolver with the intention
of taking it out of his enemy, is an original genius,
but a thundering bad marksman. Amateur
revolver-shots are deserving of much commisera-
tion. If they try to wing a man they kill him and
are hanged; if they try to kill him they miss him
THE SLEEPING BEAUTY: A STORY WITHOUT WORDS.
and are sent to gaol. With the average man, a
revolver is never a deadly weapon unless he doesn t
know it's loaded.

ayear? # # *

about David, the Hebraic Lighterman, and Abi-

* * *
The N.S.W. Cremation Society has shut
its office, and the committee have had to pay from
their private pockets the amount of the society’s
liabilities, some £6O or £7O. The fact is that an
ounce of practice is worth a ton of theory. Let
the committee get an oven, and baking operations
will soon be in full swing. The “ sight of means
to do the deed” would effectually establish in Aus-
tralia the crematory system. If the society really
means business, it should procure a small plant
and hang out its little shingle—“ Corpses baked
here.”

see” he replied, “we have to roam over many
Oriental women to find the elements of perfection
centred in one of those I see around me, 1 his
cuts more ways than one. Twenty Oriental women
in one London lady !

At Melbourne the other day a Protestant
clergyman, who was attending a sick publican,
met a well-known priest coming out of the latter s
bar-parlour. “Why, father,” quoth the former,
“what are you doing here?” “Whisht!” said

* * *
ASouthern paper reports, with an uncalled-
for air of surprise, that a Sydney photographer
took portraits of seven members of theN.S.W.
Parliament and sent them to an eminent Englisn
physiognomist with the explanation that they
were the last seven murderers hanged in the pro-
vince. The reply was that six of them were evi-
dently irreclaimable ruffians, and the odd one,
though showing possibilities of better things, had
been brutalised by vile associations. This seems
to be coming as near to accuracy as could reason-
ably be expected.
# * *
The N.Z. Freemasons have protested
against the invasion of their country by a foreign
Masonic power, by the recent opening by Sir
Robert Stout of a French lodge. Those who
joined the latter are declared unworthy of recogni-
tion. One thing is to be said about the Grand
Orient Lodge of France—it is really and in truth
and in its effects democratic, upholding to day
those noble principles of human fraternity which
the first and most ancient of all trades-unions was
founded to perpetuate, ; whereas, sad to state,
English Masonry, having come under the wither-
ing control of princes and lords, has lost most of
its worth. The Grand Orient in New Zealand
means that Masonry will have to take its proper
place in assisting those who, in every land, are
bearing the heat and burden of-the day.
* * *
“Our office binds us,” says the priestess
Hero, at Melbourne Princess’s, and strikes a sym-
pathetic chord in a reporter’s heart
Yes, ’tis on “ stated lines ” we move,
The dull “ report” for high aims blinds us,
The paper’s “ policy ” the groove
To which through life our “office” binds us.
Though Thought may long such chains to break,
Each day that dawns for ever finds us
Still doom’d some brainless crowd to “take” —
As mere machines our office binds us.
Among us many soar, indeed,
Beyond the dull routine that grinds us,
But Leisure’s brief to think or read,
For day and night our office binds us.

A literary curio, in connection with the
strike, in Melbourne, is a half-sheet of newspaper.,
Herald matter on one side and Standard matter
on the other. It was “ run off ” when both papers
were being printed in the Age office, the other day.
Editor Winter thinks it’s an omen : “ The
Herald, with which is incorporated the Evening
Standard.” Ha, ha.

At the laying of the foundation-stone of
St. Peter’s Cathedral, Adelaide, on September 27,
200 children are to march in procession with
purses capable of containing £l, emblematic of
the blessed hat upon which all well-conducted
churches depend. Bishopess Kennion is making all
arrangements, and the whole of Adelaide s society
will be there. Kintore lays the stone.

The Referee.
The sporting community will be sorry to hear
that Mick O’Brien has been suffering severely from Tom
Hales’ complaint, asthma, and is unlikely to be seen in
anybody’s colours this season. Not very encouraging for
the backers of Carbine.
* * *
Lord Marcus Beresford has been paying as
much as 3000 guineas apiece for yearlings on behalf of
the Waies-Hirsch racing confederacy. Baron Hirsch pro-
vides the sugar in this costly arrangement, while senior
partner “ Tummy” assists the firm with smiles and other
marks of royal favour. Hirsch has cut out Colonel North
( w ho had previously supplanted an uncanny Scot named
Mackenzie) in the position of financial benefactor to the
Heir Apparent.
# # *
A Melbourne sporting paper is shrieking aloud
because one of its prophets, all of whom tip differently,
was the only local genius “who plumped for Prelude ” to
win the Hawkesbury Guineas. Fancy plumping for a
“ 5 to 2 on ” chance in a field of five !
# * *
The writer has received a letter from one of the
Australian Eleven saying that Ferris will probably have
big inducements held out to him to play for Gloucester.
Grace is very anxious to secure him, and regards the left-
hander as a' wonderfully promising all-round man. The
Australians have not been disunited, as the English papers
hinted repeatedly.
# #
Discovering a soiled- dove on the stand at
Charters Towers (Q.) race meeting lately, one of the gentle-
men who officiated as stewards requested her to “ with-
dror.” The lady of doubtful antecedents promptly burst
into tears and said that the world was a hard place to live
in and didn’t give “a pore gel no chanst.” Then her
companion, male, chipped in and swore roundly that
she was respectable, and that they were engaged to be
married. The pious steward at once interjected that if
they wanted to stay on the stand they must prove their
respectability by getting married in the luncheon-hour,
and that he’d' officiate himself. Both parties were
agreeable, but later, when the ceremony was about to
take place in the weighing-room, the bridegroom didn’t
come to scale, and so the match (also the girl) was off.
* * *
One of the many reasons given for the collapse
of our cricketing cracks in England this season is “ weak-
ness in bowling.” What about the strength of their
flowing howl-ing ?
* * *
“When did Dailey die?” asked one bookmaker
of another, after the hero of the Soudan was buried.
“What!” exclaimed the other, “is Dailey dead?” “Of
course he is.” “ Good shot; I laid him heavy for the
Two-year-old, and a few doubles. Gannon’s luck again !”
Such is fame.
» * *
No less than 30,729 paid Is. to the big match at
Lord’s. Our boys’ share was £750.
* * *
We have been favoured with a “souvenir” of
the Griffo-Murphy sizzle, which contains portraits of
these eminent feather-weight thumpists, also those of
two punchers, apparently heavy-weights, named Belisario
and Ridsdale, unknown as yet to fistic fame, but who are,
apparently, to provide the next man-fight in Sydney.
# * *
Cranbrook, stolen from H. C. White at Havilah,
has been discovered hidden in a cave. It is probable that
he was only “borrowed” for the service of a few mares*
not stolen. The Australian Blair Athol was similarly
treated some years ago. i
* * *
The enthusiastic Sydney amateur who gave a
four-oared boat to be rowed for by club eights, received
many promises of entries, but only two crews came to the
post on Saturday. The Mercantile defeated the Uni-
versity easily, and the latter club deprived Mr. Goodyear,
the E.A. aforesaid, of all chance of recoupment by run-
ning a steamer on their own account.
# * *
There is no telling a trotting-horse from appear-
ances. At Lillie Bridge on Saturday night the angular,
fiddle-headed Burwood trotted well and looked all over a
winner until the last round, when he broke badly, and
Ben Bolt passed him and won.

Queensland, by Newbold out of a well-performed Queens
land mare.

Of course, footballers are professionals, all of
them. Up to Ist September Melbourne Cricket Club took
during the season £2825, football “gate.” £322 went for
expenses, and only £2190 was paid to the competing
clubs. The M.C.C. kept £687, plus £641 extra money to
grandstand. Next door to the big club, East Melbourne
C.C. took £2058 up to Ist September, of which sum £1122
went to the footballers, £936 to the “ground,” and the
E.M.C.C.’s bank overdraft was reduced by some £4OO.

With the football premiership virtually theirs,
the mighty South Melbourne covered themselves with
glory on Saturday, by playing a decidedly “ cronk ” game
against Carlton, the runners-up. Several secret confer-
ences between leading supporters of both teams are
said to have taken place by the sad sea waves on
various evenings before the match. Result —all
the tobacconist bookies rushed by gilded youths
and fair ladies “in the swim,” anxious to take 7 to 4
against Carlton and then to lay 2 to 1 on them. Of course
the Association is to “ investigate,” and, of course, it s
700 to 4 that the charge—levelled chiefly against four
leading South players-will not be sustained. With a
little more dirt in the “national” game, cricket and base-
ball will come to the front. “A consummation, &c.

The travelled Australian boxer, like the Ameri-
can, seems to be more showman than fighter. Jackson
left Australia a modest man—one of deeds, not of words
but on the night of the Griffo-Murphy fight he prefaced
his exhibition spar with a vainglorious prologue worthy
of Bombastes Furioso, and afterwards insisted on
getting rid of an epilogue which was listened to with
marked respect by Dooley, his antagonist,. but wasiso
windy that some irreverent person invited him to snut,
up ” Him, Peter rebuked more in sorrow than in anger,
and then wentcalmlv on. Fancy anyone being so blind to
what is due to greatness as to tell a champion to shut
up !” Then he boxed a final round, and at its finish was
eager to chip in once more, but the spectators were
“ lack,” and so he let them off and majestically retired to
the dressing-room. The newspaper-fighter is a curse, but
the chin-music fighter is even worse. When a pugilist
begins to bore people with his jabber he is on the down
grade. In pleasant contrast was the behaviour of George
Dawson, the light-weight champion, hvho was presented
with a medal for his courage, skill, and civility. He is an
Australian who has not as yet grown too big for bis boots
and, though greeted with a roar of applause, retired
after the referee had spoken in return for him
Then Murphy had the calm cheek to keep several hundred
people waiting for over an hour, and on arrival entered the
rim' without deigning to make an explanation of any sort.
Then he began business as if he had a contract to knock
Griffo out in half a round, rushing at him swinging ternffic
right and left hits at Grilfo’s jaw, but the latter, who is a
a very clever boxer and looks every inch a born pugilist,
used to duck and get out of harm’s way every tune.
Murphy kept this going till he was really well done up,
then made a useless rush, and calmly gave _ this fight to
Mr Griffo.” Murphy was not apparently injured, though
he' said his hands 'were damaged, certainly was not ex-
hausted, and his excuse that the gloves were faked
was an unworthy subterfuge, which has forever shut him
out from the light of the Sydney Gymnastic Club.

son, which he still keeps as' a family heirloom. In the

Mot. ‘

The British Trades-Union Congress, by
263 votes to 93, lias rejected the Socialist pro-
gramme which included legislation on the eight-
hour system, land - nationalisation, the State
control of the railways, the nationalisation of the
mines, and using the union funds for the employ-
ment of members in certain trades. The old and
Conservative trades-unionist imagines that Union-
ism has said and will say the last word for Labour,
but the Socialistic trades-unionist has merely em-
braced Unionism as an expedient rendered neces-
sary by prevailing conditions. All over the
■world Socialism progresses by leaps and bounds ;
it would not have received in the Congress nine
votes nine years ago.
A DIFFICULT CASE.
There is a certain learned Judge who sits in one
of the London High Courts of Justice who says
that patent medicines, or, what he is pleased to
term them, “quack medicines,” should never be
used, except on the advice of a medical man.
The Judge may be correct as to the large majority
of patent medicines; but there are exceptions to
this rule. If our readers will carefully follow the
history of the case below, we feel quite sure that
they will agree with us. The case in question is
that of the young son of Mr. Thomas Buzzard, of
Belton, Uppingham, Rutland, who in January,
1881, was attacked with rheumatism in the feet,
which rapidly extended over the entire body.
The family doctor was called in, who diagnosed
the case “ rheumatic fever,” and attended the
patient until October, 1882, when he gave the case
up as beyond his skill, ordering him to be sent to
the Leicester Infirmary, where, at the end of two
weeks, he was discharged as incurable. From
here he was brought home completely crippled,
and very badly deformed, his feet being drawn up
to his hips, and seemingly firmly fixed. He was
perfectly helpless, and suffered most intense pain.
As a last resource he was placed under the care of
a celebrated physician in Leicester, where he
remained for a long time, but continued to grow
worse. Having now been a helpless cripple for
over three years, and his case being pronounced
incurable by some of the most celebrated medical
men in the Midland counties, his parents were
persuaded by Mrs. Mary Ann Halls, who lives at
Uppingham, to use St. Jacobs oil, Mrs. Halls
relating to them her own case of having been a
cripple for years, and being permanently cured by
the use of the oil. A few bottles were purchased,
with the result that, after applying the contents,
the son was able to get about on crutches. Con-
tinuing the use of the oil, he became per-
fectly cured, and Mr. Buzzard, writing to
the proprietors of St. Jacobs oil under date
of 16th March, 1888, says that his son
now often walks eight miles in a single day,
and that he is perfectly cured. The case
is regarded by himself and wife, as well as his
neighbours as a most miraculous cure. From the
foregoing, as well as from many similar cases
which might be cited, it is clearly shown that the
preparation in question, although under the ban
of a patent medicine, possesses wonderful curative
properties ; and, it being an outward application,
can do no one any harm. While the learned
Judge may advise us not to use patent medicines
“ unless prescribed by a member of the medical
profession, ” there are oases, as the facts above
show, where St. Jacobs oil has accomplished in a
very short period that which the most eminent
medical men have vainly tried to accomplish for
three years, and then signally failed.
EXPERTS AND CRYSTALS.
Have you ever examined critically the crystals
used in the bezels of the Waterbury Watches ? If
not, then this will suggest so doing at your first
opportunity. Each of the glasses used undergoes
a minute and careful scrutiny in the hands of an
expert —an expert whose precision and untiring
fidelitv appear almost marvellous. Crystals are
often rejected whose defects are so slight as to
escape not only the ordinary eye, but even the
vision of foremen and trained employes in other
branches. Yet, upon taking one of them to an
expert, that person will invariably point out the
fault, and do it. withal, so quickly that one would
almost believe the spot to have been marked for
identification. The testing of crystals, rigid as it
is, is but a sample of the method and care pre-
valent in every branch of the Waterbury Watch
Co. 's factory. Precision is the keystone to their
success ; and therein lies the secret of the wonder-
ful results obtained in the timekeeping qualities
of their marvellously reliable watches at a nominal
cost. The people of Great Britain and of the
United States are eminently practical, while those
of the Australasian colonies are nowise behind
them in this particular, and the continued flow of
acknowledgments received from them could not
be wrung forth by misrepresentation, nor sus-
tained by anything else than the most deservedly
| practical worth.
