THE BULLETIN (Published at Sydney, N.S.W)
The Unique Weekly, Australia’s Nonpareil
Illustrated Newspaper.
The Bulletin, the Premier Australian Literarj
Journal, is the only paper which has a con-
Bistent and distinctive Australian National
Policy.
The Bulletin favours—
A Republican Form of Government.
Payment of Members.
One Man, one Vote.
State Revenue derived diiectly from the Land.
Complete Secularisation of State Education.
Reform of the Criminal Code and Prison
System.
A United Australia and Protection against
the World.
The Bulletin denounces —
t Religious Interference in Politics.
Foreign Titles.
The Chinese.
Imperial Federation.
The public eye rejects as uninteresting more
than half of what appears in the newspapers of
the day. It is only the other half that is found
in The Bulletin.
The Bulletin is an aggressive Democratic
paper which strives to exclude from its artisti-
cally-condensed columns all matter which is not
of general human interest. The Bulletin was
started seven years ago with no capital but brains,
and has become a vast property, because it pos-
sessed a vitality lacked by the countless news-
paper-ventures in which Australian capitalists
have unavailingly spent largo fortunes.
The Bulletin’s conductors claim that ever)
progressive paper on the Australian continent has
more or less endeavoured to profit by its example
and follow its lines. The result of its powerful
influence and its unprecedented literary and com-
mercial success can at once be seen by comparing
the methods pursued by the Australian daily and
weekly press prior to and immediately after the
first issue of The Bulletin.
From the date of The Bulletin’s inaugural
publication its success was assured. It had at
once a dozen more or less servile imitators, many
of which even took, so far as was possible, its
name. They are now all dead, while The Bul-
letin continues the one phenomenal success in
Australian journalism, the one completely non-
local and distinctly universal Australasian news-
paper.
The Bulletin proprietary have, during the
last year, spent some thousands of pounds in
fitting their buildings with the latest appliances
for high-class printing and the production of illus-
trations, and they have now issued The Bulletin
in an entirely new form—printed with new type
of improved legibility on the finest paper, and
adorned with sketches from the hands of the
ablest and best-paid newspaper artists Australia
has ever seen.
The Bulletin is the only well-established Aus-
tralian journal which, throughout its career, has
been consistently Radical. It points with pride
to the fact that for years it stood alone among
secular newspapers in advocating the Irish
National Cause, and that it was the only Aus-
tralian journal which denounced the Soudan
Expedition from its inception.
The voice of The Bulletin is the beacon-fire
of national progress: it is the forceful summons to
the crusade against the monopolies in land,
wealth, power, and privilege —the direct cause of
half the misery which now afflicts British
humanity. Against the claims of avaricious
clergy who claim to monopolise salvation, against
the dark despotism of grasping plutocrats, and
against the exercise of any human authority
which has not been granted by the people over
whom it is exercised, The Bulletin will fearlessly
and ruthlessly contend.
The Bulletin, unlike the ordinary weekly
journal, is not loaded with a weight of matter
transferred from the columns of a daily paper
issued from the same office. The Bulletin is
not connected with any other paper, and the
matter it contains is all specially prepared by its
own unequalled staff.
The Bulletin, which is the only paper sold
by runners in the streets of every Australasian
capital, is the most widely-circulated of all papers
published south of the equator, and is the only
Australasian publication which enjoys a popu-
larity unhampered by the political boundaries
that so closely restrict other journals to the
colonies in which they are printed.
The Bulletin’s red cover is equally familiar
to the bushman of the Far North, the stockman
of Central Australia, the pearl-sheller of Torres
Straits, and the digger in the New Zealand
Ranges. A paper which is at once the
most popular city publication and the organ of the
ntelligent bushman must indeed be broadly based.
The circulation of The Bulletin in Sydney is
unrivalled by that of any other weekly journal,
and more than a hundred copies of it are sold in
Melbourne to one of any other paper printed out-
side that city. Perhaps the strongest proof of The
Bulletin’s popularity is found in the unchallenge-
able fact —easily ascertainable from any postal
official —that, of all journals issued in Australasia,
The Bulletin is the one most frequently re-
mailed from one reader to another.
The Bulletin is the journal which, of all Aus-
tralian journals, is most frequently quoted in Eng-
land, America, and the colonies.
The Bulletin is going ahead faster than any
other paper, because its vast mass of original and
specially-written reading matter is the brightest
and best, its illustrations the cleverest and most
artistic, and its policy the boldest and most
straightforward ever submitted to the Australian
public.

“The Conventional Lies
of our Civilisation.”
THE ECONOMIC LIE.
BY MAX MORDAU.
Beneath we reprint a first instalment of the
essay entitled ‘ ‘ The Economic Lie ” from Max
Nordau’s “Conventional Lies of Our Civilisa-
tion.” No writer, living or dead, handles
economic questions with greater freshness, and
few with equal lucidity and force ; at the pre-
sent juncture Nordau’s ideas will strike home
with enhanced vigour :
Those circumstances of our civilisation which
affect the largest number of human beings, with
the most painful and lasting results, are the grie-
vous errors prevailing in the economic world.
There are plenty of people who have never taken
any interest in abstract questions, to whom God is
a matter of as much indifference as primal causes 5
the encyclical as uninteresting as the theory of
evolution, whose faith or knowledge is alike super-
ficial. Many people also are totally indifferent to
the political problems of the day, and the number
is much larger than is usually supposed, who do
not care in the least whether they are governed in
the name of a personal king or of an impersonal
republic, so long as the State remains visible in
the shape of public officials, tax-collectors, and
drill-sergeants. But, on the other hand, there is
not a single man of our civilisation who is not
daily confronted by the question of supply and
demand. The circumstances of the economic
force themselves upon the dullest observation and
the most secluded intelligence. Every human
being possessed of consciousness, experiences cer-
tain wants and grumbles at the difficulty or rebels
at the impossibility of satisfying them. With
bitterness does he see the disproportion between
his labour and the enjoyments he is able to pur-
chase as the results of it, and compare his own
share of the gifts of nature and productions of art
to those enjoyed by others. He grows hungry
every few hours, he is fatigued and weary at the
close of each working day, every time that he sees
a beautiful or brilliant article he longs to possess
it, in obedience to that natural instinct of human
nature to attract notice and admiration to its
personality by ornamental or distinguishing ap-
pendages. Thus he is led by the circumstances of
his physical conditions to reflect upon his relation
to the general movements of political economy,
the production and distribution of wealth. There
is consequently no subject in which the masses are
more vitally interested than this. During the
Middle Ages millions were aroused to action by
the name and cause of Religion. In the latter
part of the last century and up to the middle of
this, the nations of the world were aflame for their
abstract needs of enlightenment and political
liberty. The cry for bread for the masses fills this
latter part of the Nineteenth Century. This cry
is the sole import of that European policy which
tries to turn the people from this engrossing idea
by side issues of all kinds, by persecution of some
social class, by wars, colonisation schemes, exposi-
tions, dynastic comedies, parliamentary twaddle
and civil service reforms; but it is constantly
brought back to it by the pressure of public
opinion which demands a consideration of
the great, world-wide problem of the day,
the question of how to support one’s self.
Crusades for the rescue of a Holy Sepulchre, for
the conquest of a Golden Fleece, are no longer
possible. The causes of modern revolutions are
not constitutions on paper and democratic party
cries, but the longings experienced by so many to
toil less and live better.
* * *
At no period in the world’s history were the
contrasts between rich and poor so decided, so
prominent, as at present. Those writers on
political economy who commence their scientific
works with the axiom that pauperism is as old
as humanity itself, betray either a lack of reflec-
tion or truth. There is an absolute and a relative
poverty. Absolute poverty is that condition in
which a man is partially or totally unable to
satisfy his actual wants—that is, those which are
the result of the organic act of living. Hence it is
that condition in which he finds it impossible to
procure sufficient food, or where to procure it, he
is obliged to curtail the rest and sleep which his
system requires and without which he pines and
dies prematurely. Relative poverty, on the other
hand, signifies a condition of lack of means to
satisfy the wants which man has artificially
acquired, not the indispensable requisites for the
preservation of life and health, but those of which
the individual usually becomes conscious by the
comparison of his manner of living with that of
others. The working-man feels poor when he is
not able to smoke and drink his whisky; the
shop-keeper’s wife, when she can not dress in
silk and till her house with superfluous household
goods ; the professional man, when he cannot
accumulate capital sufficient to free him from the
haunting anxiety in regard to the future of his
children and the support of his declining years.
The poverty is evidently not only relative—the
shop-keeper’s wife appearing rich in the eyes of
the working-man, the professional man consider-
ing as the height of luxury what would seem
shabby to those brought up in the luxury of an
aristocratic home —it is also subjective, as it only
exists in the imagination of the individual in
question and is by no means an objective, appre-
ciable lack of the indispensable conditions of
existence, entailing suffering upon the organism.
In short, it is not physiological poverty, and old
Diogenes proved that this is the boundary line of
the subjective sensation of happiness—viz., that a
man can be well and comfortable so long as his
physical wants can be easily and abundantly
gratified.
* # *
From the point of view of a man of this civilisa-
tion of the Nineteenth Century, who is a slave to
all the customs and wants of civilised life, the
great majority of mankind appear to have been
alwavs relatively poor in the past, growing poorer
and poorer as they are more and more removed
from the present. The clothing was coarser and
less frequently renewed, the dwelling-places were
less comfortable, the food more primitive, the
utensils less in number, there was less money in
circulation and less abundance of unnecessary
articles. But the picture of relative poverty is
not affecting. Only an empty-headed fool could
find anything tragic in the fact that an Esquimau
woman protects herself from the cold by a sack-
shaped garment made out of sealskin instead of
a complicated affair of velvet as expensive as it is
ungraceful. In fact, I doubt whether the senti-
mental wish expressed by that good king, Henry
TY.—that every peasant might have a chicken in
his kettle every Sunday—would have ever touched
or inspired genuine peasants so long as they could
eat their fill of pork. But absolute physiological
poverty, as a permanent condition, never has
appeared except as a consequence of the highly-
developed and unhealthy state of civilisation. It
is actually inconceivable in the natural condition
of mankind and even at a lower stage of social
development. The procuring of sufficient nourish-
ment is the chief and most important act in life of
all organic beings, from the polyp to the elephant,
from the bacteria to the oak-tree. If it fails, it
dies. It never voluntarily submits to the perma-
nent lack of nourishment. This is a biological
law, governing man as well as all other, living
creatures. A primitive man does not accommodate
himself to circumstances of want but struggles to
overcome them. If he is a hunter, and the game
leaves his usual grounds he starts in search of others.
If he is a farmer on unproductive soil, he packs up
and emigrates when he learns of more fertile fields
that he can get. If other men stand between him
and his food, he takes his weapons and kills them,
or is killed by them. Abundance is then the re-
ward of strength and courage. So the tide of
emigration sets from unfruitful districts into those
blessed by the sun; the heroism of a Genseric, of
an Attila, a Ghengis Khan, and a William of
Normandy, has its origin in the stomach, and on
the bloodiest and most glorious battle-fields, which
the poets sing and history loves to dwell upon,
the question of the midday meal was decided by
the iron dice.
* * *
In short, primitive man will not endure
genuine poverty—that is, hunger. He takes
up his arms against the encroaching wretched-
ness at once, and wrests for himself the super-
abundance of the enemy or dies beneath his
hatchet, before he perishes of privation. Absolute
poverty is also incompatible with a civilisation
which has not yet passed beyond the standpoint of
physiocracy. As long as a people are only familiar
with agriculture, cattle-raiding, and domestic in-
dustries, although they may be poor in money and
articles of luxury, yet the necessaries of life are
within thereachof every individual. Only whenman
loses his direct dependence upon food-producing
Mother Earth, only when he forsakes the furrow
in the field and passes beyond the reach of Nature
who offers him bread and fruits, milk and honey,
game and fish, only when he shuts himself up be-
hind the city walls and gives Up his share of forest 1
and stream, procuring his food and drink no longer
from the grand store-house of the animal and
vegetable kingdoms, but by an exchange of
the products of his labour for the gifts of
nature monopolised by others, only at this
period does the possibility arise for a small
minority of persons to accumulate great wealth
and for a large majority of persons to sink into
absolute poverty, physiological distress. A nation
which consists of free tillers of the soil is never
poor. It can only become so by the subjection of
the farmer into a slave, working for another, who
deprives him of the results of his labour on his
land, or else applies his labour in some other way,
so that he can no longer till the land, or else by
the growth or increasing number of cities, absorb-
ing or diminishing the agricultural public. A
highly-developed civilisation thus condemns a
group of individuals increasing daily in numbers
and importance, to absolute poverty. The
cities grow at the expense of the farming
population. It favours the great manu-
facturing industries at the expense of animal
and vegetable production, and produces a
numerous wage-receiving class, whose members
can not call a single inch of ground their own and
live under abnormal conditions of existence, con-
demned to slow starvation the day that their
factory, work-room or dockyard is closed. This
is the point at which all the countries of Western
Europe have arrived, considered to be the
wealthiest and most highly-civilised in the world.
Their population is divided into a small minority,
living in the midst of an aggressive and extreme
luxury, partly attacked by a very frenzy of ex-
travagance, and a great mass, consisting of persons
who can only support life by the hardest
exertions, or who in spite of all their efforts, ■
find it impossible to attain to a normal
human existence. The minority is daily grow-
ing richer, the contrast between its life and
that of the millions is daily growing more decided,
its importance and influence in the community is
hourly increasing. When we are speaking of
the unprecedented, foolish extravagance of certain
millionaires and billionaires ot our days, some
self-conceited, would-be historian is sure to in
terrupt us and quote, with a smile of compassion
for our ignorance, the words of some musty old
writer describing the extravagant goings-on in
Rome under the Empire, or even in the Middle-
Ages. He will maintain that the disproportion
between the very rich and the very poor was in
former ages far greater than at present. But it is
all only a trumped-up, learned fraud. There never
was a fortune in the Middle Ages like the hundred
millions of a Vanderbilt, a Baron Hirsoh, Roths-
child, Krupp, &0., as we know them to-day. In
ancient times such an amount might have been
accumulated by some favourite of a tyrant, or a
satrap or pro-consul, by plundering a country or a
continent; but the wealth thus amassed had no
permanence. It was like the treasures in the
fairy-tale. To-day in his possession, to-morrow
lost. Its owner dreamed a few hours, and
was then awakened by the dagger _of an
assassin, the persecution of his sovereign, or
by the brutal confiscation of his wealth. There
is not a single example of the descendance of such
a fortune from father to son for even three genera-
tions, or the calm and undisturbed enjoyment of
it by the possessor, in the Roman Empire or in
any Oriental state. And in former times, the
number of these millionaires and billionaires was
incomparably smaller than in these days, when, in
England alone, there are from eight hundred to
one thousand millionaires, in Europe altogether—
not counting any other continent —there are at
least a hundred thousand persons with fortunes of
a million and over. On the other hand, never
before were there so many property-less indivi-
duals as at present—men who, according to my
definition above, do not know in the morning
what they can get to eat during the day, nor
where they can sleep at night. The slave in
ancient Rome, the serf in Russia, were completely
without property, as, in fact, they formed part of
the property of their master, but their actual
physical wants were supplied, they had always
food and shelter. During the Middle Ages the
outcasts, gypsies, robbers, strolling players, and
tramps of all kinds were the only persons without
the pale of property holding. They could call
nothing on earth their own, no table was ever set
for them, the ruling authorities even deprived
them theoretically of the right to look upon the
gifts of nature as spread for them. They fought
their way out of the wretchedness in which the
social systems of their day sought to im-
prison them, by begging, robbery, and poach-
ing, and even if the gallows and the wheel
were more frequently the causes of their
death than old age, they had notwithstanding a
full and merry life up to the very steps of the
scaffold. The modern proletariat, or lowest wage-
receiving class, has no precedent in history. It is
the child of our times.
# * #
The modern day-labourer is more wretched than
the slave of ancient times, for he is fed by no
master or anyone else ; and if his position is one
of more liberty than the slave, it is principally the
liberty of dying of hunger. He is by no means as
well off as the outlaw of the Middle Ages, for he
has none of the independence of that gay free-
lance. He seldom rebels against society, and has
neither means nor opportunities to take by
violence or treachery what is denied him by the
existing conditions of life. The rich are thus
richer, the poor poorer, than ever before since the
beginning of history. The same thing is true of
the extravagance of the rich. We are continually
being bored by the anecdotes told by grubbers in
history as to the wonderful banquets spread by
Lucullus. But it remains yet to be proved
that ancient Rome ever saw a feast that
cost 80,000 dollars, like the ball given by
a New York Croesus, of which the newspapers
have been giving us accounts recently. A private
individual who set before his guests dishes made
of nightingales’ tongues, or presented a hundred
thousand sestertia to some Grecian hetera, made
such a stir and commotion in Rome that all the
satirists and chroniclers of those and after days
repeated his name again and again. Nowadays,
no one speaks of the thousands upon thousands
who pay 40;000 dollars for a set of china,
100,000 dollars for a racehorse, or let some adven-
turess spend a hundred thousand pounds for them
within one year. The extravagant luxury of the
ancient world and of the Middle Ages aroused
attention and astonishment by its rarity. Besides,
it had the modesty to limit its display to a com-
paratively small circle. The masses saw nothing
of it.
* # *
Nowadays the insolent parade of the wealthy
is not confined to the ball-rooms and banquet-
ing-halls of their set, but flaunts along the
streets. The places where their aggressive luxury
is most prominently displayed are the promenades
of the large cities, the theatres and concert-
halls, the watering-places and the races. Their
carriages drive along the streets, splashing
mud on the bare-footed, hungry crowd; their
diamonds never seem to sparkle with such
brilliancy as when they are dazzling the eyes
of the poor. Their extravagance loves to have
journalism as a speculator and delights to send
descriptions of its luxury by the columns of the
papers into circles which otherwise would have no
opportunity to observe the life-long carnival of
the rich. By these means an opportunity of com-
parison is given the modern wages-receiver which
was wanting to the poor man of ancient times.
The Lavish squandering of wealth that he witnesses
around him gives him an- exact measure by which
to gauge his own wretchedness, in all its ex
tent and depth, with mathematical precision
But as relative poverty is only an evil
when it is recognised as such by comparison
with others, the millionaires are exceedingly un-
wise to flaunt their luxury in the eyes of the poor,
whose misery is sharpened by the contrast. The
unconcealed spectacle of their existence of idleness
and enjoyment arouses necessarily the discontent
and envy of the labouring classes, and this moral
poison corrodes their minds far more rapidly and
deeply than their material deprivations.
* * *
But these natural deprivations must not be
under-estimated. The great masses of the poor
in civilised countries maintain their bare existence
under conditions worse than those of any animal
in the wilderness. The dwelling-place of the day-
labourer in a large city of the old world is far
more filthy and unhealthy than the den of a beast
of prey in the forest. It is by far less perfectly
protected against the cold than the latter. His
food is barely sufficient to sustain life, and death
from actual starvation is of daily occurrence in the
capitals of the world. The writers on political
economy have invented a phrase to quiet the un-
easy conscience of the rich —the “ iron law of
wages.” According to this law the wages paid
in any locality are at least what is actually
necessary to support life there. In other
words, the labourer is certain of earning sufficient
to satisfy his actual necessities, even if he has no
surplus. This would be very fine if it were only
sustained by facts. If it were true, the rich man
could say to himself, morning and evening, that
everything is arranged for the best in this best of
all possible worlds, and no one would have a right
to disturb his digestion and his nightly rest
by groans and curses. But the misfortune is,
that this famous iron law of wages is only a
Jesuitical play upon words. At the best, it does not
apply to those who can not procure work at all.
And during the time when he has really work to
do, it is impossible for the labouring man in
Western Europe to earn enough so that he can
have anything left over for days when he is out of
work. He is thus reduced to beggary during part
of the year, or to a gradual physical decline from
lack of sufficient nourishment. But the iron wage-
law does not apply even to the amount of daily
wages earned by those actually employed. What
is the minimum of income that will support an
individual ? Evidently it is that which willkeep
his system in a good condition, and allow him to
develope fully and attain to the natural limit of
his life. As soon as he attemps more than his
system is capable of enduring, or gets less food,
warmth and sleep than his system requires to
remain at the summit of its type, then he falls
into physiological distress. Overwork is as equally
the cause of organic decline as insufficient food,
but the latter is synonymous with slow starvation.
# * *
If the iron wage-law were actually what it pre-
tends to be, then the wages-reoeiver would earn
sufficient to bring his organism to and maintain it
in that condition of development to which it is
possible for it to attain, by the natural law of its
being. But experience shows us that the day-
labourer finds this impossible anywhere in Europe.
The optimistic political economist points with
triumph to his iron wage-law, when he sees that
the wages-receiver does not drop dead of hunger
at the close of his day’s work, but fills his stomach
with potatoes, smokes his pipe, drinks his whisky,
and persuades himself that lie is satisfied and
comfortable. But then comes the science of
statistics, and shows us that the average length of
life of the wages-receiving class is a-third, and in
some eases a-half, less than that of the well-to-do
individuals of the same nation, living under the
same conditions of climate and upon the same soil.
What robs the wages-receiver of the years of life
to which, as son of a given race, and the inhabi-
tant of a given country, he is entitled ? Hunger,
wretchedness, want of all kinds, _ these slowly
undermine his health and weaken his constitution.
The wages he receives are also, at best, merely
sufficient to protect him from pressing hunger and
cold ; they do not avert the gradual wasting away
of his whole being, from insufficient food, clothing
and rest. The statistics of the records of disease
and death among the labouring classes of Europe
brand the “ iron law of wages ” as an infamous he.
* * *
The portrait of the economic organisation of
society will not be complete if I omit tc
describe along with the recklessly extravagant
millionaire and the labouring man, condemned
inexorably to disease and an early death, another
class of beings who play in our present conditions
of social life, nearly as melancholy a r6le as the
industrial slaves of the great city. These are
the cultivated men without any regular income,
who have to support themselves by intellectual
labour. The supply exceeds the demand in this
branch of labour, to a frightful degree. The so-
called liberal professions are everywhere so over-
crowded that those who seek in them a livelihood,
trample upon each other until the struggle for
existence assumes in them the gravest and most
hideous phases. Those unfortunates whose efforts
are directed to obtaining a public or private situa-
tion, a position to teach, or success in art,
literature, the law, medicine, civil-engineering,
&c., are capable of appreciating their wretched-
ness in a greater degree, on account of
their higher intellectual development. Their
intimate intercourse with those more pros-
perous keeps the picture of wealth constantly
before them, side by side with that of their own
poverty, which is thus never forgotten. Social
prejudices require them to gain their livelihood in
a way which, without being hygienically prefer-
able, lays far greater burdens upon their shoulders
than those borne by the daydabourer. _ The price
paid for prosperity in their career is constant
humiliation, suppression of their true character
and denial of their own individuality, a yoke mqre
galling to a nature of true nobility than material
want. Owing to the fact that these persons are
capable of suffering more intensely, they bear with
even more impatience than the wages-receiving
class the burdens imposed upon them by the in-
ternal economy of society and property-holding.
Those among them whose efforts have not met
with success are looked down upon by the man of
wealth, who calls them failures, and affects to
despise them. But these “ failures” are the in-
trepid vanguard of the army that is besieging the
proud fortress of society, and that sooner or later
will raze it to the ground.
(To be continued.)


Sir Henry Parkes has been greatly over-
taxing his strength since his return to office.
Should he continue to work in his present style,
ugly things, the surgeons predict, will come of his
inflamed heel.
“Timotheus” the other day very wittily
transposed a well-known proverb by thus com-
menting upon some political situation “ This
may be war, but it certainly is not magnificent.”
The Melbourne Daily Telegraph editor, several
days afterwards, on the same subject, “boned”
bodily the “Timothean” brilliancy, and without
acknowledgment. How do we explain away this,
Editor Fitchett ?
Will some member of the N.S.W. Parlia-
ment ask Parkes whether it is not true that the
Government had made arrangements to land Im-
perial troops from the warships in the event of a
labour emeute? N.S.W. is a country ruled in
the name of the people, and possessing represen-
tative government. Is it true that the Parkes
Government have thus tacitly confessed repre-
sentative government to be a fraud and a failure ?

846 Macquarie-street North, Sydney, September 10,1890.
Mv Dear Moorabinda,—
Already those old stagers who are prinking in anticipa-
tion of the Melbourne Cup, are elbow-deep in fashion-
diagrams, and the wise woman who doesn’t confide herself
altogether to the stereotyped compassions of her dress-
maker is harassed in her dreams by phantom gores that
won’t set and flounces that refuse to pucker. Unfor-
tunately, however assiduous the seeker, there is no future
new idea to come out with and annihilate the other woman
who hasn’t studied the subject. Draperies are plain and
straight; sleeves are humped ; collars are Elizabethan,
and hats are floral in this “ sweet Spring time ” as they
were during the winter. Patterns are to be Pompadour,
and never are, somehow, and the best-dressed woman is
seldom sprigged all over. A faint novelty will be con.
trasting silk sleeves in place of velvet; and the passover-
cake hat is rather new, though the cold-blooded milliner
does all she can to make it common-place and “as now
worn ” by sticking in an inside crown and trimming it
strictly according to the dictates of the last four seasons.
A striking mode in an era of stale styles is to have the
front of the skirt and the bodice made in one, and shaped
and tucked to fit the figure. Only the willowy Brown-
Potterish belle should adopt this novelty, however. A
full-blown female tucked round the waist would remind
the ribald of a blown-out accordeon.
And lastly, if your gown is the fashionable butter-eup
but your parasol isn’t, you will be in the outer darkness
of fashion and the Gehenna of “the rage.” In other
dresses the gamp may v ary in tone (an ugly novelty is to
trim dark parasols with light-coloured chiffon), but that a
“ yaller ” shade shall fall on a “ yaller ” shoulder is one of
the necessary “points” by which one judges a bilious
get-up this Spring. And when I have said that cloaks
will be largely worn, that mauve will be the leading colour-
and that short-handled parasols, so short that the popular
feminine trick of “ walking ” with them will be impossible
unless one stoops, I think I have enumerated all there is
worth mentioning in the market-place at present.
A point to be well borne in mind by the confiding
young man who wagers with girls is that four-button
gloves are fashionable for street wear, and that ten
buttons, no matter how scraggy the elbow, how hairy the
epidermis, is the maximum evening length for ladies.
Why is it that the Thespienne invariably goes forth in
a x wide, rakish, piratical hat hauled down to larboard and
aggravated with nodding plumes and several yards of
veil through which the gifted lady’s more or less white
teeth, and a sort of a guess at a nose, are dimly per-
ceptible? They all sport ’em—Achurch, Potter (par
excellence), Stewart, everyone—-and now the lower-rung
mimes are taking up the parable, and the sagging hat
and the bobbing feather are almost a public nuisance.
And the coiffure beneath this übiquitous gable is never
the calmly-ordered locks of common-sense, but a frenzied
touzle, reminding one of a magpie’s nest after a long and
severe difference of opinion inside.
The fair young editresses of Sydney Parthenon— a
periodical circulating in the very best circles of Darling
Point—have thrown up their task in disgust, finding that
sustained effort is bad for the complexion, and now the
next number of that absorbing publication is to be edited
by a nice young man graduate who ought to feel his posi
tion keenly. What will his Greek roots and his Latin de-
clensions avail him when the respective merits of the
pickled onion and the jar of jam loom upon the horizon ?
Where will he hide his head when the parcels-post brings
him in the broidered flannel under-garment and tucked
camisole of competition ? Sappho is not in the editoria
confidence, but she will venture to bet that the Parthenon
sewing set-to’s will shortly consist solely of shirts, or
“ weskits,” or articles well within the province of the
giant intellect which has now grasped the helm. I here give
you a superb allegory which only wants a little sifting.
The thin edge of the wedge—the wedge of seventeen
generals to tour privates—is being inserted into the
N.S.W. Cadet corps. Minister Carruthers has discovered
(from a bird’s-eye view, gained from the top of that
elongated nutmeg-coloured steed probably) that there are
enough small soldiers to form a brigade, when, according
to Imperial regulations, the officer in command must have
a plurality of colonels beneath him, who, in their turn
must have their swarm of “ commissioned ” parasites, of
course.
Lady Onslow, the leader of Maori society, is evidently
as pawky as her Southernmost subjects. She allows it to
get about that she doesn’t want to pump-handle the same
people at her two monthly at-homes, and the consequence
is that, at reception No. 2, one tea-pot holds quite enough
to damp the dusty souls of the rear-guard. By-the-by,
the Onslows have lost their French cook, and the vice-
regal menu is once more thrown upon the hands of the
private-secretary, who was spelling “ pomes de terre ” like
that at latest advices.
A small band of fifteen children formed the nucleus of
the present N.S.W. Children’s Society for the Prevention
of Cruelty to Animals, which now numbers 215 large
“ bands of mercy” in the Sydney roll. Bands have been
established at the various State schools, and the hon.
secretary assures me that great heart is put into the work
by the youngsters, who elect their own office-bearers,
correspond with the hon. secretary, and contribute to the
society’s magazine without the aid of teachers. The
magazine (which is dedicated to verse, short stories, and
the feats of the society) is a penny monthly, edited by the
hon. secretary, Miss Levy, “Oreba,” Point Piper road,
Woollahra, who is the dumb animal’s friend to the extent
of trying to abolish the fashionable bearing-rein. The
bearing-rein, I may explain, is the instrument of torture
by which horses are made to proudly champ the bit and
otherwise present a haughty and baronial appearance,
The two breathless points about the “front” on the
“Macbeth” premier at Her Majesty’s were the cocksure
cackle of the female who had brought a pocket edition of
“Shakespeare ” along, and who always howled an incident
ahead of the prevailing situation, just to air her learned
research, as it were, and the fact that almost all the
pressmen who hang on to the back of the circle or scin-
tillated in reserved-seats were in the noblest of war-paint
—even to white hyacinth button-holers. The house was
a bumper, and the d.c. and the boxes were uncomfortably
full. Miss Aohuroh made up Lady Macbeth as a pale-
faced, golden blonde, wearing in the first act a dull
crimson cloak over a spangled under-dress, kerchief of
dark-blue patterned in white ; in the third act, a bar-
barously-gorgeous attire, the front and long sleeves of red
velvet, corselet of green beads, and train of black-and
gold brocade, a crown on the flowing butter-coloured
locks, of course; while the sleep-walking scene was
soliloquised through in the flowing snowy robes of night
though tradition has it that in those good old days people
weren’t so particular about putting on clothes after they
had just taken others off. Still the stage cannot always
be archsaologically correct, can it ?
Preceded by that hysterical martyr, “Barbara,” “A
Fool’s Paradise” at Sydney Criterion seems almost light
comedy, redolent though it is of arsenic a la Maybrick.
As Beatrice, Fanny Enson—always gowned to perfection
—wears in the first act a flesh-pink silky stuff with plain
skirt bordered with gold-brown velvet, sleeves of the pink
and Zouave bodice and high collar of the velvet. In act
two, sleeves and under-dress of pale pink veiled in white
chiffon, a trained sleeveless gown of pink designed in
crimson and fawn florally, the Shakesperian collar and the
fronts of the gown faced with crimson velvet. Mrs.
Brough, who is looking more attractive than ever, has
changed her face make-up from its former chalky white-
ness to the Brown-Potter flush round the eyes and on the
temples, combined with the favourite rings of black round
the ocular to enhance the size and brilliancy of the eye
—an unexpected alteration of her usual methods
to which the spectator doesn’t cotton all at once.
She wears in the second part of this act, a fashion-
able mustard-colour woollen en Princesse, with con-
trast of brown velvet, the robe fastening on one
side and open to the feet, being trimmed with brown
buttons and guipure, toque en mite. In the last
act Miss Enson wears a charming dinner-dress in dawn-
blue silk, with picture sleeves and bodice-folds of pale-blue
chiffon ; a small Zouave front is edged with silver pom-
poms, and the scarf, which is knotted loosely in the front,
is finished with silver tassels —a delicious dress. Mrs.
Brough’s get-up in white voile, star-shaped at the neck
over gold pattern, shoes and hose of tan colour, is also
soothing in a tin-pot way.
Unlike last year, when locomotion from “picter” to
“ pictchaw ” (you may take your choice of pronunciation
—both are popular) was an utter impossibility, N.S.W.
Art Society’s private view at the Pitt-Street rooms on 6th
inst. was attended by a comfortable number of invitees,
and though there was a crush, of course, still one did
occasionally see more than the frames of the works of art
But despite the temperate attendance, it was the same
private-view-cum-Turkish-bath which ever distinguishes
these oil-and-water-colour orgies in an oblong garret
three flights up, over which the sun seems to hang sus-
pended. Next year the show will very likely be held
in more possible premises—men will be pleased to
hear, since women don’t scorch like they do—or is it that
the pearl-powder dries-up the moist signal of distress?
Miss Achurch (who brought her dutiful hubby along) wore
a thick tweed dolman and a big black hat covered with a
suffocating veil: Lady Innes wore a cool black flsher’s-net
skirt, and a Zouave bodice of warm black velvet, with coat
draperies relieved with black passementerie, bonnet in
bugles and candy-tuft and black net veil; Mrs. J. R. Ash-
ton, Indian-red cloth and black velvet, a black drawn
gauze aureole hat trimmed with daffodils ; while another
salamander seemed rather to enjoy if in purple velvet and
poplin and a hat of pansies, mignonette and gardener’s
garters.
The thing about the show which most forcibly and
quickly strikes one is the gay, Oriental brilliancy of
colouring murally observed. In some instances the
tones are cheerful to the verge of the Xmas almanac.
Mrs. Threlfall’s portrait by President Ashton is an instance
of the riotous reds and blues which dominate the display.
The picture isn’t a good likeness to begin with, and the
face-colouring is to me simply atrocious. As for the hair>
the bang has all sorts of alarming tints which I can never
manage to see in hair, while the post-cranial Psyche knot
—well, it reminded me of ebb-tide in the dye-bottle, it is
so impossibly streaky.
The portraits of the show are undoubtedly those of Mr.
E. L. Monteflore by Mr. Ashton, and that of Mr.
a’Beckett, by Mrs. Mary Stoddard. The former, a
mosi admirable and dashing effort, will undoubtedly
please the artists best; but the latter, though huug in a
somewhat obscure even if well-lighted place, will obtain
a fair share of the suffrages of the more prosaic general
public who are addicted to admiring careful portraiture
and who are not yet educated in the mysteries of im-
pressionism.
In painting a portrait of Lord Carington in Masonic
full-fig, Mr. Ashton undertook a task the prospect
whereof might well strike terror into the stoutest heart.
Did anyone ever see a sight so dreadfully vulgar—mind
you, I am speaking now only of the make-up of the artist’s
subject—as this combination of every colour under
heaven? I had just been watching the great labour proces-
sion ere entering the gallery, and when my eye first
alighted on the gubernatorial portrait it at once
struck me as a slab out of one of the gaudy street
banners. Lord Carington’s complexion, as put on
canvas by Mr. Ashton, is disappointing, for in real
life the vice-regal facial cuticle is a faint shade of cedar,
not pale powder-pink. Mr. Dan. O’Connor’s portrait
doesn’t strike me as a perfect likeness, either facially or
as regards characteristic attitude’, but then Mr. Ashton
has had the advantage of seeing Mr. O'Connor’s face in
repose, whereas whenever I have encountered him he has
always been saying something—something nice.
The landscapes of the show are unquestionably Mr.
Arthur Streeton’s “Spring Pastoral,” Mr. Pigueuit’s
“Out West,” and Mr. Lister-Lister’s “ After a Shower on
Lane Cove River.” The first-named takes my fancy most
up to now, though I must confess I have never seen fresh
water of so blue a tint anywhere, nor, presuming
that Mr. Streeton intended the water as saltwater, am
I aware of any place in Australia where grass and
oliage of such a colour are to be found right alongside an
ocean-arm. Nevertheless, “ A Spring Pastoral” is a
lovely and boldly-painted picture. “ After a Shower,” by
Mr. Lister-Lister, which has been purchased for the
N.S.W. National Gallery, seems a little lacking in trans-
parency, but it is altogether a canvas of which the artist
may be very proud ; and Mr. Piguenit’s delightful “Out
West,” with its rich evening light, will, I predict, grow
on people the more they see it. There are a lot of other
pictures among the 300 exhibited about which I should
like to say something, but present space forbids and I
must stop here.
The completion of Sydney Centennial Hall puts money
"into the purses of the various ball-committees, for it
doesn’t lend itself to decoration. There are no bald
patches of grimy grease to be concealed with a flag or a
motto, and the oiled looks of the city fathers haven’t as
yet left horizontal shadows on the civic walls. Decoration
is a towering item in ball expenses, even if you only hire
at current rates a confirmed Domain-resident with string-
tied shoes to go forth and limb the everlasting Zamia an
the tough “horse-bed” fern from their sandy lair.
“Wanted,” from a Sydney daily :
V/WANTED, by an English Lady, a SITUATION as
" Housekeeper, with boy of 9; good plain cook,
and thorough knowledge of housekeeping ; musical, if
required. Apply O. Olarilla, Boulevarde, Dulwich
Hill.
“Musical if required ”is very English, isn’t it ? But what
a position for a female Britisher if she is snatched in by a
family that hasn’t music in its soul! Fancy feeling that
her whole being is surcharged with the “Maiden’s
Prayer,” or conscious that her eye is flashing in a martial
manner through suppressed Battle-of-Prague, and all the
while unable to relieve her inwardness on the keyboard !
Awful!
It cannot be that every girl whom one meets carrying a
fiddle in a case is actuated solely by an inward worship of
Euterpe that will have vent, or else their faces wouldn’t
look so desolate. Any girl who takes up with the piano
or violin, without a distinct taste therefor, is a common
idiot. Even if she studies with a bread-and-butter motive
there are lots of other industrial pursuits less dreadful
than mastering and imparting a knowledge of an instru-
ment the crude sound of which is simple torture. Scruti-
nise the faces of the dozens of girls you meet hawking
ungainly violin-cases (too infrequently used as recep-
tacles for gloves and laces, as is jocularly stated)
and see the dull eyes, the weary mouths, the lifeless
complexions, the tired gait. They have come from a
“lesson”—the easiest part of the programme, for when
they get home, tired out, the practice commences, when
the conscientious student—the girl studying hungrily to
be the sooner herself a teacher—is obliged to stand prying
at the new “ piece ” and giving herself and the household
the ear-ache with the shrill pierce of false flat notes for
hours at a stretch. To the fiddle-fanatic all this is like
cream to the cat-but there are few fiddle-fanatics. It is
my belief that compulsory music is souring the very soul
of the rising woman, spoiling her prettiness, her finger-
tips and her temper. The unwilling violinist needn’t carry
her gun to proclaim herself. The “fiddle-face” is fast
becoming as unconcealable as big feet.
Yours affectionately,
SAPPHO SMITH.

After an unprecedented run, the pretty but eternal
“fairy lamp” has gone out—as it always did on an
emergency, by the way. Cheap Jacks made them in
coarse “china,” the “wulgar” sported ’em proudly on
the round table in the parlor and their doom was spoken.
Miniature lamps—chimney, globe and all—covered with
dainty shkdes, are the correct supper-table illumination
just now, and are called “elf-lights.”
The indefatigable, hard-grafting committee of the
N.S.W. “ Women’s College ” have arranged for a dividend-
paying concert in the Great Hall of the Sydney ’Varsity,
which has been granted for the occasion by the com-
plaisant Senate.

To the Women of the Strike.,
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
Strike, strike, strike ! _
Ye sweethearts and wives of men !
For your hearts are needed to stay the hands and
the brain from evil ken.
Your palm, girl—straight—in your lover’s,
Your fearless eyes in his eyes,
Not’for puny Self, not for golden Pelf—for the
Nation’s Soul to rise !
Strike, strike, strike !
No question of paltry wage !
The Old World is watching to see you write a line
on the Earth’s life-page.
The World has stayed from its moaning,
The World is waiting to-day, _
For a nation’s Youth and a nation’s Truth to cut
its old chains away.
Strike ! Not for Greed or Glory—
For the Land that gave you birth,
That her children may sing and her lovers cling
and the sun shine on her mirth ;
Strike ! —For the floods are rising,
The waters 6’erflow the banks,
And to save the Soil the sons of Toil must labour
in serried ranks.
Strike ! Not with deadly weapon,
With powder and pistol blaze,
But the patience grave and the forehead brave of
those who the Standard raise !
For some may faint in the fighting,
And others may seek to roam,
But the weakest there will stand strong and
square if the Standard be raised at home !
Strike ! Ay, I know ’tis bitter
To see the cupboard so bare,
To offer the children a crust of bread when the
cake was always there !
But the creed you teach the children
Is one our old heroes gave :
Better crust of bread, with erect proud head, than
the cake and cringe of the slave !
List! To the weary sobbing
That over the water steals,
Where the brain is worn and the flesh is torn with
the crunch of the ceaseless wheels.
Weak women, and little children,
And men who have had no prime,
Are bedragged and crushed, their low wailings
hushed, with poverty for their crime.
The new and the ancient cities
Have legends so sad and fell!
New York and Paris and London —do you know
what their pavements tell ?
Can you hear the worn stones echo
The tramping of restless feet,
Men old with care, girls young and fair, all tramp-
ing in vain for meat!
Is the future’s hopeless sorrow
Worth buying with the present ease,
With the Sunday dish, and the children’s wish,
■ and the hundred things that please ?
Will you risk your son’s supineness,
Will you risk your daughter’s shame?
Or stand with pride by your good man’s side, and
strike, and risk the blame ?
The daughters of the people
Are the mothers of the race,
And the working lass must be free to pass through
life with unsullied grace.
The sons you bear the nation
Must lead in the Old World’s van,
Free, their hearts to give, free to love and live,
serving Honour more than Man !
Strike, strike, strike !
Your patience shall not be vain,
It shadows the hour when Earth owns the power
of Work, whether hand or brain.
Strike, so your patient endurance
Shall loose the chained souls beyond seas
And your children say in that fair, free day:
“ Our mothers were such as these !”
Strike, strike, strike ! _
Ye sweethearts and wives of men !
For your hearts are needed to stay the hands and
the brain from evil ken.
Your palm, girl—straight—in your lover’s,
Your fearless eyes in his eyes,
Not for puny Self, not for golden Pelf-for the
Nation’s Soul to rise !
ROSE DE BOHEME.
The New Zealand Herald , lifts up its
voice in lamentation on the subject of female
labour :
In several businesses women are supplanting men ; that
is, young women are supplanting young men. This is
one part of the struggle between Whitcombe and Tombs
and the Typographical Society. I hold that for a young-
woman to" work in an unwholesome workroom at a tedious
and fatiguing occupation, is utterly to spoil her'for the
duties of a wife and a mother. Then, if she takes the
place of a young man, what is the result? That young
man is compelled to leave the country, or walk about
the streets idle. Nowadays, if a young man
wants to go to the theatre, he has to get a
girl, who is" doing less than a man’s work at much less
than a man’s wages, to pay for his ticket. And these
employments are taking- women out of their sphere.
What is to become of posterity if women are to spend the
years when they should be strengthening their frames by
household work, which is congenial to their systems, in
hurtful labour? Why, posterity would not be worth
troubling about if it depended upon them. Besides, by
taking- up men’s occupations, young women are driving
out of the colony the young men who. ought to be their
husbands. They are condemning themselves by their
owm act to the miserable fate of spinsterhood.
Just so. If woman is going to jump the claim of
man, and drive him out of the country by taking
his job from him, then the population will dwindle
away till nothing remains but a spectacled, gaunt,
old maid with a shrill voice and a cat, labouring
in a tough and ancient fashion for 15s. a week.
For if the male citizbn can’t get a billet because a
woman has taken it at half the wages, it is evident
he can’t marry till she has saved enough to keep
him, and with her humble income, the probabilities
are that she will be a bony old harridan of 62
before that joyous result is arrived it, and then he
will most likely rise up and state that he doesn’t
want to waste his bright affections on a venerable
relic with her head and both feet in the grave, and
the romance of love’s glittering dream will fall to-
pieces. Consequently, where a happy household
ought to be, there will be only a sour and wheezy
old spinster swilling tea by a lonely hearth, while
a hoary paralytic from the workhouse will call
round occasionally in a perambulator to tell her
what might have been if she hadn’t collared his
situation at reduced rates, and left him out in the
cold when he was young.
Killing the Pig: A Bush Sketch.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
This is the time of year when the thrifty dweller
in the bush who has been pampering the corpulent
hog in the choek-and-log stye for at least nine
months sharpens the family carving-knife and
grinds a fresh edge on the unassuming but busi-
ness-like wood-axe. He also sends his eldest boy
out to look for tea-chest lead, and when that
smart youth returns with a double armful which
he has “copped ” from the neighbours’ ash-heaps,
he is belted with a strip of green-hide for having
lost the bullet-mould. Then the rusty saucepan
with the cracked bottom is once more dragged
forth to the light of day and loaded to the muzzle
with lead and old jam-tins, and set on the fire
while the old man unhooks the gun from the wall
and abuses his wife for not knowing where the caps
are, and enquires “ where the blazes she put that
there powder. ” While these murderous appliances
are being found, the lead melts or is capsized by the
pet goat, or heaved out at the back-door by the
irate house-mother, because she wants the fire to
set her camp-oven on. If the melting process
is achieved without any fatalities, the hardy
bread-winner proceeds to burn many holes in his
clothes in the attempt to cast bullets. He also, as a
rule, drops several blobs of molten lead on his feet,
and startles the quiet atmosphere with a quantity of
crackling electric talk. If the house-dog takes an
interest in the proceedings, as he usually does, he
never fails to acquire a share of the fluid metal on
his moist and quivering nostril which draws from
him an agonised howl as he runs between the
baby’s legs, and spills that infant rearwards into
the boiler of water that is being heated for the
ceremony, retiring at the double as_ the child’s
mother pursues him with the rake. When the old
man has* succeeded in moulding a bullet in shape
something between a broad bean and a quartz
crystal, he loads up his gun.
* f #
All the neighbours within a seven-mile radius
are now assembled, and each one of them has
eleven separate and distinct theories on the sub-
ject of hog-slaughter. If the owner of the about-
to-be-murdered porker adopted any one of these
plans, he would be regarded as a hopeless
idiot by all the remaining citizens. But
he doesn’t. His method of proceeding differs
totally from that of each and every one of his
ad visers, and is looked upon with utter contempt
by them all. Everyone of them wants the privi-
lege of shooting the obese animal, but the pro-
prietor proudly avers his intention of performing
the onerous duty himself. As he_ shoulders the
gun and, with a tremulous assumption of cool in-
difference, proceeds towards the stye followed by
23 neighbours and his wife and family and the pet
goat in skirmishing order, the excitement is in-
tense. The affair is positively thrilling, and the
only individual whose nerves are not in a painful
state of tension is the condemned quadruped him-
self. He looks up in a mildly inquiring way as
the procession nears his residence, and then shuts
his eyes again with an air of bored indifference.
Then, amid dead silence, the chief slaughterer,
after a long aim with the gun resting on the logs
of the stye, pulls the trigger, and—the gun misses
fire. “ Powder must be damp,” he says. He puts
on a fresh cap and “tries her again’’—this time
without any other formality than just pulling the
trigger while the barrel is sagging round in a
promiscuous and strictly impartial way. She
goes off this time, with a roar like a Nordenfeldt,
and the bullet chips the left ear off one of the
spectators. With an unreasonable display of
acrimony, this person takes the gun away from
the owner and bangs it against a big rock till it is
fit only for kindling-wood and gaspipe. ihen
another spectator, whose feelings have been
wrought up to a state bordering on frenzy, climbs
into the stye and brains the pig with a morticing-
axe which he has brought on the off-chance that it
might be useful.
* * *
This diverts the general attention from
the gun-smasher, and the _ defunct porker now
gets the undivided attention of the multitude.
He is soused in hot water and cold water, and
dragged through the sand, and finally dumped
down on a sheet of bark. All hands then set to
work at tearing handfuls of hair from the carcase,
and in two hours and a-half quite a considerable
quantity has been removed, and the performers
ooze out pig’s bristle and perspiration at every
pore. But the majority of the bristles won't
come out, because the water was too hot,
as one man says, or too blamed cold, as
another opines, or because the blanky fools
don’t none of ’em know how to scald a gory pig,
as a dispassionate observer remarks. The
disembowelling process is next performed, and
the innocent cause of all the trouble is then sus-
pended to a bough, and left alone for the night.
The spectators gradually disperse, and as night’s
sable mantle falls on an atmosphere of gore and
steam and hog’s lard and swear words, the family
retires to rest. Generally somebody comes along
about midnight and removes the silent sufferer
from the scene of his woes. In that case the
amount of profanity and undiluted misery in that
household the next day is something awful in its
immensity. But if the victim does not fall a prey
to body-snatchers, there is even a greater amount
of hard talk perpetrated during the dissecting
process. Nothing in this world so demoralises a
man, takes the virtue out of him, and shakes-up
his original sin, as cutting up a pig. The only
other occasion on which he shows signs of a similar
depravity is when the hams and bacon begin to
show signs of decrepitude, become off-colour, so
to speak, ere mortification sets in. The hog-
cultivator then “chucks the whole bloomin’ lot
out,” while indulging in parti-coloured oaths.
After this, he is a miserable being until he has
paid 255. for another pig. When it has eaten
about seven pounds worth of corn, the matinee
performance is repeated, and is received with un-
diminished enthusiasm.
“Age cannot wither, nor custom stale
Its infinite variety !” —Bacon-Shakspeare.

Tales of “The System."
No. IX.: The Little Joke of No. 6006.
(FOR THE BULLETIN.)
It would be a mistake to suppose that the
System dealt solely in the vulgar, horrible and
tragic. No impression could bo more errone-
ous than that which would attribute to It an
unrelieved ghastliness of character. It had its
humorous moments and its farcical intervals. It
invented “situations” of comicality surpassing
the extravagancies of the pantomimists. Its
records supply materials enough to build-up a
reputation for a dozen mfit-makers like George
Selwyn or Theodore Hook. Viewed, indeed,
from the standpoint of a British Governing
Official, the System itself was but one huge joke
that suffered an occasional aberration towards
seriousness.
Undoubtedly, the System was a joke—a capital
joke! Only under a System which was itself
but an expression of jocularity could the situation
have arisen which Convict No. 6006 made an oc-
casion for a little sport, by which he, at one and
the same time, advantaged himself, and rather
disadvantaged two or three other people.
* # #
On a certain Monday morning in the year
188 that very ably-conducted and respectable
paper, the Hobart Mercury, contained a death-
notice, a funeral-advertisement, and a long “Town
Talk” paragraph, all having reference to the same
person.
The death-notice was thus worded :
X.—On the 15th inst., at his residence, “The Grass-
plot,” Sandy Bay-road, Zedekiah X., Esq., J.P., formerly
of Uffeulm®, near Exeter, England, aged 63 years. Deeply
regretted.
The undertaker’s advertisement, in the cus-
tomary tearful phraseology, extended a respectful
invitation to all and sundry of the friends of the
late lamented Zedekiah (and particularly to his
fellow-worshippers at St. David’s and his brother-
members of 1.0. R., 1.0.G.T., and L.0.L.) to
follow the “ remains of the deceased” to Cornelian
Bay Cemetery.
The paragraph, owing to the great limitations
on our space, we cannot reproduce at full length,
and our quotation of a few passages must satisfy
the reader. Said contemporary ;
We regret to have to record the departure, to join the
great majority, of Mr. Zedekiah X., J.P., of Sandy Bay-
road, who, it is unnecessary to inform the public, was an
esteemed resident of about forty years’ standing. .
The deceased gentleman had, in the course of his long
and useful career, taken part in most of the public move-
ments of the colony, and was a particularly ardent mem-
ber of the Anti-transportation League, whose patriotic
labours freed this Island, the most beautiful of God’s
creations, from the curse of convictism He
was frequently urged to consent to be placed in nomina-
tion for the Legislature, but the modesty of his nature
prevented his acceptance of any other honorary trust at
the hands of his fellow-citizens or the authorities than a
Commission of the Peace, As a magistrate he was con-__
stant in attendance at the Queensborough Court, and his
decisions, founded as they were on a wide experience of
life and a more than ordinary knowledge of the law, were
seldom at conflict with equity and justice. . . Con-
siderable success had attended the business enterprises
whereon he entered after leaving the employ of the
Government in which he was previously engaged. .
In his home circle he was both respected and loved. Of
consistently pious life and a devoted Churchman, yet
there was nothing austere in his disposition, and, being
ever fond of a little joke, he was the pleasantest of com-
panions.
This elegant obituary record concluded by re-
porting the interment of Mr. X.’s remains in Cor-
nelian Bay Cemetery—that charming Necropolis
which, according to a local guide-book, “offers
the most salubrious situation and the most magni-
ficent of scenic aspects to tourists desirous of
accommodation ” in the presence of a large and
reverent throng.
» # *
Of course the newspaper put a surname
where we have put “ X.,” but we will explain why
we substitute for the deceased Zedekiah’s patrony-
mic, the algebraic symbol icpresenting an un-
known quantity. It affords no clue to Zedekiah’s
identity, so far as the public are
concerned, “X,” a quantity unknown. Again, no
other symbol or expression would adequately re-
present the extent to which that deeply-deplored
party would go in order to indulge his fondness for
an innocent joke. And, finally, Zedekiah himself
desired to remain unknown in connection with the
transaction hereinafter to be recorded.
In short, we substitute the letter for the name in
the newspaper, because we have identified with
the defunct Zedekiah, Convict No. 6006, who was
very much alive in August, 184-.
* * #
No. 6006—-variously addressed on the
Island as “ Writer Zed.,” “ Six-two-ort-six,” and
” Six thou’ an’ six ” was “ Storekeeper’s writer,”
that is, prison-clerk to the official whose duty it
was to receive and give out the Government
“ stores.” Always “ fond of a little joke,” he had
in Ins boyhood played witticisms of the practical
kind on a rabbit and a lord of the manor. Some-
how the Game Laws, Judges of Assize, and a High
Sheriff got mixed up in the joke in a rather
curious fashion, a result of their undesired inter-
ference being an inexpensive, if lengthy, sea-trip
and some other corollaries which need not be re-
capitulated. Domiciled later at Point Puer,
the “ boys’ settlement ” at Port Arthur, V.D.L.,
the Rev. Mr. Manton, Chaplain and School-
master, received No. 6006 into his especial favour.
Mr. Manton, good man, placed him in a monitor-
ship and gave him a sound drilling in reli-
gious doctrines and the three R’s. Re-
moved at the ago of 20 to Oatlands gaol,
as assistant writer, he was soon enabled, by the
companionship he there enjoyed, to qualify for
Norfolk Island by a second conviction. Imitating,
in another jocular moment, Mr. Manton’s n'gna-
ture, and affixing the copy to an order on a Hobart
Town tradesman for certain goods, he obtained
the articles, sold them, went on the spree with the
money, and after an interview with Chief Justice
Pedder, found himself on his way to the Island.
His expertness with the pen, coupled with a letter
conveying Mr. Manton’s testimony to his
familiarity with religious dogma (which document,
we regret to say, was forged) obtained for him,
immediately on arrival, the much-coveted billet of
a writership, and he had been general factotum to
the storekeeper for a couple of years when C. C.
Scragge gave his picnic party.*
# # *
When Knatchbull, the Commandant’s
writer, set about the work of preparation for that
high officer’s festival, the one “ free ” clerk in the
“Office,” Mr. Sutherland, had applied for and
been granted the services of the storekeeper’s
writer, No. 6006, so that the heavy clerical work de-
manded by the routine should not fall into arrear.
Zed. had, on prior occasions, assisted the office
staff, and was quite conversant with the manner in
which things were done and left undone there.
He did not, therefore, experience the slightest
tw 'tcn of surprise when on the picnic morning
and half-an-hour subsequent to the departure of
the party of picnicers from the Grassplot, Free
Clerk Sutherland threw him a couple of figs of
tobacco, and gavehirna “snifter”of nun. No. 6006
perfectly understood that he was thereby bribed
to do the Free Clerk's work for the day in addition
,i' >Wn ’ au(l Nearly apprehended that the
F .C., disgusted at not being included among the
Commandant s invitations, was determined to
have a little amusement on his own account. As
a simple fact, the F.C. went for a very enjoyable
day’s shooting in the scrub at the base of Mount
Pitt.
* * #
Writer Zed,, being left alone in the office,
and knowing that in the absence of the superior
officials he would be undisturbed for several hours,
didn’t see why he also shouldn’t have some fun.
His idea of fun, of course, meant doing what he
ought not to do. He began his self-entertainment
by filling and lighting his pipe. We may here
interject that the tobacco with which he filled it,
aS as rum which he had previously ab-
sorbed, were the “special medical comforts” of
“ Lifer ” Kelly, who was in the hospital with a
broken leg. The F.C. was also hospital dispen er,
and, in diverting to his own use and profit many
articles intended for convict “comfort,” he was
merely doing the regular thing. The System
devoutly believed in honesty—for other than its
administrators.
Now, in the very first hour of No. 6006’s tem-
porary tenure of Knatchbull’s post, he had noticed
that the books of official communications,
brought to the C.C. by the Governor Phillip brig
on her last trip, were still unopened. As pre-
viously recorded, Commandant Scragge had gone
on a drinking-bout, as soon as the brig’s arrival
permitted him to repleni-h his liquor stock; and
he had barely recovered from this orgy, when ti e
preparations for his picnic claimed his attmtion.
The few bundles of correspondence that he had
withdrawn from their wrappers had accordingly
been but cursorily examined, and the mass of the
despatches were as yet untouched. The custom
of the Comptroller-General’s department in
Hobart I own was to allow documents needing
transmission to the Norfolk Island authorities to
accumulate till the succeeding voyage of the
Governor Phillip (the brig made four trips yearly),
and then forward all papers of a like character
enclosed within one cover. Thus, all “Comp-
troller’s minutes on Civil Commandant’s reports,”
however numerous, would be covered by one
wrapper; “Storekeepers’ Summaries,” by
another ; “ Schedules of Transfers,” or the list of
“ Commitals to Norfolk Island from Y.D.L.,” by
a third ; and so on. Upon the wrapper of each
would be endorsed the description of its respective
contents.
Turning over the heap of despatches that still
bore their seals intact, for no earthly reason than
that he knew he should not touch the papers on
the Commandant’s table at all, No. 6006 observed
that one thin, unopened packet was t bus -id
dressed :
Orders for Discharge. )
Schedule of lie-transfers. )■
Pardons. ‘ )
Per
“ Governor Phillip," pg.
19th August,
The CIVIL COMMANDANT,
H.M. Penal Establishment,
NORFOLK ISLAND.
Office of the Comptroller-General of Convicts,
„ Hobart Town,
J.F. 18/8/4—
Convict No. 6000 took the envelope in his
hand and inspected it with a keen curt si tv
wondering the while how long it would be before
his “discharge” came in that way. The idea
would project itself into his mind that when his
order lor freedom arrived, it would reach him as
it reached most of the prison-population of 'the
Island, That is to say, it would be wrapped
round a bullet, or dangling on to a rope's-end, or
tendered on the point of the surgeon’s scalpel
Twenty-four years old—and he had still to servo
four years of his original sentence, and ten on top
of that for the Y.D.L, forgery ! Fourteen years
which ought to be reduced to ten, if he gave no
chance to his capricious masters to complain of
him, and no opportunity to the hundred or so of
fellow prisoners who were hungering for his billet
to “ put him away ! ” Ten years ! And then’
perhaps, he would go out into the world with the
infamous brand of a “Norfolker” upon him -out
unto the world which when it was not a stabbim-
world was a jeering world ; but, perhaps, on the
other hand, he would, by the time he was “duo
for freedom,” have learnt to love his chains, and
have grown friendly with granite escarpments and
the fringes of coral that held him within their vyce-
like grasp. Convict “ Six-two-’ort-six ” shuddered
as these thoughts stirred him ; shuddered— and
then laughed aloud with a harsh and scabrous
laughter that, had it claimed a close cousinship
with the bitter cry of pain, would have had its
demand admitted. Laughed and shuddered
again; and the gusty passion which so often
seized prisoners denied the ordinary channels of
venting their temper gripped him; and, throwing
the packet on the matted floor, he stamped on if
His canvas slippers— “regulation ” issue to all
men in “ light billets ’’—were fortunately nail-less
Their flushed sprigs did not mark the envelope •
and the only damage done was the breaking of the
red-wax seal. That lay in a dozen dull-glinting
fragments.
Convict 6006, his mad spasm expended, picked
the letter up with some confused notion of re-
placing the lumps of wax on the wrapper, but a
moment’s consideration showed him the useless-
ness of an attempt of that sort. So he searched
for the Commandant’s official seal. He knew it
was somewhere about, for the C.C. had, the pre-
ceding day, cursed the Comptroller in diverse
forms of imprecation for having failed to fill the
requisition for a new seal. When the Island
had been handed over by New South Wales
to Van Diemen's Land the year before,
a fresh seal had _ been ordered from Hobart
Town, but, so far, it had not turned up, and Mr
Scragge had, perforce, to use the old one. There
was no difference in the designs of the depart-
mental seals of the two colonies, save in the ini-
(Continued on page 24.)

We notice Avith pleasure that the Equitable
Mortgage, Discount, and Deposit Bank, of 4
Moore-street, Sydney, has recently been incor-
porated, and is hoav opened for business. This
bank has been formed for the purpose of assisting
those who may at any time be in pecuniary diffi-
culties and wish to avoid paying the excessive in-
terest which is too often charged. The bank has
secured the able services of Mr. Henry A. Birk-
myre, Avhich should be a guarantee that clients will
be fairly dealt with. In these days of extortion, the
above institution should about fill a long-felt

As best for this young country’s weal.

Write for plans £lO Building sites, Perth,
