Company, Limited, 96 Bathurst-street, Sydney.
tials of the colony. Circular in shape with a
crown in the centre, the legend, “Penal Estab-
lishment,” curved above the symbol, the letters
“N.S.W.” or “V.D.L.” filled the base. The
impress of either seal would pass on slight exami-
nation for that of the other, so close was the re-
semblance of the instruments. Such, at all events,
was the conclusion of Convict “ Six-thou’-an’-
Six,” as, after discovering the seal in the 0.0. ’s
private and locked drawer, he dropped a pool of
wax on a scrap of paper, pressed the stamp upon
it and compared the result with one of the sealed
packets from Hobart Town. And Convict “ Six-
thou’-an’-Six ” was so well acquainted with
Soragge’s habits that he knew the seal would not
receive even a cursory inspection. A few days
before the brig started on her return voyage the
C.C. would rush into the office, tear off rapidly
the wrappers of the remaining despatches, and
distribute most of their contents among the avail-
able “clerical strength ” co be acknowledged and
attended to.
That was the Civil Commandant’s method of
management, and No. 6006 was quite easy
in his mind as to the prospects of the
substituted seal escaping detection. Very
likely Scraggs, indeed, would never notice
the absence of the seal, but, nevertheless, No.
6006 thought he had better be on the safe side,
and he was accordingly about to drop a lump of
heated wax on the packet when the thought
occurred to him that he might as well know who
were “ down for freedom. ” What a capital joke
it would be to set a rumour floating about the
Island in the curious way that authentic reports
and well-founded information were accustomed to
circulate —originating no one could say how,
transmitted no one would say whence —that Such-
and-Such a Number was booked for liberty. What
a joke to hear the protests from the man himself
that “ it was too good to be trueto listen to the
junior officials indignantly scouting the notion as
utterly improbable ; and then for the fact of the
discharge to come out a week later by the voice of
the Commandant himself. Perhaps this may not
strike the reader as being a very humorous con-
ception, but Convict No. 6006 knew it would be
regarded as a brilliant joke on the Island, because
it would “dish” the C.C. One of Scragge’s
pleasing characteristics was to withhold from the
man concerned and the subordinate officers the
knowledge that a prisoner had been pardoned or
otherwise discharged till a few hours before the
time of the departure of the brig; and nothing
annoyed him more than to have the effect of his
announcement of the fact discounted.
So No. 6006, being fond of his joke, determined
to learn who were “ down for freedom ” from the
documents wrapped in the packet. Scarcely sooner
determined upon than the thing was done. The
opening of an official envelope was less difficult
than the forcing of the lock of the C.C.’s private
door, which had taken the accomplished No. 6006
exactly one minute and ten seconds by the Settle-
ment clock.
There were precious few things, let us say. re-
quiring a deft touch that Convict No. 6006 couldn’t
do; and fewer still of the artistic methods of
manipulating documents with which he was not
acquainted. Old Pounce, ex-scrivener’s clerk and
five-times-convicted forger (he had been cast for
death twice —once in the old country and once in
Sydney) and principal writer at Oatlands Gaol,
had no reason to be ashamed of his pupil. He had
taken No. 6006 when he was comparatively inno-
cent, having little more on his conscience than
that poached rabbit, and he had made him an
expert “penman.” “Boy,” he had said to his
scholar, when he was shown the forged order on
the Hobart Town tradesman, before it was uttered,
“Boy, you do me proud!” He was always
paternal, was old Pounce.
But we are digressing. We left No. 6006 read-
ing the documents he had withdrawn from the
now-nnsealed packet.
The first he took up was the Schedule of Re-
transfers—men who, for various reasons, were to
be shipped back to V.D.L. The schedule included
but six names, and as none of them belonged to
men numbered in the list of “ six-thou’-an’-six’s”
friends, he was not interested in it.
Then he took up the abstract of “ Orders for
Discharge.” Three men were down for release
therein —Edward Hogan, Simon Sugden, John
Julius.
“Gosh!” said Writer Zed., to himself; “I’m
blest if the Gov’ment ain’t gettin’ liberal. Three
men a-quarter freed out o’ nineteen hundred!
Why, it’s a whole dozen a-year ! Ain’t they
generous ! ” And he stopped his reading of the
papers to calculate how long it would take to de-
populate the Island at that rate, and allowing for
no new arrivals.
“A hundred an’fifty-eight years ! Lor’, it’s a
d d good thing for Gov’ment that Skeleton
Johnny* takes a hand at th’ game ! What ’ud
Gov’ment do without Jack Ketch and the
Croaker ?+ Fancy a-rationin’ us all for a hun-
derd-an’-fifty years ! ” And he almost regretted
that he was not compelled to mess in one of the
big yards so that he might repeat the jokelet to
ten score of prisoners and so set the table in a roar.
Having sufficiently enjoyed his own pleasantry
for the time being, he continued his perusal of the
enclosures. Attached to the “abstract” were
some other papers. These were the “Discharge
Orders. ”
“ Hogan, Sugden, Julius—none of ’em my pals,”
he reflected, as he scanned the sheets, and then he
suddenly recollected that he did know Hogan.
“Edward Hogan—why, o’ course, that’s Ned
the Bricky, th’ ol’ cove I give th’ ’baccy to when
Greenwood’s gang was buildin’ in th’ new oven for
th’ bakery.”
This was a fact, and, perhaps, the circum-
stance is worth narrating as showing how the
System worked in little things. Old Ned, seeing
the “Writer” standing at the store-door, had, in
the mute language that all Government men un-
derstood, begged a bit “o’ th’ stuff.” No. 6006
had only enough on him for that night’s “ chaw,”
but he was aware that, except by a miracle, poor
old Bricky would not taste the weed for a twelve
month together. So, though the act would be one
of uncommon self-denial, he resolved to give him
the remains of his fig. First, however, he would
have his little joke.
He went up to the gang and spoke to Hogan.
The Bricky, on pain of “ cells ” or the “ tube gag ”
dare not speak to the Writer, but the Writer,
being in a fair degree of favour with the Settle-
ment bosses, would not be informed on by Works-
Overseer Greenwood for addressing Ned.
“Bricky,” quoth the genial Writer, “you’d
like some ’baccy, wouldn’t you ?” As the magic
words fell from No. 6006, the Overseer and all his
gang pricked up their ears. The former expected
“a case” for Darrell; and the latter fellows
thought that if there was any of the “stuff”
going to be about they might “stand in for a
chaw.”
“Ned the Bricky” glanced round fearfully at
the Overseer, but Greenwood’s eyes were turned
the other way—purposely. Then Ned dared to
look his acquiescence ; a look of feverish, hungry
consent.
“But, Bricky,” went on No. 6006, “it ’ud do
you harm ; the croaker says th’ immoderate use o’
’baccy injures di-jestshun, spoils gastric juices, you
know—l ’udn’t like to do you harm, Bricky, an’
if your di-jest-shun was spoilt, you c’udn’t enjoy
that fowl an’ green peas what you’re goin’ to have
next Sunday when th’ Comm’dant asks you to
dinner !”
Bricky, stupefied by over a quarter of a century
of Government work, didn’t quite follow the
Writer’s pleasant jesting. He knew, however, he
was being laughed at, and the light went out of
his seared and lash-bitten face. The Overseer and
the others chuckled merrily,
“And, parson says,” proceeded No. 6006,
“ that it’s good for th’spirit to mortify one’s de-
sires. Now, Bricky, take my advice, I’m a young
’un, Bricky—but I’ve seen more o’ th’ world ’n you
—you ain’t been travellin’ much o’ late
years, Bricky, have you ? You’re one o’
th’ stay-at-’omes, ain’t you, ol’ chap?”—the
sentiment in the gang was now that the store-
keeper’s clerk was quite a funny dog!—“Now,
just you mortify th’ flesh, and don’t indulge in no
desire for ’baccy! Do that, Bricky—an’ lor’!
before you know where you are, you’ll have your
wings a-sproutin’ an’ go where all th’ good ’uns
like Parson Ford, an’ Tommy Taylor, an’ Mr.
Scragge’s a-goin’. Good day, ol’ chap; don’t yer
bother ’bout no ’baccy—’baccy ain’t good neither
for your soul nor body.” Greenwood had
turned away, convinced No. 6006 was simply on
joking bent, and—a rapid movement of the Wri-
ter’s wrist, and a splash of mortar from old Ned’s
trowel covered the oo veted half-fig. Bricky’s wings
didn’t sprout exactly, but he spent the succeeding
two nights in Elysium. “ Six-thou’-an’-six” might
be fond of his joke, but there was a lot of good in
him. The good proved itself (to society’s satis-
faction) in the later days, when Zed. became a
churchwarden, and a Grand Something in the
Good Templars; but we’ll vow his gift of tobacco
beat his after-deeds by a long head. It cost him
something, you see; it was pure self-sacrifice,
which couldn’t be always said of his doings when
he was “respectable.”
It was this Ned the Bricky whose order of dis-
charge we left No. 6006 perusing. He was specu-
lating as to what the miserable devil would do
with the morsel of existence that remained to him,
when he saw, pinned to the back of the foolscap
sheet, a note addressed to “0. C. Scragge—Pbi-
vate.”
“That’s Chief-Clerk Scribbins’s fist,” he said,
as he recognised the superscription. “What’s in
it, I wonder ?” He wondered just as long and no
longer than it took to open the note, and he, much
interested, read therein as follows :
H. T., 15 August, 184 —.
My dear Scragge,—l want you to do me a favour—or,
rather, Slyde. Slyde doesn’t care to address you direct
on the matter. I suppose he thinks you would not do it
for him, as perhaps you still bear him a grudge for getting
that girl away when you wanted her (though I don’t see
why you should, seeing the devil of a row into which he
got over the jade), and as he had, in any case, to ask me
to put the thing straight in our books here, he has left it
with me to make it right with you. The case is this : A
man named Hogan came out per Neptune in 1808 under
two sentences, one of 10 and the other of 21 years’. He
was a bricklayer by trade, and so Macquarie kept him at
work for Government instead of letting him out, and it
must have been because he was a good hand that he was
sent to the Island, as no prison offence can be found re-
corded against him. Now, supposing he was entitled to
no remission (as he must have been, as there is no gaol
offence) he was due for liberty in ’39, and, of course, has
overstayed his time some years. According to your last
annual Abstract of Gaol Strength he was included in your
Works Gang.
It appears that by some negligence at Sydney, for
which Slyde says he will be held responsible unless we can
adjust matters, the fact of the old wretch being due for
discharge was overlooked, and has only now been dis-
covered on the Sydney people writing off from their
records all men transferred to ns when this Government
took over the Island last year. Now Slyde wishes me to
fix up the thing here, and you to square'the books at your
end. He wants me, and you through me, to put one or
two prison offences against the fellow, so as to make it
appear he has done the extra time regularly, and you
can easily fix it. up, if you like. A few years ago
a slip of this kind would not have mattered either one way
or the other, but with these d—d newspapers poking their
noses where they are not wanted, we have to be careful.
Do it, there’s a good fellow, and let me have particulars
so that I can make my books straight here. Of course,
it’s just possible that Hogan was really entitled to do
extra time through committing some offence with you,
and that it has not been reported. This, however, did not
occur to me till after I had got the old man to authorise
his release as a “ special grace remittee,” so, having got
the order, I think it best to let it go. I needn’t say the
old man doesn’t know of the real reason. He’d be only
too glad to pick a bone with the Sydney lot. By the way,
he says you’ve been reporting too many suicides recently.
He says you ought to know better— fix them up as “acci-
dental drownings,” or “escapes.” He’s afraid of those
infernal newspapers—wishes, every day, old Bose was
back again to run the Courier on Government lines and
back up the System. We sadly want an organ. Ta-ta !
kind regards. Hope you will like the latest addition to
your garrison, Mrs. Browning, who goes up by the brig
this trip.
Sincerely Yours,
George S. Scribbins.
P.S.—Of course Hogan must not suspect he’s done over-
time, so keep the fact from your writers. I know the
fellows: they’ll blab. I’d take it out of the man somehow,
though, if I were you. Fancy his getting at Government
for his keep all these years—why, he ought to be flogged
for stealing rations.
G. S. S.
# # *
Thanks to Mr. Pounce’s admonitions—
Pounce had one or two gentlemanly instincts lin-
gering in his nature —No. 6006 had conceived a
dislike to the blasphemous and obscene forms of
ejaculation. “ Leave all such to the officers,” was
Pounce’s advice, and his pupil had followed it, as
he had followed worse counsels. So, now, although
in the extreme of amused surprise at the peculiar
humour of hfr. Chief Clerk Scribbins, and
although obeying a necessity to say something
very strong indeed, he did not swear. Not he ;
but he used the most expletive expression in his
vocabulary, and exclaimed :
“ May Ibe Parson Eord-ed, if I thought Mr.
Scribbins ’ud ha’ written that! Lor ! How he’s
changed—he ’udnt ha’ written so once. ”
The System being sui generis a Thing by itself,
had its peculiar language and its distinctive
figures of speech. To be “ Parson Ford-ed ” was,
in convict vernacular, to be reduced to the level of
that eminent ecclesiastic, who, to the convict
mind, symbolised everything damnable. No.
6006, who had been under Scribbins at Port Arthur
when the latter was Commissariat Clerk, and a
very good master to the prisoners, was greatly
surprised by the tone of the private and con-
fidential note to Scragge. “ How Scribbins must
ha’changed for the wuss !” he repeated. “It’s
that Corn’troller as has done it, I’m sure.”
Perhaps “the old man ” was partly accountable
for Mr. Scribbins’ observation; but Zed.
didn’t make sufficient allowance for the System of
which the “old man,” though Comptroller of
34,000 convicts, was a comparatively insignificant
factor. “The System, your Excellency,” as
Chaplain Taylor said when handing in his resigna-
tion after 14 years’ battle with it —“the System
would convert Patinos into Pandemonium, and
transform St. John into Beelzebub.” It would
have been an exceptionally curious circumstance
had Scribbins not changed for the worse.
» * *
No. 6006 replaced Scribbins’ letter in its
cover, and was re-folding the “Orders for Dis-
charge ” when the notion suggested itself to him
that it was a pity that order was not in his
favour instead of old Bricky's. What the dickens
would Hogan do with his freedom when he got it ?
Over 70 years old, and after a half-century of
convict routine, what would he do with it ? Get
“into trouble ” again the first hour he spent in
Hobart Town—that’s what he would do with it,
to a dead certainty. And there was he,' No. 6008,
still young and hale, whom the System had not
robbed of all vim and vigour—he* was to stop—
stop—stop within that sea-girt prison ! The
thought was maddening. Why was his name not
in that order, and that abstract, and not Hogan’s?
Why shouldn’t it be ? And then his little joke—
the joke par excellence of his life—assumed bodily
shape.
No. 6006 did not go for his mid-day ration,
and as, being a “billet” man, he was not mustered
except on Sundays, his absence excited no com-
ment. He was, in his judgment, much better
employed than in masticating what remained of
his sixteen ounces of meat and eight ounces of
potatoes after boiling-down and the peculations
of the cooks. He spent the shining hours in
making a slight alteration in the wording of one
the discharge orders.
He did not touch the body of the document,
which was partly printed and partly inscribed to
this effect :
H.M. Penal Establishment,
Hobart Town,
17 August, 184—.
This is to command you to release from your
custody the body of the prisoner named in the
margin, his Excellency the Governor-in-Council
having been pleased to remit the unexpired por-
tion of the sentence passed upon the said prisoner
in due course of law.
(Sd.)
James Fubster,
Comptroller-General of Convicts.
To the Civil Commandant and Gaoler,
H.M. Penal Establishment,
Norfolk Island,
V. D. L.
He only made a slight alteration in the name
noted in ting margin. For “ Edward Hogan, per
Neptune, No. 10,131,” he substituted “ Zedekiah'
X , per Morley (boys), No. 6006.” Nor did
he touch the manuscript footnote—
The swithin-nauied prisoner to have free passage pe r
Government brig to Hobart Town.
Sd. J.F. 12/8/4 --
You may wager your life, he didn’t alter that!
After he had manipulated the Discharge Order,
he devoted some little attention to the Abstract.
The paper on which this document was prepared
was of a more substantial character than the woven
foolscap of the Order, and occasioned him less
trouble in substituting for the particulars of Mr.
Hogan’s identity those relating to himself. The
result in both cases was satisfactory.
“There,” he said, as he finished. “I don’t
think old Pounce ’ud be ashamed o’ his pupil. ’E
c’uldn’t ha’ done ’em better himself! ”
He was re-folding the papers, when his eye
caught the solitary “Pardon” contained in the
packet. He opened the parchment, and found
that it was a “ Conditional Pardon ” in favour of
Walter Edward Tappin, No. 18,969, per Governor
Ready.
“Phew!”ejaculated No. “six-thou’-an’-six” “A
‘C.P.’ for his Worship lying here all these ten
days past, an’ him gettin’ ‘cells,’ an’ bein’sent
to D Gang all the time! Well, it’s too jolly
aggervatin’! ” And having made this friendly
and sympathetic observation, he proceeded to close
the packet and to seal it—taking especial care with
the sigillatum, you may be sure. The operation
concluded, he again recreated himself with a
hearty laugh. Yery musical was his laughter this
time ; there was nothing harsh' about it, nothing
bitter. Why should there be? Wasn’t it a splen-
did joke that he was playing on Scragge and the
Establishment generally ? And then he began to
prepare himself for the sudden shook which his
nerves would receive when the fact of his dis-
charge having arrived would be communicated to
him by the Civil Commandant! If the C. 0.
failed to perceive that the seal on the “Release ”
despatches was not a “ V.D.L.” one, but the . old
“N.S.W.” stamp, there would be nothing to
excite suspicion, and it was a thousand chances to
one against his noticing the wax impression. Ex-
ternally, with that exception, there was nothing
to show Scragge the packet had been tampered
with.
* * #
Internally, the papers were the same, ex-
cepting the verbal alterations already noted, and
in one other particular.
Convict No. 6006 did not think it necessary to
re-enclose Mr. Scribbins’ confidential letter to the
C.C. ; thought, indeed, it would be preferable to
retain that note in his own possession, and for this
very good and all-sufficient reason—that note was
his trump-card.
The routine on the discharge of a prisoner in
pursuance of formal order, or by the fiat of Death
was for the Island Authorities to advise the Comp-
troller-General’s Department in Hobart Town by
the next trip of the brig, so that the prisoner’s
“ record ” might be entered up. The Island Office
would notify the Department when Convict No.
600(i took his departure, and then, of course, the
fraud that that jocular individual had perpetrated
would be discovered. On the happening of this
contingency, which was less of a contingency than
a certainty, No. 600(5 determined to throw down
his trump. And he smiled and laughed and
danced as he reflected that there could be no
doubt of his winning the trick with it.
* * #
Events foil out pretty well as our joker
anticipated, though one thing he could not fore-
see. Convict John Knatchbull was not imme-
diately re-instated as writer, and so No. 6006 was
kept at work in the Commandant’s Office.
One morning in the week following the picnic-
party the C.C. rushed impetuously into the office.
“Look here, Sutherland, and you, Writer Zed,,
you’ll have to buckle to to-day and to-monow.
Skipper says he must go in a couple of days, and
we’ve all the last mail’s despatches to go through !'’
Seating himself, he hastily tore off iho wrappers
of the unopened bundles, and glancing at their
contents, threw those which could be attended to
by his subordinates on to his desk and then called
to No. 6006.
“Here, Writer, glance over these abstracts, and
tell me whom the Comptroller is going to take out
of this d d hole —some of my most useful men,
I expect!”
No. 6006 obeyed. He took the papers-includ-
ing the ones which ho had manipulated—-and read
out calmly the “Schedule of Re-transfers.” Then
he took up the Abstract of Discharges, and began:
“Zedekiah X ” He stopped—gasped out
“ O, God !” —and fell to the floor in a fit.
It must have been a capital piece of acting, for
it completely deceived both Scragge and Suther-
land, both of whom were tolerably familiar with
the epileptic shammer.
“They’ve discharged him, sir,” said Sutherland,
“and the sudden discovery of it has knocked him
over. ”
“So I presume,” growled the C.C. “ Too bad
of Dabster to take away one of the few steady
writers we have.”
Zed. revived at this juncture. “Oh, sir, beg
pardon, sir—l’m sure —gom’ off like this, but—
you see, sir, ’twas such a surprise—sir—my head
swims so—l hardly know yet—■whether it’s true ! ’
Scragge picked up the papers. He glanced over
them. “ Yes, it’s true enough—but why the
devil —(this with a sudden suspiciousness)—they
didn’t send, a O.P. instead of a Discharge I don’t
know, with all that time to do. I don’t know.
Or, indeed, why they should release you at all.”
No. 6006 had got up, and said, tremulously :
“ I think, sir, they’ve given me a discharge’ stead
o’ a pardon, because, sir, ’twouldn’t ha’ to be
gazetted, and pe’pie ’udn’t talk !”
“ But why should they give you a discharge at
all now—that’s what beats me!” persisted the
Commandant.
“Oh, sir, don’t Tike to mention it—but you see
I’m a great man’s son.”
“ The deuce you are !” - -
“Yes, sir—a nobleman’s son—over the left,
, sir—what you call ‘natural,’ sir. He let me be
lagged, but I know’d he’d relent by-and-oye.”
And artful 6006 here wiped away, what seemed to
be a tear.
“Oh,” said Scragge, “if you’ve got powerful
friends, that explains all !”
“ Powerful friends ” explained a lot of things in
the System.
# * *
A fortnight later No. 6006 played his
trump card. Within an hour after the Governor
Phillip hauled up to her moorings in Sullivan’s
Cove, in the Derwent, he walked into the pri vate
office, in Murray-street, of Mr. C. S. Scribbins,
Chief Clerk to the Comptroller-General.
Mr. Scribbins looked up from a pile of Mr.
Scragge’s despatches—amazed.
“You here, Zed.? —I thought you were doing
your time at the Island. Never expected to see
you again!”
“ Ain’t you read Scragge’s despatches, sir ?”
“Scragge! No, Mister”—Scribbins couldn’t
believe his ears. However, he replied :
“Not—all yet. Why?”
“Well, I’m discharged.”
“By whose order? —no order has gone from
here !” Mr. Scribbins rose in all the wrath of an
offended official.
“ By yours, sir.”
Then No. 6006 explained. He concluded by
saying: “Fix up my discharge all right, Mr.
Scribbins, and you shall have the letter back.”
“ Devil take you,” cried the Chief Clerk. “I’ll
have you arrested as an escapee !”
“No you won’t,” rejoined our humourist, coolly.
“If I’m not at a certain place to-night, copies o’
that letter, with full particulars, will be on their
way to every ‘damned newspaper,’ Mr. Scribbins,
here, Launceston, Melbourne and Sydney.”
“ But,” said the Chief Clerk, “ I can’t do it!”
“By—Parson Ford, sir!” exclaimed No. 6006,
vehemently, “ you could do it for Slyde, and you
shall do it for me !”
* * *
The result of that conversation was that
Mr. Scribbins capitulated. He consented to “fix
up ” the little matter of No. 6006’s freedom.
Of course he got back his letter ? Well, to tell
the exact truth, he didn’t, Zedekiah seized an
opportunity of Mr. Scribbins being momentarily
called away to concoct another little joke. He
possessed himself of several sheets of officially-
headed notepaper from the latter’s stationery
cabinet.
By the aid of one of these sheets, No. 6006 was
enabled to present Mr. Scribbins with a forged
copy of his own letter.
And by the aid of the original, at a future date,
i he was enabled to build up the beginnings of that
substantial bank-balance, owing chiefly to which
he died “deeply regretted.”
